Gift of Non-Existent Items: The Principle of Ma'dum
Ruling on the invalidity of hiba when the gifted item is non-existent or attached

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between invalid gifts of non-existent items (ma'dum) and valid gifts of attached but existing items, and state the requirement of a new contract after separation.

Core Idea
A gift of a non-existent item is invalid; attachment prevents possession, but separation with a new contract validates.

Gifting something that does not yet exist may seem harmless, but Islamic law requires the subject of a gift to be existent and deliverable at the time of contract. This ruling clarifies the key distinction between items that are truly non-existent (like flour in unground wheat) and items that exist but are attached to their source (like milk in the udder). Understanding this difference is essential for valid gifts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and ruling
The act is a gift (hiba) of an item that is still part of its source, e.g., flour in wheat. The ruling is invalid (batil).
The gift is void from the start, not just defective.
2
Locate the reason (illa)
The reason given is that the gifted item was non-existent (ma'dum) at the time of the contract. The text explicitly says 'because the gifted item was non-existent'.
This is a rational proof (talil) based on the nature of the subject matter.
3
Distinguish attached existing items
Items like milk in the udder, wool on the sheep, crops in the ground, and fruit on trees exist physically but are attached. They are likened to undivided property (musha') because attachment prevents possession.
The prohibition here is due to the impediment of possession, not non-existence.
4
Apply the exception and remedy
If the item is separated and delivered, the gift becomes valid only if a new contract is made after separation. The original contract does not become valid automatically.
Separation removes the attachment impediment, but the original contract was void ab initio.
5
Contrast with invalid cases
A gift of an unborn child (haml) remains invalid even after birth because its existence was uncertain at contract time, making it like non-existent. Similarly, a house containing furniture or a sack containing flour are treated like musha'.
Uncertainty of existence is treated as non-existence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن وهب إلخ: أي فإن وهب رجل لآخر دقيقا في بر لا تصح وإن طحن البر وسلم الدقيق، وكذا لا يصح لو وهب الدهن في السمسم أو وهب السمن في اللبن وإن استخرجهما وسلمهما؛ لأن الموهوب معدوم، إلا إذا تجدد العقد بعد استخراجها، وإنما جازت الوصية بهما؛ لأن الوصية بالمعدوم تجوز واللبن في الضرع، والصوف على ظهر الغنم والزرع والنخل في الأرض، والثمر في النخل بمنزلة المشاع؛ لأنها موجودة، وامتناع الجواز للاتصال، وذلك يعود إلى امتناع القبض كالشائع، فإذا فصلها وسلم جاز؛ لزوال المانع، كما في هبة الدين، بخلاف ما لو وهب الحمل وسلمه بعد الولادة حيث لا يجوز؛ لأن في وجوده احتمالاً، فصار كالمعدوم، والدار التي فيها المتاع، والجوالق الذي فيه الدقيق كالمشاع. [رمز الحقائق: ٢٦٠/٢]
If a man gifts to another flour in wheat, it is not valid even if he grinds the wheat and delivers the flour. Similarly, it is not valid if he gifts oil in sesame or ghee in milk, even if he extracts them and delivers them, because the gifted item was non-existent; unless the contract is renewed after extraction. Bequest (wasiyyah) of them is valid because bequest of non-existent items is permissible. Milk in the udder, wool on the sheep's back, crops and palm trees in the ground, fruit on the tree are like undivided property (musha') because they exist, and the prohibition is due to attachment, which reverts to prevention of possession like musha'. So if he separates and delivers them, it is valid due to removal of the impediment, as in the gift of debt. Unlike if he gifts an unborn child and delivers after birth, it is not valid because its existence is uncertain, making it like non-existent. A house containing furniture and a sack containing flour are like musha'.
Source pages p. 131

Commentary
This passage is a ruling on the invalidity of gifting items that are not yet separated from their source (e.g., flour still in wheat, oil still in sesame, ghee still in milk). The reason is that the gifted item is non-existent (ma'dum) at the time of the contract. If the contract is renewed after separation, it becomes valid. However, items like milk in the udder, wool on the sheep, crops in the ground, and fruit on trees are considered like musha' (undivided property) because they exist physically, but the impediment to validity is the attachment (ittisal), which prevents possession. Thus, if separated and delivered, the gift becomes valid, analogous to the gift of a debt. The text distinguishes this from gifting an unborn child (haml) which remains invalid even after birth due to uncertainty of existence. Similarly, a house containing furniture or a sack containing flour is treated like musha' (i.e., gift of the container without its contents is problematic).

Key Terms
معدوم (ma'dum): Non-existent; an item that does not exist at the time of the contract, making hiba invalid.
اتصال (ittisal): Attachment or connection of the gifted item to its source (e.g., fruit on tree), which prevents possession and thus invalidates hiba.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is a gift of flour in wheat invalid even if the wheat is later ground and flour delivered?
A) Because flour is a food item and requires measurement.
B) Because the gifted item (flour) was non-existent at the time of contract.
C) Because wheat is not owned by the donor.
D) Because the donee did not accept properly.

Key Takeaway
A hiba is invalid if the gifted item is non-existent at contract formation; attachment also prevents validity, but both can be remedied by separation and a new contract.

'Uqr for Mukatab and Ma'dhun: Immediate vs Deferred
Ruling on 'uqr timing for mukatab and ma'dhun.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish when 'uqr is due immediately vs after freedom for a mukatab or ma'dhun, based on whether the intercourse occurred in a trade or marriage context.

Core Idea
'Uqr is due immediately for trade, deferred for marriage without permission—except if she is a virgin.

The concept of 'uqr (compensation for intercourse) applies to slaves who are mukatab or ma'dhun. The timing of payment depends on whether the act falls within the scope of their permitted activities. This passage from Kanz al-Daqaiq explains the critical distinction between trade and marriage, and the special rule for deflowering a virgin.

Guided Path
1
Identify the actors and context
Determine whether the slave is a mukatab (under contract of manumission) or a ma'dhun (permitted to trade). Then identify the context of intercourse: did it occur through a trade transaction (e.g., purchasing a slave-girl) or through a marriage contract?
The ruling on 'uqr timing depends on this context.
2
Analyze the trade scenario
If the intercourse is part of a trade transaction (e.g., purchasing a female slave and having intercourse), the 'uqr is due immediately. Why? Because kitabah and trade permission encompass sale and purchase, making the slave authorized in these matters. Thus the master's rights are affected immediately.
This follows the principle that authorized actions bind the master.
3
Analyze the marriage scenario
If the intercourse occurs through a marriage contract without the master's permission, the 'uqr is deferred until after the slave becomes free. Why? Because marriage is not part of earning or trade, so it is not encompassed by kitabah. Therefore, it does not affect the master's rights until the slave is free.
Deferral protects the master from liability for actions outside the slave's authorization.
4
Note the exceptions: virgin deflowerment and permission
Exception 1: If the woman is a virgin and is deflowered, the 'uqr becomes due immediately even without permission, because of the physical harm. Exception 2: If the marriage was with the master's permission, then the mahr (not 'uqr) is due immediately.
These exceptions modify the general rule for marriage without permission.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فالعقر في المكاتبة إلخ: يعني وجب على المكاتب والمأذون العقر في هاتين الصورتين في الحال في حالة الكتابة قبل العتق؛ لأن هذا من باب التجارة، والتصرف تارة يقع صحيحا وتارة فاسدا والكتابة والإذن ينتظمان البيع والشراء بنوعيهما، فكانا مأذونين فيهما كالوكيل بهما، فيظهر في حق المولى، فيؤاخذ به في الحال. [تكملة البحر الرائق: ١٠٠/٨] ولو بنكاح إلخ يعني لو تزوج المكاتب امرأة بغير إذن المولى فوطئها، يؤاخذ بالعقر بعد العتق، وكذا المأذون له في التجارة؛ لأن التزوج له ليس من الاكتساب ولا من التجارة، فلا ينتظمه الكتابة، فلا يظهر في حق المولى، فلا يؤاخذ به بخلاف الفصل الأول تكملة البحر الرائق ١٠٠/٨٢] قال ملا مسكين: هذا إذا كانت الأمة ثيبا، أما لو كانت بكرا فافتضها يؤخذ بالعقر في الحال، وكذا لو تزوجها بإذن مولاه، يؤخذ بالمهر في الحال.
So the 'uqr in the kitabah etc.: meaning that the 'uqr is obligatory upon the mukatab and the ma'dhun in these two cases immediately, in the state of kitabah before freedom; because this falls under trade, and transactions are sometimes valid and sometimes invalid. And the kitabah and permission encompass sale and purchase of both types, so they are authorized in them like a agent, so it appears in the right of the master, so he is held liable immediately. [Tatimmat al-Bahr al-Ra'iq: 8/100] 'And if by marriage etc.' means if the mukatab married a woman without the master's permission and had intercourse with her, he is held liable for the 'uqr after freedom, and similarly the ma'dhun for trade; because marriage for him is not from earning nor from trade, so it is not encompassed by the kitabah, so it does not appear in the right of the master, so he is not held liable, unlike the first section. [Tatimmat al-Bahr al-Ra'iq: 8/100?] Mulla Miskin said: This is if the slave-girl is non-virgin; but if she was a virgin and he deflowered her, the 'uqr is taken immediately. Similarly, if he married her with his master's permission, the mahr is taken immediately.
Source pages p. 199

Commentary
This passage clarifies when 'uqr (compensation for intercourse) is due from a mukatab or ma'dhun. If the intercourse occurs as part of a trade transaction (e.g., purchasing a slave-girl and having intercourse with her), the 'uqr is due immediately because the kitabah or permission covers such trade dealings. However, if the intercourse occurs through a marriage contract without the master's permission, the 'uqr is not due until after the slave's freedom, because marriage is not considered trade and thus not covered by the kitabah. A special case: if the woman is a virgin and is deflowered, the 'uqr is due immediately even without permission. Also, marriage with permission requires mahr, not 'uqr.

Key Terms
العقر (al-'uqr): Financial compensation for intercourse that is not based on a valid marriage or ownership.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
When does a mukatab have to pay 'uqr for intercourse with a woman he married without his master's permission?
A) Immediately
B) After he becomes free
C) Never
D) Only if the woman is a virgin

Key Takeaway
'Uqr timing hinges on whether the act falls within the slave's authorization (trade) or not (marriage).

When Is ‘Aqr Due for a Mukatab? Trade vs Non-Trade Acts
Explains when 'aqr is due immediately or after emancipation based on the nature of the act.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish when 'aqr is due immediately vs after emancipation for a mukatab or ma'dhun, and explain the rationale based on the scope of kitabah.

Core Idea
‘Aqr is immediate for invalid purchase (trade) but delayed for marriage (non-trade), unless virgin or permission.

The mukatab slave has permission to trade to earn his freedom. But does that permission extend to marriage? The Hanafi school distinguishes: invalid purchases fall under trade, so liability for 'aqr is immediate; marriage without permission is not trade, so liability is postponed until the slave is free. This lesson unpacks the reasoning and its exceptions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
Read the matn and note the two cases: invalid purchase of a slave-girl and marriage without permission. For each, determine when the 'aqr is due.
The matn states: 'Or by an invalid purchase and it is returned, then the 'aqr is upon the mukatabah. And if by marriage... the 'aqr is obligatory after emancipation.'
2
Note the difference in timing
For invalid purchase, 'aqr is due immediately. For marriage without permission, it is due after emancipation. This contrast is the core of the ruling.
3
Understand the rationale
The commentary explains the legal cause (ta'lil): kitabah and permission (idhn) are for trade (buying/selling). Invalid purchase is a trade act, so the master can claim immediately. Marriage is not trade, so kitabah does not cover it, and liability is postponed.
The key principle: the scope of authorization determines immediate liability.
4
Recognize the exceptions
Mulla Miskin adds two exceptions: (a) if the slave-girl is a virgin, deflowering causes harm, so 'aqr is immediate even in marriage; (b) if marriage was with the master's permission, the dower is immediate because permission brings it under trade-like authorization.
These exceptions show that the ruling is not absolute but based on harm and explicit permission.
5
Apply to the ma'dhun
The text states that the same rule applies to the ma'dhun (slave permitted to trade). Confirm that the reasoning is identical.
The commentary explicitly says 'and likewise the one permitted for trade'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أو بشراء فاسد فردت، فالعقر في المكاتبة، ولو بنكاح أخذ ملكها الأمة إلى بائعها واجب مذ عتق. فالعقر في المكاتبة إلخ: يعني وجب على المكاتب والمأذون العقر في هاتين الصورتين في الحال في حالة الكتابة قبل العتق؛ لأن هذا من باب التجارة، والتصرف تارة يقع صحيحا وتارة فاسدا والكتابة والإذن ينتظمان البيع والشراء بنوعيهما، فكانا مأذونين فيهما كالوكيل بهما، فيظهر في حق المولى، فيؤاخذ به في الحال. [تكملة البحر الرائق: ١٠٠/٨] ولو بنكاح إلخ يعني لو تزوج المكاتب امرأة بغير إذن المولى فوطئها، يؤاخذ بالعقر بعد العتق، وكذا المأذون له في التجارة؛ لأن التزوج له ليس من الاكتساب ولا من التجارة، فلا ينتظمه الكتابة، فلا يظهر في حق المولى، فلا يؤاخذ به بخلاف الفصل الأول تكملة البحر الرائق ۱۰۰/۸۲] قال ملا مسكين: هذا إذا كانت الأمة ثيبا، أما لو كانت بكرا فافتضها يؤخذ بالعقر في الحال، وكذا لو تزوجها بإذن مولاه، يؤخذ بالمهر في الحال.
Or by an invalid purchase and it is returned, then the 'aqr is upon the mukatabah. And if by marriage, her possession is taken... the slave-girl to her seller is obligatory from the time of emancipation. 'The 'aqr upon the mukatabah etc.' means: The 'aqr is obligatory upon the mukatab and the ma'dhun in these two cases immediately during the state of kitabah before emancipation, because this falls under trade. Transactions sometimes occur validly and sometimes invalidly, and kitabah and permission encompass sale and purchase of both types, so they are permitted in them like an agent with both, thus it appears in the right of the master, so he is held liable immediately. [Takmilat al-Bahr al-Ra'iq: 100/8] 'And if by marriage etc.' means: If the mukatab married a woman without the master's permission and had intercourse with her, he is held liable for the 'aqr after emancipation, and likewise the one permitted for trade, because marriage is not part of earning nor trade, so kitabah does not encompass it, so it does not appear in the right of the master, so he is not held liable, unlike the first section. [Takmilat al-Bahr al-Ra'iq: 100/82] Mulla Miskin said: This is if the slave-girl was previously married (thayyib); but if she was a virgin and he deflowered her, the 'aqr is taken immediately. Likewise, if he married her with the master's permission, the dower is taken immediately.
Source pages p. 199

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with clear reasoning distinguishing between trade-related acts and non-trade acts under kitabah. The matn states that if a mukatab purchases a slave-girl with an invalid contract and then returns her, the 'aqr (compensation for intercourse) is due from him immediately. Similarly, if he marries a woman without permission, the 'aqr is due only after emancipation. The commentary explains the rationale: kitabah gives the slave authorization for trade (buying/selling) because that is the purpose of earning to pay the kitabah. So invalid purchases fall under trade, and the master can claim liability immediately. Marriage, however, is not trade, so kitabah does not cover it, and liability is delayed until the slave becomes free. An exception is noted: if the slave-girl was a virgin, deflowering incurs immediate 'aqr because of harm, and if marriage was with the master's permission, the dower (mahr) is due immediately as a proper marriage contract.

Key Terms
العقر (al-‘aqr): Compensation paid for intercourse with a slave-girl under an invalid contract or without valid marriage, akin to dower for free women.
المكاتب (al-mukatab): A slave who has entered a contract of kitabah with his master to earn his freedom by paying a sum.
المأذون (al-ma’dhūn): A slave permitted by his master to engage in trade.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
When is the 'aqr due for a mukatab who marries a slave-girl without his master's permission?
A) Immediately
B) After emancipation
C) Only if the slave-girl is a virgin
D) Never, because marriage is invalid

Key Takeaway
The timing of 'aqr for a mukatab depends on whether the act is trade or not: immediate for trade, delayed for non-trade, with exceptions for virginity and permission.

Ruling on Kitaba in Marad al-Mawt When Value Exceeds Price
Explains the ruling on kitaba in marad al-mawt when value exceeds price

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why a kitaba in marad al-mawt is valid only up to one-third of the slave's value and state the ruling when heirs do not approve.

Core Idea
In death illness, a kitaba that favors the slave is a bequest, valid only up to one-third of the estate.

When a master writes a kitaba (manumission contract) for his slave during his death illness at a price below the slave's market value, this involves a favor. Since the master's transactions in death illness are considered bequests, they are limited to one-third of his estate. This passage from Kanz al-Daqaiq explains the ruling when the value exceeds the price and the heirs do not approve, detailing how the one-third limit applies.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
Recognize the key facts: master in marad al-mawt, kitaba price less than slave's value, master dies with no other property, heirs do not approve.
2
Recognize the favor (muhabah)
The favor consists of two parts: reducing the price (forgiving part of the value) and deferring the payment. Both are donations subject to bequest rules.
3
Apply the one-third bequest limit
In marad al-mawt, all donations are treated as bequests and cannot exceed one-third of the estate. The estate here is the slave's value (since no other property).
4
Determine validity
The master's disposal is valid in one-third of the value (the permitted bequest portion) and invalid in the remaining two-thirds. Thus the slave must pay two-thirds of the value or be returned to slavery.
5
Understand Muhammad's resolution
Imam Muhammad distinguishes this case from the one where price exceeds value: here the favor reduces the price, so the one-third limit applies to the forgiven amount; in the other case, the favor is only deferral, which the master could have done anyway.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولم يجيزوا، أدى ثلثي القيمة ،حالا، أو رد رقيقا لي أي الورثة العبد إلى حالته الأولى كتابة أم ولد والمدبر كاتب عن عبد بألف وأدّى، عتق، فإن قبل العبد فهو مكاتب، وإن كاتب الحاضر والغائب، وقبل الحاضر. العبد العبد المولى العبد العبد العقد الحر عنه أدى ثلثي إلخ أي إذا كاتب المولى عبده في مرض موته على ألف إلى سنة، وقيمته ألفان، فمات ولم يترك مالا سواه، ولم يجز الورثة، أدى ثلثي القيمة وهي الألفان اتفاقا، وهذه الصورة عكس الصورة الأولى، ففي الصورة الأولى كانت القيمة ناقصة عن بدل الكتابة وفي هذه الصورة القيمة زائدة عليه، فههنا محاباة أي تبرع في القدر، وهو إسقاط ألف درهم، والأجل، وهو تأجيله الألف، فصح تصرفه في ثلث القيمة وهي الألفان، ولم يصح في ثلثيهما لا في الإسقاط ولا في التأخير، والفرق لمحمد حله بين هذه المسألة والأولى : أن الزيادة على القيمة كانت حق المريض في الأولى حتى كان يملك إسقاطها بأن يبيعه بقيمته فتأخيرها أولى؛ لأنه أهون من الإسقاط، وهنا وقعت الكتابة على أقل من قيمته فلا يملك إسقاط ما زاد على ثلث ،قيمته ولا تأجيله؛ لأن حق الورثة متعلق بجميعه بخلاف الأولى. فتح بزيادة من المحشي حالا إما من الحلول أو مقابل المستقبل.
And if they do not approve, he pays two-thirds of the value immediately, or he is returned as a slave to his original state... The commentary: i.e., if the master wrote a kitaba for his slave in his death illness for one thousand to be paid in one year, and the slave's value is two thousand, and he died leaving no other property, and the heirs did not approve, he pays two-thirds of the value, which is two thousand by agreement, and this case is the opposite of the first case: in the first case the value was less than the kitaba price, and in this case the value exceeds it, so here there is a favor (muhabah) i.e., a donation in the amount, which is forgiving one thousand dirhams, and in the term, which is deferring the thousand. So his disposal is valid in one-third of the value, which is two thousand, and invalid in two-thirds of it, neither in the forgiveness nor in the deferral. The difference resolved by Muhammad between this case and the first is that the excess over the value was the right of the sick person in the first case, so he could cancel it by selling him at his value, so deferring it is more appropriate because it is lesser than forgiveness; here the kitaba was for less than his value, so he cannot forgive what exceeds one-third of his value nor defer it because the right of the heirs is attached to all of it, unlike the first case. Fath: 'with a surplus from the commentator immediately, either from being due or in exchange for the future.'
Source pages p. 203

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on a matn ruling regarding a kitaba (contract of manumission) made by a master in his death illness. The master writes a kitaba for his slave for 1000 dirhams while the slave's market value is 2000, and the master dies leaving no other property. If the heirs do not approve the transaction, the ruling is that the slave must pay two-thirds of his value (2000) immediately or be returned to slavery. The commentary explains that this is because the master's transaction involves two types of favor: reducing the price (forgiving 1000) and deferring payment. In marad al-mawt, all transactions are treated as bequests and are only valid up to one-third of the estate. Since the slave's value is 2000, the master's favor in reducing the price exceeds one-third (the benefit is 2000, one-third is 666.67, but the actual favor is 1000 reduction plus deferral extension). Imam Muhammad resolves the difference between this case and the first case (where the value was less than the price) by noting that in the first case, the excess over value was a right of the sick person, so he could forgive it. Here, the kitaba is for less than the value, so the portion exceeding one-third of the value cannot be forgiven or deferred. The note from Fath clarifies that the word 'hālan' (immediately) refers to either immediate payment or payment at the due date.

Key Terms
مكاتب (mukatab): A slave who has entered into a contract of manumission with his master, agreeing to pay a specified sum in exchange for freedom.
محاباة (muhabah): A favor or donation in a transaction, such as reducing the price or extending the term.
مرض الموت (marad al-mawt): A deathly illness that often leads to death, during which a person's transactions are considered bequests.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the case discussed, why is the master's act invalid in two-thirds of the value?
A) Because the slave did not consent
B) Because the value exceeds the kitaba price
C) Because it exceeds the one-third bequest limit in marad al-mawt
D) Because the heirs did not approve

Key Takeaway
A kitaba in marad al-mawt with a favor is valid only up to one-third of the slave's value; the slave must pay the remainder or be returned.

Free Man Writing Kitaba for a Slave: The Role of Condition and Istihsan
Commentary explaining the ruling when a free man acts as representative in kitaba

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rulings for a free man writing a kitaba for a slave in two scenarios—with and without a condition—and apply the istihsan-based mufta bihi position to determine the slave's freedom.

Core Idea
A slave can be freed by a stranger's payment of kitaba even without a condition; istihsan overrides qiyas due to no harm.

This passage from the commentary Fath al-Qadir explains a nuanced case in kitaba (contract of manumission). When a free man asks the master to write a kitaba for a slave with a condition that if the free man pays the amount, the slave is freed, the slave is freed immediately without his acceptance. However, if the free man pays without such a condition, the Hanafi school rules by istihsan that the slave is freed, even though qiyas would not free him. Understanding this illustrates the Hanafi preference for istihsan in matters where there is no harm and benefit to the slave.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
2
Understand the role of the slave's acceptance
3
Contrast qiyas and istihsan
4
Determine the mufta bihi position
Authority_weight: zahir_riwayah

(فتح) كاتب إلخ: لما فرغ المصنف عن بيان أحكام تتعلق بالأصل في الكتابة شرع في أحكام تتعلق بالنيابة فيها، فقال: "حر كاتب عن عبد إلخ، وصورة المسألة: أن يقول أجنبي لمولى عبد: كاتب عبدك فلانا على على أني إن أديت إليك ألفا فهو حر فكاتبه المولى على هذا الشرط، وأدى الرجل الألف، يعتق العبد بحكم الشرط من غير قبول العبد ،وإجازته، وإن قبل العبد الكتابة حين سمع كلامه قبل أدائه صار مكاتبا، فإن هذا العقد له جهتان نافذ في حق ما ينتفع العبد، أن وهو يعتق عند أداء الشرط وموقوف على إجازة من له الإجازة، فإذا قبله صار مكاتبا؛ لأن الإجازة في الانتهاء كالإذن في الابتداء. ألف درهم وإن قال: لا أقبله، ثم أدّى القائل الألف لم يعتق؛ لأنه ارتد برده، ولو لم يقل الحر في هذه المسألة: على أني إن أديت إليك ألفا فهو حر وأدى لا يعتق في القياس؛ لأن العقد موقوف والموقوف لا حكم له، ولم يوجد التعليق، وفي الاستحسان يعتق؛ لأنه لا ضرر على العبد في عتقه بأداء الأجنبي، قال بعضهم: وهذه الصورة الأخيرة هي صورة المسألة المذكورة في المتن . (ملتقط من الحواشي والشروح)
Fath: 'Kitaba etc.': When the author finished explaining rulings related to the origin of kitaba, he began rulings related to representation in it. He said: 'A free man writes for a slave, etc.' The scenario: A stranger says to the slave's master: 'Write a kitaba for your slave so-and-so on the condition that if I pay you one thousand, he is free.' So the master writes the kitaba on this condition, and the man pays the thousand. The slave is freed by virtue of the condition without the slave's acceptance or permission. But if the slave accepts the kitaba when he hears his words before payment, he becomes a mukatab. For this contract has two aspects: effective in terms of what benefits the slave, and suspended on the permission of the one who has permission. So if he accepts it, he becomes a mukatab because permission at the end is like permission at the beginning. One thousand dirhams. And if he says: 'I do not accept it,' then the one who spoke pays the thousand, he is not freed because he retracted by his rejection. And if the free man did not say in this case: 'If I pay you one thousand, he is free' and pays, by qiyas he is not freed because the contract is suspended and the suspended has no ruling, and there is no ta'liq; by istihsan he is freed because there is no harm to the slave in his freedom by the payment of a stranger. Some said: This last scenario is the scenario mentioned in the matn. (Compiled from the commentaries and supercommentaries.)
Source pages p. 203

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on a matn about a free person writing a kitaba for a slave. The author explains two scenarios. First, a stranger (free man) asks the master to write a kitaba for a slave with the condition that if the stranger pays the amount, the slave becomes free. The master agrees, and the stranger pays. Here, the slave is freed immediately without his acceptance because the condition is directly between the stranger and the master. If the slave accepts the kitaba before payment, he becomes a mukatab, and the contract becomes binding. Second, if the stranger pays without the condition, the ruling differs: by qiyas (analogy), the slave is not freed because the contract is suspended and there is no express condition; however, by istihsan (juristic preference), the slave is freed because there is no harm to the slave in being freed by a stranger's payment. The relied-upon position is the istihsan ruling, as it is more beneficial for the slave.

Key Terms
نيابة (niyabah): Representation or agency; here it refers to a free person acting on behalf of the slave in the kitaba contract.
استحسان (istihsan): Juristic preference, a method of deriving rulings that departs from strict analogy for a more suitable outcome.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) The slave is freed
C) The free man becomes the owner
D) The kitaba is void

Key Takeaway
Istihsan frees the slave when a stranger pays his kitaba without condition, overruling qiyas due to lack of harm.

Kitaba for Present and Absent Slave and Female Slave with Children
Explains the validity of kitaba for two slaves (present and absent) and for a female slave with children by istihsan.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on kitaba when one slave is absent, explain the istihsan basis, and apply the same principle to a female slave with her children.

Core Idea
In kitaba, an absent slave or child can be included as dependent without consent; the contract is valid by istihsan.

This lesson examines two scenarios of kitaba (contract of manumission) where one party is included as a dependent: a present slave with an absent slave, and a female slave with her young sons. By default qiyas would render the contract invalid for the absent, but Hanafi istihsan, relying on the legislated example of a mother's children, validates it. Understanding this ruling reveals the Hanafi method of preferring istihsan over strict analogy.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario and the qiyas ruling
Read the passage: a kitaba contract includes a present and absent slave. By qiyas, only the present slave becomes a mukatab; the contract for the absent is suspended pending his consent.
Qiyas analogy: like selling one's own property and another's.
2
Understand the istihsan reasoning
The master intentionally addressed the present slave and made the absent a dependent (tabi'). This is legislated, just as a female slave's children enter her kitaba as dependents.
Dependency (taba'iyyah) is key: the absent is not a primary obligor.
3
Apply to the female slave with children
The same istihsan applies: a female slave includes her young sons in her kitaba. They are dependents, and their consent is irrelevant.
Mother has no guardianship; children enter by condition, not guardianship.
4
Trace the consequences of payment and manumission
If either party (present or absent, mother or child) pays the full price, all are freed. The master cannot take earnings from the dependent. If the master releases one, adjustments are made to the shares.
Liability is primarily on the present/mother; dependent's payment is voluntary.
5
Summarize the key rules
The contract is valid by istihsan; the dependent's consent is unnecessary; full liability is on the primary obligor; payment by any one frees all; recourse between them is not allowed.
These rules ensure practical benefit and align with the spirit of the law.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن كاتب إلخ: يعني إذا كاتب عبدين أحدهما حاضر والآخر غائب، سواء كان البداية من العبد بأن قال: كاتبني بألف عن نفسي وعن فلان الغائب، فكاتبهما المولى، فقبل الحاضر، أو من السيد بأن قال لعبده: كاتبتك وفلانا بألف، فقبل الحاضر، صح العقد عليهما استحسانا والقياس أن يصير الحاضر مكاتبا وحده؛ لأنه عقد الكتابة على نفسه وعلى الغائب، فينفذ عليه، ويتوقف في حق الغائب على إجازته كما إذا باع ماله ومال غيره، أو كاتب عبده وعبد غيره. وجه الاستحسان: أن المولى خاطب الحاضر قصدا وجعل الغائب تبعا له والكتابة على هذا الوجه مشروعة كالأمة إذا كوتبت دخل في كتابتها ولدها المولود في الكتابة، أو المشترى فيها، أو المضموم إليها في العقد تبعا لها حتى يعتقوا بأدائها، وليس عليهم شيء من البدل، فإذا كان كذلك ينفرد به الحاضر، ويستغني عن شرط رضا الغائب،
صحت، وأيهما أدّى عتقا، ولا يرجع على صاحبه ولا يؤخذ الغائب بشيء، وقبوله أي لا يطالب العبد لغو، وإن كاتبت الأمة عن نفسها وعن ابنين صغيرين لها، صح، وأي أدّى، لم يرجع.
المؤدي أي للأمة على صاحبه - ويطالب الحاضر بكل البدل؛ لأن كله عليه دون الغائب، ولا يعتبر إجازة الغائب ولا ردّه، ولا يؤاخذ الغائب بالبدل، ولا بشيء منه، ولو اكتسب شيئا ليس للمولى أن يأخذه منه ولو أبرأ المولى الحاضر، أو وهب بدل الكتابة له عتقا جميعا، وإن وهبه للغائب لم يعتقا، ولو أعتق الغائب سقط عن الحاضر حصته من البدل، ولو أعتق الحاضر لم يعتق الغائب، وسقط حصة الحاضر من الكتابة، ويؤدي الغائب حصته حالا، وإلا رد إلى الرق كذا في "التكملة" و"العيني" وغيرهما.
وأيهما أدى عتقا إلخ: أي وأي من الحاضر والغائب أدى بدل الكتابة عتقا جميعا؛ لوجود شرط عتقهما، ويجبر المولى على القبول، أما إذا دفع الحاضر؛ فلأن البدل عليه وأما إذا دفع الغائب؛ فلأنه ينال به شرف الحرية، فيجبر المولى على القبول، لكونه مضطرا كما إذا أدى ولد المكاتبة فإنه يجبر على القبول، وإن لم يكن البدل عليه.
دین ولا يرجع على صاحبه إلخ يعني لا يرجع واحد منهما بما أدى من البدل على الآخر، أما الحاضر؛ فلأنه قضى نفسه، وأما الغائب؛ فلكونه أدى بغير أمره، وليس مضطر فيه (عيني) ولا يؤخذ إلخ يعني لا يطالب المولى العبد الغائب ببدل الكتابة؛ لأنه لا دين عليه؛ لأنه لم يلتزم له بشيء، وإنما دخل في الكتابة تبعا، فصار نظير ولد المكاتبة. (عيني) وقبوله لغو: يعني قبول الغائب ورده لغو؛ لأن الكتابة قد نفذت وتمت من غير قبوله، فلا يعتبر بعد ذلك قبوله ولا رده كمن كفل دينا عن غيره بغير أمره فبلغه فإجازته باطلة ولا يتغير حكمه حتى لو أدى لا. صح: أي هذا العقد صحيح استحسانا والقياس أن لا يجوز، وقد ذكرنا وجهه في صح مسألة الغائب؛ لأن هذه مثلها في جميع ما ذكرنا من الأحكام؛ لأن الأم والأب الرقيق لا ولاية لهما على ولدهما، فيكون دخول الولد في كتابتهما بالشرط لا بالولاية كدخول الغائب في كتابة الحاضر، وقبول الأولاد وردهم لا يعتبر.
يرجع. وأي أدى إلخ: أي أي واحد من الثلاثة وهم الأم والابنان. (عيني) أي لا يرجع أحدهم على صاحبه لما ذكرنا في مسألة الغائب، ويجبر المولى على القبول، ولو أعتق الأم بقي عليهم من بدل الكتابة بحصتهم يؤدونها في الحال، بخلاف المولود في الكتابة والمشترى حيث يعتقان بعتقها ويطالب المولى الأم دونهم، ولو أعتقهم سقط عنها حصتهم، وعليها الباقي على نجومها، وليس له أن يبيعهم، ولو أبرأهم عن الدين أو وهبهم لا يصح، ولها يصح، ويعتقون معها، لما ذكرنا في كتابة الحاضر مع الغائب.
And the kitaba for a present and absent slave...: meaning if he writes a kitaba for two slaves, one present and one absent, whether the initiation is from the slave saying: 'Write a kitaba for me for one thousand on behalf of myself and so-and-so the absent,' and the master writes for them both, and the present accepts, or from the master saying to his slave: 'I have written a kitaba for you and so-and-so for one thousand,' and the present accepts, the contract is valid for both by istihsan, while by qiyas the present becomes a mukatab alone because he contracted for himself and the absent, so it is effective for him and suspended for the absent pending his approval, as if he sold his property and another's. The reason for istihsan: the master addressed the present intentionally and made the absent dependent on him; this type of kitaba is legislated, like when a female slave is written a kitaba, her child born during the kitaba enters it, or a child purchased or included in the contract as dependent, so they become free when she pays, and nothing of the price is due from them. So the present is alone [in liability] and there is no need for the absent's consent. It is validated: if either pays, both are freed, and neither can claim against the other, and the absent is not pursued for anything, and his acceptance is irrelevant. And if a female slave writes a kitaba for herself and her two young sons, it is valid, and if any of them pays, none can claim against the others. The commentary: 'The one who pays for the female slave, cannot claim against the other' – the present (in the first case) is demanded for the entire price because it is all due from him, not the absent; the absent's approval or rejection is not considered; the absent is not held for the price nor any part of it; if he earns something, the master cannot take it; if the master releases the present or gifts the kitaba price to him, both are freed; if he gifts it to the absent, they are not freed; if he frees the absent, the present's share of the price is dropped; if he frees the present, the absent is not freed and the present's share is dropped, and the absent must pay his share immediately, otherwise he is returned to slavery. And if either pays, both are freed, and the master is compelled to accept; if the present pays because the price is on him; if the absent pays because he attains the honor of freedom, the master is compelled to accept, as when a child of a female mukatab pays. There is no recourse against the other; the present paid for himself, the absent paid without being ordered and is not compelled. The absent is not pursued for the kitaba price because he has no debt; he entered as a dependent, like the child of a mukatab. His acceptance is irrelevant because the kitaba was executed without it. As for the female slave case: it is valid by istihsan; the mother and father have no guardianship over their children, so the children enter by condition, not guardianship, like the absent. The children's acceptance or rejection is not considered. If the mother is freed, the remaining price due from the children's share must be paid immediately, unlike a child born during kitaba who is freed with the mother. If the children are freed, the mother's share is dropped and she pays the remainder on her installments. The master cannot sell them; if he cancels the debt or gifts it to them, it is not valid; but if he does so for the mother, it is valid and they are freed with her.
Source pages p. 203

Commentary
This passage covers two related rulings. First, when a kitaba is made for two slaves, one present and one absent: by istihsan (preferred ruling) the contract is valid for both, with the present slave bearing full responsibility for the payment. The absent slave is a dependent; if either pays, both are freed. The master cannot demand payment from the absent, and the absent's acceptance is irrelevant. If the master frees one or the other specific rules apply about shares of the debt. Second, the same principle applies to a female slave (mother) who writes a kitaba including her young sons. The children enter as dependents; the mother bears primary liability. If she is freed, the children are freed with her and must pay their share immediately. If the children are freed, the mother's share is reduced. The end result ensures that all become free through payment by any one of them, subject to adjustments. The commentary cites al-Takmila and al-'Ayni for support.

Key Terms
تبعية (taba'iyyah): Dependence; the absent slave is treated as dependent on the present, similar to a child dependent on the mother in kitaba.
حاضر وغائب (hadir wa gha'ib): Present and absent; refers to the physical presence of the slave at the time of the kitaba contract.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school validates a kitaba inclusive of a dependent without their consent by istihsan, with full liability on the primary slave.

Joint Mukatab: One Owner Permits the Other to Write Kitaba
Intra-Hanafi dispute kitaba divisibility

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi intra-madhhab difference on the divisibility of kitaba and apply it to determine ownership of payments and freedom in a jointly owned slave scenario.

Core Idea
Kitaba is divisible per Abu Hanifa; indivisible per Sahibayn – this determines whose money and whose freedom.

This lesson dissects a classic intra-Hanafi dispute regarding a joint owner permitting the other to write a kitaba contract for his share of a slave. The core disagreement hinges on whether kitaba is divisible (tajazzi) or indivisible – a question that profoundly affects the rights of the owners and the slave. We will walk through the positions, proofs, and practical consequences (thamarah al-khilaf) so that you can confidently analyze such cases.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position (Abu Hanifa)
Identify Abu Hanifa's ruling: the kitaba is divisible, so the slave becomes mukatab only for the permitter's share. The collected money belongs solely to the receiver (the permitted partner).
Abu Hanifa's view relies on the analogy with emancipation (itq), which is divisible.
2
Identify the opponent (Sahibayn) and their view
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad hold that kitaba is indivisible, so the permission covers the entire slave. The slave is mukatab for both owners, and the collected money is shared.
They analogize kitaba to a single contract that cannot be split.
3
Follow the opponent's proof
Understand the supporting argument: since kitaba is indivisible, permission for the share implies permission for the whole. The permitter acts as principal for half and agent for half.
Their dalil: 'al-idhn bi kitabati nusbihi idhn bi kitabati al-kull.'
4
Analyse the Hanafi response
Abu Hanifa counters: kitaba, like emancipation, can be partial because it gradually benefits the slave. The permitter's permission applies only to his share, and the slave uses his own earnings to pay; thus the money is for the receiver.
Key principle: the permitter donates his share to the slave, so no claim after payment.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
Apply the differences to concrete outcomes: upon full payment, only the permitter's share becomes free (Abu Hanifa) vs. the whole slave (Sahibayn). Upon inability to pay, the collected money stays with the receiver (Abu Hanifa) vs. is shared (Sahibayn).
The mufta bihi is Abu Hanifa's view, as preferred by later authorities.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
عبد لهما أذن أحدهما صاحبه أن يكاتب حظه بألف ويقبض بدل الكتابة فكاتب وقبض بعض الألف، ثم عجز العبد فالمقبوض من الألف للقابض، وهذا عند أبي حنيفة، وقالا: هو مكاتب لهما، والمقبوض بينهما وأصله أن الكتابة تتجزأ عند الإمام، وعندهما: لا تتجزأ كما ذكر في الإعتاق فإذا كان كذلك فإنها تفيد الحرية من وجه فتقتصر على نصيبه للتجزئة عنده. وعندهما: لما لم يتجزأ الكتابة، فالإذن بكتابة نصيبه إذن بكتابة الكل، فهو أصيل في النصف، ووكيل في النصف، فهو بينهما، والمقبوض مشترك بينهما، فيبقى كذلك بعد العجز، وللإمام أن المكاتب نصف كسبه للآذن، فإذا أذن للمكاتب أن يصرف نصف كسبه الذي هو نصيبه، صح إذنه، وتم ذلك الإذن بقضاء المكاتب دينه به، فكان المقبوض للقابض، فإن عجز المكاتب لا يرجع الآذن بذلك، وإن لم يحصل مقصوده، وهو الحرية؛ لأن المتبرع عليه هو العبد، ولو رجع الآذن يرجع على العبد، والمولى لا يستوجب على عبده دينا. وإنما قيده بقوله: أذن أحدهما؛ لأنه لو لم يأذن أحدهما وكاتبه الآخر، فله حق الفسخ قبل أن يؤدي بدل الكتابة عند الكل؛ لأنه يضر بالكتابة؛ إذ بها يمتنع بيع نصيبه في الحال، ويصير مستسعيا في ثاني الحال، ولا يصح إلا بقضاء القاضي أو برضاء العبد وإن لم يفسخ الكتابة حتى أدى العبد بدل الكتابة عتق حظ المكاتب عند أبي حنيفة؛ لأن الكتابة تتجزأ عنده وللساكت أن يأخذ من الذي كاتب نصف ما أخذ من بدل الكتابة، ثم ينظر إن كان المكاتب كاتب العبد كله بألف لم يرجع على العبد المكاتب بشيء مما أخذه منه شريكه، وإن كاتب نصيبه فقط على المكاتب بما أخذه شريكه، وعندهما: بالأداء عتق كله، ويرجع الساكت على شريكه إن كان موسرا، وإلا فعلى العبد كما لو أعتقه، وله أن يأخذ نصف ما بقي من الأكساب؛ لأنه كسب عبد مشترك. ويقبض إلخ: إنما قيد الإذن بالكتابة بالقبض؛ لينقطع حقه في المقبوض ويختص به القابض؛ لأن إذنه بالقبض إذن لعبده بالأداء إليه فيكون الآذن متبرعا بنصيبه على العبد المكاتب إلا إذا نهاه قبل الأداء، فيصح لهيه؛ لأنه تبرع لم يتم. فتح باختصار بألف فكاتب وقبض بعضه، فعجز، فالمقبوض للقابض.
A slave owned by two, one of them permitted his partner to write a kitaba for his share for one thousand and to receive the payment of the kitaba. So he wrote the kitaba and received part of the thousand, then the slave became unable to pay. The received portion of the thousand belongs to the receiver. This is according to Abu Hanifa. And they (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) said: He is a mukatab for both, and the received money is between them. The basis is that kitaba is divisible according to the Imam, and according to them it is not divisible, as mentioned in (the chapter of) emancipation. So when it is so, it (kitaba) benefits freedom from one aspect, so it is confined to his share due to divisibility according to him. According to them, since kitaba is not divisible, permission to write kitaba for his share is permission to write kitaba for the whole. So he is principal in half and agent in half, so it is between them, and the received money is shared between them, so it remains so after inability. For the Imam: the mukatab's half of his earnings belongs to the permetter. So when he permitted the mukatab to spend his half-earnings which is his share, his permission is valid, and that permission is completed by the mukatab paying his debt with it, so the received money is for the receiver. If the mukatab becomes unable to pay, the permetter does not get that money back, even if his objective (freedom) is not achieved, because the beneficiary is the slave. If the permetter were to get it back, he would get it from the slave, and a master does not have a debt claim against his slave. He restricted it by his saying 'one of them permitted' because if he had not permitted and the other wrote kitaba, he has the right to cancel before payment of the kitaba price according to all, because it harms the kitaba, since by it the sale of his share is prevented immediately, and the slave becomes obliged to earn and pay in the second stage, and it is not valid except by a judge's decree or the slave's consent. And if he does not cancel the kitaba until the slave pays the kitaba price, the share of the one who wrote kitaba becomes free according to Abu Hanifa, because kitaba is divisible according to him. And the silent partner takes from the one who wrote kitaba half of what he received from the kitaba price. Then if the mukatab wrote kitaba for the whole slave for one thousand, he does not claim from the mukatab anything of what his partner took from him. If he wrote kitaba only for his share, then the silent partner claims from the mukatab what his partner took. According to them, by payment the whole becomes free, and the silent partner claims from his partner if he is solvent, otherwise from the slave, as if he emancipated him. And he takes half of the remaining earnings, because it is the earnings of a jointly owned slave. 'And receives' etc.: He restricted permission for kitaba with receipt so that his right in the received money is cut off and it becomes exclusive to the receiver, because his permission for receipt is permission for his slave to pay to him, so the permetter becomes a donor of his share to the mukatab, unless he prohibits before payment, then his prohibition is valid, because it is a donation not completed. (From Fath, abridged) He made the kitaba contract and received part of it, then the slave became unable to pay, so the received money is for the receiver.
Source pages p. 205

Commentary
This passage presents a dispute between Imam Abu Hanifa and his two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani) regarding a jointly owned slave where one owner permits the other to write a kitaba (contract of manumission) for his share. The issue hinges on whether kitaba is divisible (tajazzi). According to Abu Hanifa, kitaba is divisible, so the permitted partner acts only on his own share; the slave becomes partially free upon payment of that share. The collected money belongs solely to the receiver because the permettre donated his share. According to the Sahibayn, kitaba is indivisible, so the permission effectively covers the whole slave; the slave is mukatab for both, and the collected money is shared. The passage then discusses the consequences if the slave pays the full amount or becomes unable, and the rights of the silent partner. The commentary elaborates on the ruling and its reasoning, emphasizing the principle of divisibility of kitaba.

Key Terms
كتابة (kitābah): A contract between a master and his slave whereby the slave pays a sum of money to the master in installments, and upon full payment, the slave becomes free.
تجزئ (tajazzi’): Divisibility; the quality of a legal act that can be split into separate parts, each producing partial effect.
مكاتب (mukātab): A slave who has entered into a kitaba contract with his master, undertaking to pay a specified amount for his freedom.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the mufta bihi (Abu Hanifa's view), if a jointly owned slave is made mukatab by one owner with permission, and the slave pays the full kitaba, what happens?
A) Only the permitter's share becomes free.
B) The whole slave becomes free.
C) The slave remains a slave.
D) The owners split the money equally.

Key Takeaway
Kitaba's divisibility determines ownership rights in a joint ownership scenario; Abu Hanifa's view is mufta bihi.

Jointly-Owned Mukatibah: Recovery After Failure
Ruling on liability when one partner frees a joint mukatibah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling when one partner frees a jointly-owned mukatibah who later fails to pay, and distinguish the positions of Abu Hanifa and the two disciples (Sahibayn) on the divisibility of emancipation.

Core Idea
Emancipation is divisible under Abu Hanifa, allowing recovery from the slave; under Sahibayn it is indivisible, blocking recovery.

This passage presents a classic intra-Hanafi dispute concerning a joint mukatibah. When one partner frees her while he is wealthy, and she later fails to fulfill her kitabah agreement, the scholars differ on who bears the loss. The disagreement traces back to a fundamental difference in legal theory: whether emancipation can be divided or is an indivisible act. Understanding this dispute will sharpen your ability to apply Hanafi usul to concrete cases.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Scenario
Read the passage and identify the key facts: two partners both made a female slave a mukatibah (she contracts to buy her freedom in installments). Then one partner frees her while he is wealthy (mūsir). She later fails to pay the remaining installments (عجزت). Determine what issue arises for the partners.
The key is that only one partner freed, and the slave fails.
2
State Abu Hanifa's Position
According to the text, what is the ruling per Abu Hanifa? He holds that the freeing partner is liable to his partner for half the slave's value (ضمن لشريكه نصف قيمتها), but then he recovers that amount from the slave (ورجع به عليها). This is presented as his madhhab.
3
State the Sahibayn's Position
According to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (Sahibayn), the freeing partner does not recover from the slave (لا يرجع المعتق عليها). Instead, the silent partner has the option to make the slave earn compensation (يستسعيها الساكت) if the freeing partner is insolvent. If the freeing partner is wealthy, the silent partner can claim compensation from him directly.
4
Identify the Underlying Usul Principle
The text explains that the difference stems from a principle: whether emancipation is divisible (يتجزأ) or indivisible. Abu Hanifa holds that emancipation is divisible, so freeing half is valid, leaving the other half still as a mukatibah. The Sahibayn hold that emancipation is indivisible, so freeing one half immediately frees the whole, dissolving the kitabah contract.
This is a classic usul al-fiqh issue applied to emancipation.
5
Apply the Principle to Determine the Practical Outcome (Thamarah)
Under Abu Hanifa's divisible view, the freeing partner's liability is contingent on the slave's failure; he can later recover from the slave. Under the Sahibayn's indivisible view, the freeing partner bears the full liability immediately and cannot recover from the slave, but the silent partner can pursue the slave if the freeing partner is insolvent.
The practical consequence (ثمرة الخلاف) is whether the freeing partner can shift the loss back to the slave.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن كاتباها، فحررها أحدهما موسِرًا، فعجزت ضمن لشريكه نصف قيمتها، ورجع به عليها. وهذا مذهب أبي حنيفة. وعندهما لا يرجع المعتق عليها، ويستسعيها الساكت إن كان المعتق معسرًا. والأصل في هذا أن الإعتاق لا يتجزأ عندهما، والكتابة السابقة لا تمنع العتق فلما حررها أحدهما عتقت كلها للحال وانفسخت الكتابة، فيضمن المعتق لشريكه إن كان موسرًا ولا يرجع عليها ويستسعيها إن كان معسرًا، ومن أصل الإمام أن العتق يتجزأ عنده فجاز إعتاق النصف، فلا يؤثر الفساد في نصيب شريكه بل يبقى على حاله مكاتبًا، فلا يضمن المعتق قبل العجز؛ لعدم ظهور أثر الإعتاق فيها، فإذا عجزت ظهر أثر العتق، وكان للساكت الخيارات المذكورة في العتق وهي إن كان موسرًا فله أن يعتق أو يستسعي أو الضمان، فإذا ضمن كان للمعتق أن يرجع على العبد.
And if both made her a mukatibah, then one of them freed her while he is wealthy, then she failed to pay, he is liable to his partner for half her value, and he recovers it from her. This is the madhhab of Abu Hanifa. According to the two disciples, the one who freed does not recover from her, and the silent partner may make her earn if the one who freed is insolvent. The principle is that manumission is indivisible according to the two; the prior kitabah does not prevent freedom. So when one freed her, she becomes fully free immediately, the kitabah is dissolved, so the freeing partner is liable to his partner if he is wealthy, and does not recover from her, and makes her earn if he is insolvent. According to Imam Abu Hanifa's principle, manumission is divisible, so freeing half is valid, so the defect does not affect his partner's share but remains as mukatibah. So the freeing partner is not liable before her failure; when she fails, the effect of freedom appears, and the silent partner has the options mentioned in emancipation: if he is wealthy, he may free, or make her earn, or claim surety. If he claims surety, the freeing partner may recover from the slave.
Source pages p. 208

Commentary
This passage discusses a difference within the Hanafi school (intra-Hanafi dispute) regarding a jointly-owned female slave who has been made a mukatibah by both partners, then one partner frees her while he is wealthy (mūsir), and then she fails to pay her kitabah. The ruling: according to Abu Hanifa, the freeing partner is liable to his partner for half the slave's value, and then he can recover that amount from the slave (by making her work or from her earnings). According to the two disciples (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), the freeing partner does not recover from the slave; rather, the silent partner has the option to make her earn compensation if the freeing partner is insolvent. The dispute stems from a difference in usul: Abu Hanifa holds that emancipation is divisible (yatjazzā), so the freeing of half is valid, leaving the other half still as a mukatibah. Thus, the full effect of freedom only appears upon her failure to pay, at which point the silent partner can exercise his options. The two disciples hold that emancipation is indivisible (lā yatjazzā), so as soon as one partner frees her, she becomes fully free immediately, dissolving the kitabah. Therefore, the freeing partner is directly liable for the value of the other partner's share (if wealthy), and that liability cannot be passed to the slave because it arose from his own act of freeing.

Key Terms
موسر (mūsir): A person who has wealth (yusr) sufficient to pay compensation or dower.
استسعاء (istis'ā'): A legal device where the slave is required to earn money to compensate the master, often used when the master is insolvent.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The freeing partner cannot recover it from the slave
B) The freeing partner can recover it from the slave
C) The silent partner must bear the loss
D) The slave is freed without compensation

Key Takeaway
When one partner frees a joint mukatibah who later defaults, Abu Hanifa allows the freeing partner to recover from the slave, while the Sahibayn do not — the difference stems from the divisibility of emancipation.

Jointly-Owned Slave: Tadbir and Subsequent Manumission – An Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Ruling on the options of the first partner when the second frees the slave after tadbir.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the views of Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn regarding the effect of tadbir on a jointly-owned slave and explain the practical consequences (thamarah al-khilaf).

Core Idea
Tadbir is divisible according to Abu Hanifa but indivisible according to the Sahibayn — this changes the partner's rights.

When two partners co-own a slave and one makes a tadbir (declaration that the slave will be free upon his death), what happens if the other partner frees the slave immediately? This lesson explores the intra-Hanafi dispute between Imam Abu Hanifa and his two disciples (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani) on this question. The core difference lies in whether tadbir is considered divisible or indivisible, leading to different legal outcomes for the partners.

Guided Path
1
Identify the case and the dispute
We have two partners (A and B) co-owning a slave. Partner A makes a tadbir on the slave. Then partner B frees his share of the slave while he is wealthy. The dispute: Is B's freeing valid? What are the rights of A (the mudabbir)?
2
State Abu Hanifa's position and his reasoning
According to Imam Abu Hanifa, tadbir is divisible. Therefore, the tadbir applies only to A's share, but it 'corrupts' B's share (i.e., gives B options). When B frees his share, the freeing is valid and confined to B's share. A then has limited options: he can keep his tadbir or demand compensation from B.
Abu Hanifa's view: tadbir is like a contract that can be partially assigned.
3
State the Sahibayn's position and reasoning
According to the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), tadbir is indivisible. Therefore, A's tadbir transfers B's share to A automatically. B's attempted freeing is invalid. A must pay half the slave's value to B (as compensation for taking ownership) regardless of A's financial status.
Sahibayn's view: tadbir is like a will that applies to the whole slave.
4
Compare the practical outcomes (thamarah al-khilaf)
Under Abu Hanifa, B can free his share and A retains tadbir over his own share. Under Sahibayn, A automatically owns B's share and must compensate B.
The practical difference: who ends up owning the share and what are the options.
5
Identify the mufta bihi position
Later Hanafi authorities (e.g., Fath al-Qadir) often adopt the Sahibayn view as the operative ruling for fatwa. Thus, the freeing by the partner is invalid, and the mudabbir owns the share with compensation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
عبد لهما دبره أحدهما، ثم حرره الآخر وهو موسِر، فللمدبر إن شاء أن يضمن المعتق نصف قيمته وإن شاء أعتق، وإن شاء استسعى، وهذا عند الإمام. ووجهه أن التدبير يتجزأ عنده، فيقتصر التدبير على نصيب المدبر لكن يفسد به نصيب الآخر، فيثبت له خيار التضمين أو الإعتاق أو الاستسعاء. فإذا أعتقه الآخر لم يبق له خيار التضمين والاستسعاء، فيقتصر الإعتاق على نصيبه؛ لأنه يتجزأ عنده، لكن يفسد نصيب الآخر، أي المدبر فله أن يضمنه نصيبه وله خيار العتق والاستسعاء. وعندهما: إذا دبره أحدهما، فإعتاق الآخر باطل؛ لأنه لا يتجزأ عندهما، فيملك المدبر نصيب صاحبه بالتدبير، ويضمن نصف قيمته موسرًا كان أو معسرًا؛ لأنه ضمان تملك فلا يختلف بهما، ويضمن نصيب قيمته قِنًا؛ لأنه صادفه التدبير، وهو قِن.
A slave belonging to two men: one made a tadbir on him, then the other freed him while he is wealthy. So the mudabbir, if he wishes, may hold the freeing partner liable for half his value, or free, or make the slave earn. This is according to Imam Abu Hanifa. His reasoning: tadbir is divisible according to him, so the tadbir is confined to the share of the mudabbir but it corrupts the share of the other, so the other has the options of surety, freeing, or earning. When the other freed him, he no longer has the options of surety and earning; the freeing is confined to his share because it is divisible according to him, but it corrupts the share of the other, i.e., the mudabbir, so he may hold him liable for his share and has the options of freeing and earning. According to the two disciples (Sahibayn): if one made a tadbir, then the other's freeing is invalid because it is indivisible according to them, so the mudabbir owns his partner's share by the tadbir, and he owes half his value whether wealthy or poor because it is a suretyship of ownership and does not vary with that, and he owes the value as a qin (ordinary slave) because the tadbir found him as a qin.
Source pages p. 208

Commentary
This passage presents another intra-Hanafi dispute concerning a jointly-owned slave (ghulām bayn ithnayn). One partner makes a tadbir (declaration that the slave will become free upon his death). Then the other partner frees the slave immediately while he is wealthy. According to Abu Hanifa, the tadbir is divisible; therefore, the tadbir applies only to the mudabbir's share, but it corrupts the partner's share, giving the partner options: he can either (1) hold the mudabbir liable (ḍamān) for the value of his share, (2) free his own share, or (3) make the slave earn (istis'ā'). However, since the parter freed his own share, he has already exercised the option of freeing; thus, the mudabbir now has options: he can hold the freeing partner liable for his share of the value, or free his own share (but that is already done by the partner? correction: the text says 'fa-idha a'taqahu al-ākhar lam yabqa lahu khiyār al-ḍamān wa al-istis'ā'...' meaning the mudabbir loses those options?). Actually careful: The text says: 'fala yabqa lahu khiyār al-ḍamān wa al-istis'ā'..' It seems that when the partner frees his share, then the mudabbir no longer has the options of surety and earning, but he can hold the partner liable? The commentary is a bit confusing. Let's interpret: The text states: at Imam Abu Hanifa: tadbir is divisible, so the tadbir only affects the mudabbir's share but corrupts the partner's share. The partner then has the three options (ضمان, عتق, استسعاء). If the partner chooses to free his share (which is the case here), then the mudabbir loses his options of claiming compensation and istis'ā', and the freeing is confined to the partner's share (since tadbir is still on mudabbir's share). But then the text says 'lakin yufsid nasib al-ākhar' i.e., the mudabbir's share is corrupted? Actually it's messy. The next part: 'fa-idha a'taqahu al-ākhar lam yabqa lahu khiyār al-ḍamān wa al-istis'ā', fa-yaqtasir al-i'tāq 'alā nasībihi...' This seems to indicate that the mudabbir's options are limited. Then the text goes on: 'fa-in ḍammanahu yaḍmanuhu qīmatahu mudabbaran' – perhaps it's a different case. Let's rely on the clear part: According to the two disciples, the mudabbir automatically owns the partner's share by virtue of the tadbir (since tadbir is indivisible), so he must pay half the value to the partner, regardless of the partner's wealth, and the partner cannot free because tadbir is already established. This is the standard Hanafi view: the tadbir of one partner gives him a right to purchase the other's share at the time of death? Actually the text says 'yamluk al-mudabbir nasīb ṣāḥibihi bi al-tadbīr' and he owes half value immediately? The commentary suggests the mudabbir must pay half value as compensation for taking ownership. This seems to be a different ruling from the previous one. I'll present both views as per text.

Key Terms
مدبر (mudabbir): The master who declares a tadbir on his slave.
قِنّ (qinn): An ordinary slave not subject to any prior manumission contract.

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) The freeing is valid but the mudabbir has options
D) The slave becomes free entirely

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school has two views on tadbir in joint ownership: divisibility (Abu Hanifa) vs. indivisibility (Sahibayn), with the latter being mufta bihi.

Death of the Mukatab and Its Effect on the Kitaba
Ruling on the mukatab's death with property and dispute with Shafi'i

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on the death of a mukatab and explain the dispute with the Shafi'i school, including the qiyas proof.

Core Idea
The kitaba contract survives the mukatab's death if he leaves property, because it is a commutative contract like sale.

When a mukatab (a slave under a kitaba contract) dies, a crucial question arises: is the contract dissolved or does it continue? The Hanafi school holds that the contract remains valid if the mukatab leaves property, making him free in the last part of his life. The Shafi'i school disagrees, arguing that the contract's purpose becomes impossible. This lesson unpacks both positions and the Hanafi proof based on qiyas with sale.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
The Hanafi ruling: if the mukatab dies with property, the kitaba is not dissolved; it is paid from his estate and he is deemed free in the last part of his life. If he dies without property, the contract is dissolved.
This ruling is attributed to Ibn Mas'ud and is the mufta bihi position.
2
Identify the opponent and their view
Imam al-Shafi'i, following Zayd ibn Thabit, holds that the kitaba is dissolved by the death of the mukatab, regardless of whether he leaves property.
3
Follow the opponent's proof
The Shafi'i argument: the contract aims at freedom through payment; after death, payment is impossible, so the contract becomes void. The death of the master is different because the master is the contractor, not the one contracted upon.
4
Analyse the Hanafi response
The Hanafi proof: kitaba is a commutative contract (aqd mu'awada) like sale, which is not dissolved by the death of either party. The contract remains after the master's death for his right to wala' and after the slave's death for his children's freedom.
Qiyas on sale is the core dalil.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence: under Hanafi law, the estate pays the kitaba and the mukatab becomes free, allowing his children to inherit as free persons. Under Shafi'i law, the contract dissolves, and the mukatab remains a slave, so his children are also slaves.
This difference affects inheritance and family status.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
موت المكاتب وعجزه وما في يده لسيده وإن مات وله مال لم تفسخ، وتؤدّى كتابته لأنه كسب عبده. وحكم بعتقه في آخر حياته. هذا قول ابن مسعود [؟] وبه أخذ علماؤنا، وقال زيد بن ثابت [؟] تنفسخ بموته، وبه أخذ الإمام الشافعي، له: أن العقد لو بقي لبقي لتحصيل العتق بالأداء، وقد تعذر إثباته فبطل، بخلاف موت المولى؛ لأنه ليس بمعقود عليه بل عاقد، والعقد يبطل بموت المعقود عليه لا بموت العاقد؛ ولأن المولى يصلح أن يكون معتقا بعد الموت، بخلاف العبد فإنه لا يثبت له العتق بعد الموت. لنا: أن الكتابة عقد معاوضة لا تنفسخ بموت أحد المتعاقدين، وهو المولى، فلا تنفسخ بموت الآخر، وهو العبد كالبيع؛ لأن قضية المعاوضة المساواة، فإذا بقي العقد بعد موت المولى لحاجته إلى الولاء وغيره، جاز أن يبقى بعد موت العبد لحاجته إلى الحرية ليتوصل إلى حرية أولاده. ولو مات عاجزا تفسخ الكتابة.
The death of the mukatab and his incapacity. Whatever is in his hand belongs to his master. If he dies and has property, the kitaba is not dissolved, and his kitaba is paid off because it is the earning of his slave. And the ruling of his emancipation in the last part of his life. This is the saying of Ibn Mas'ud, and our scholars adopted it. Zayd ibn Thabit said: it is dissolved by his death, and Imam al-Shafi'i adopted it. For him: if the contract remained, it would remain for the achievement of emancipation by payment, and proving it has become impossible, so it becomes void, unlike the death of the master because he is not the contracted party but the contractor, and the contract becomes void by the death of the contracted party, not by the death of the contractor; and because the master can be an emancipator after death, unlike the slave, for emancipation cannot be established for him after death. For us: the kitaba is a commutative contract that is not dissolved by the death of either of the two contracting parties, namely the master, so it is not dissolved by the death of the other, namely the slave, like a sale; because the requirement of commutative exchange is equality. So if the contract remains after the death of the master due to his need for patronage (wala) and other things, it is permissible for it to remain after the death of the slave due to his need for freedom to attain the freedom of his children. And if he dies unable, the kitaba is dissolved.
Source pages p. 211

Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab dispute regarding the effect of the mukatab's death on the kitaba contract. The Hanafi position, attributed to Ibn Mas'ud, is that if the mukatab dies with property, the contract is not dissolved; rather, the kitaba is paid from his estate, and he is considered emancipated in the last part of his life. The opposing view of Zayd ibn Thabit and Imam al-Shafi'i is that the contract is dissolved by death because the purpose of achieving freedom through payment becomes impossible. The Hanafi proof is based on qiyas: the kitaba is analogous to a sale (bay'), which is a commutative contract that does not terminate upon the death of either party. The contract remains after the master's death for the sake of his right to wala' and after the slave's death for the sake of his children's freedom. The passage also notes that if the mukatab dies unable to pay, the contract is dissolved, as the condition of capability is no longer met.

Key Terms
مكاتب (mukatab): A slave who has entered into a kitaba contract to purchase his freedom by paying installments.
كتابة (kitaba): A contract of manumission by which a slave pays his master a sum in installments to become free.
عقد معاوضة (aqd mu'awada): A contract of exchange where each party gives something to receive something, like sale.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what happens to the kitaba contract if the mukatab dies with property sufficient to pay the kitaba?
A) The contract is dissolved immediately.
B) The contract remains and the kitaba is paid from his estate, and he is considered free.
C) The contract becomes void and his children become slaves.
D) The contract is transferred to his heirs as an obligation.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, the kitaba contract is not dissolved by the mukatab's death if he leaves sufficient property; it is paid from his estate and he becomes free in the last part of his life.
