Death of Mukatab with Purchased Son: Immediate Payment vs Installments
Intra-Hanafi dispute on payment method for purchased child

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi positions on the death of a mukatab leaving a purchased son and no wealth, and explain the qiyas that divides Imam Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn.

Core Idea
The purchased son does not inherit the kitabah deferment because he was not part of the contract.

When a mukatab (slave under contract of manumission) dies leaving only a purchased son and no wealth, a dispute arises within the Hanafi school about how the son must settle the kitabah payment. Imam Abu Hanifa holds that the son must pay the full amount immediately or be returned to slavery, while the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) allow him to pay in installments like a child born during kitabah. This lesson unpacks the reasoning behind each position and the subtle qiyas at play.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
Note the specific facts: a mukatab dies leaving a purchased son (a son he bought during his kitabah period) and no wealth to cover the kitabah payment.
The son is distinct from a child born during kitabah – this distinction is the crux of the dispute.
2
State Imam Abu Hanifa's position
Imam holds that the son must pay the entire kitabah immediately; if unable, he is returned to slavery. The deferment (ajal) of the kitabah contract does not extend to him because he was not a party to the original contract.
For Imam, the purchased son is not automatically included in the father's kitabah.
3
State the Sahibayn's position
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad hold that the son works to pay in installments, exactly like a child born during the father's kitabah. They analogize the purchased son to a mukatab himself, since the master can free him directly.
This view treats the son as effectively entering a new kitabah by virtue of his relationship to the father.
4
Analyze the qiyas arguments
The Sahibayn's qiyas: asl = child born in kitabah (pays installments), far' = purchased son, illa = both become like a mukatab (master can free them). Imam distinguishes: the purchased son was not subject to the original contract; his freedom on immediate payment is due to necessity (the father's situation like one who died with wealth), not contract extension.
Imam's objection to the illa is a classic example of qiyas khafi (hidden analogy) being rejected based on a missing attribute.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf and note tarjih
Practical difference: if the son cannot pay immediately, under Imam he becomes enslaved at once; under the Sahibayn, he can work and pay over time. The text does not specify which view is mufta bihi.
Later Hanafi works may prefer one view; here we only present the raw ikhtilaf.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لا وفاء: أي لم يترك مالا يفي ببدل الكتابة. ولدا مشترى بأن كان اشتراه في كتابته ومات المكاتب. يعني إذا مات المكاتب وترك ولدا مشترى فقط ولم يترك وفاء عجل الولد أداء البدل في الحال، وإلا رد إلى حاله رقيقا، وهذا عند الإمام. وعندهما يسعى على نجومه كالمولود في الكتابة؛ لأنه صار بمنزلة المكاتب حتى جاز للمولى إعتاقه كما يجوز إعتاق المكاتب بنفسه، بخلاف سائر أكساب المكاتب، فإنه لا يملك إعتاقه. وللإمام أن الأجل يثبت بالشرط في العقد، فيثبت في حق من دخل تحت الكتابة والمشترى لم يدخل تحت العقد؛ لأنه لم يضف إليه العقد، ولم يسر حكمه إليه؛ لكونه منفصلا وقت الكتابة. وأورد عليه أنه قد المكاتب إذا اشترى أباه أو ابنه دخل في كتابته، وأيضا لو لم يسر حكمه إليه لما عتق عند الإمام بأداء بدل الكتابة حالا. وأجيب أن المراد بدخول الولد المشترى في كتابة أبيه ليس لسراية حكم العقد الذي جرى بين المكاتب ومولاه إليهم، بل يجعل المكاتب مكاتبا لولده باشترائه تحقيقا للصلة، وبأن عتق الولد المشترى عنده بأداء بدل الكتابة حالا ليس لأجل السراية أيضا، بل لضرورة المكاتب إذ ذاك بمنزلة من مات عن وفاء، بخلاف المولود في الكتابة؛ لأنه ... [؟]
"No payment": meaning he did not leave property sufficient for the kitabah payment. A purchased son: that is, he bought him during his kitabah and the mukatab died. Meaning: if the mukatab died leaving only a purchased son and no sufficient payment, the son hastens the payment of the kitabah immediately, otherwise he is returned to his original state of slavery. This is according to Imam Abu Hanifa. According to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), he works to pay installments like a child born during kitabah, because he has become like a mukatab such that it is permitted for the master to free him just as it is permitted for the mukatab to free himself, unlike the other acquisitions of the mukatab, which he cannot free. For the Imam (Abu Hanifa): the term (deferment) is established by condition in the contract, so it is established for whoever enters under the kitabah, but the purchased son did not enter under the contract because the contract was not extended to him and its effect did not reach him due to his being separate at the time of the kitabah. It was objected that indeed the mukatab, when he buys his father or his son, enters under his kitabah, and also if its effect did not reach him, he would not be freed according to the Imam by paying the kitabah payment immediately. It was answered that the intention of the purchased son entering his father's kitabah is not due to the extension of the contract that occurred between the mukatab and his master to them, but rather the mukatab is made a mukatab for his son by purchasing him to achieve connection; and that the freeing of the purchased son according to him by immediate payment is not for extension, but due to necessity since the mukatab at that time is like one who died leaving sufficient payment, unlike the child born in kitabah because... [؟]
Source pages p. 212

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the death of a mukatab (a slave under contract of manumission) who leaves no wealth but a purchased son (a son he bought during his kitabah). The matn of Kanz al-Daqa'iq states the ruling briefly, and the commentary expands on the positions. According to Imam Abu Hanifa, the purchased son must immediately pay the full kitabah amount or be returned to slavery. Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani (the Sahibayn) hold that the son works to pay installments like a child born during the father's kitabah, because he is treated like a mukatab himself. The reasoning for Imam is that the deferment (ajal) is part of the contract and the purchased son was not party to the contract. An objection is raised: if the effect does not reach him, how can he be freed by immediate payment? The answer is that the son's entry into the kitabah is not by contract extension but by a separate arrangement for the sake of filial connection, and his freedom upon immediate payment is due to the father's situation being akin to dying with wealth. The debate continues, but the mufta bihi is not specified here.

Key Terms
مكاتب (mukatab): A slave who has entered into a contract with his master to earn and pay a specified sum for his freedom.
ولدا مشترى (waladan mushtara): A son purchased by the mukatab during his kitabah period.
بدل الكتابة (badal al-kitabah): The payment owed by the mukatab to the master for freedom.
أجل (ajal): Deferment or term of payment.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Imam Abu Hanifa, what happens if a mukatab dies leaving no wealth but a purchased son?
A) The son works to pay installments like a child born in kitabah.
B) The son must pay the full kitabah immediately or be returned to slavery.
C) The son inherits the father's kitabah and continues it.
D) The son is automatically freed because of the father's death.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, the purchased son of a mukatab is not automatically included in the contract; the deferment of payment does not extend to him according to Imam Abu Hanifa, while the Sahibayn analogize him to a child born in kitabah.

Option of Master When Slave Commits Crime Before Kitaba
Explains that master may pay ransom or hand over slave if slave commits crime before kitaba, with condition of ignorance.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the master's option in cases of a slave's crime before kitaba, and identify the condition of ignorance and the qiyas that supports the ruling.

Core Idea
Master chooses between handing over slave or paying ransom only if he was ignorant of the crime when making kitaba.

This passage addresses a nuanced scenario: a slave commits a crime, then the master contracts kitaba without knowing, and the slave later fails to pay. What are the master's obligations? The ruling gives the master an option, but with an important condition. Let's analyse the legal reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario and the master's initial action
The slave commits a crime (janiya). The master then makes the slave a mukatab (kitaba contract) without knowing about the crime. Later the slave becomes unable to pay the kitaba ('ajz). The master's obligations are to be determined.
2
Recognise the condition of ignorance
The ruling explicitly conditions the option on the master being 'jahil' (ignorant) at the time of kitaba. If the master knew, the option disappears and he must ransom.
This is a key distinction: ignorance preserves the master's choice.
3
Understand the qiyas analogy
The ruling draws an analogy (qiyas) with other acts that prevent handover: freeing, making mudabbar, having a child with a slave-girl, or selling. In all these cases, if done without knowledge, the master must pay the lesser of the slave's value or the ransom. Similarly, making mukatab is like those acts.
The common effective cause ('illa) is that the master prevented handover by his own action.
4
Note the exception: kitaba is a temporary obstacle
Unlike freeing or sale, kitaba is revocable if the slave fails to pay. Therefore, the victim's right does not transfer to the value; it remains attached to the slave. When the slave becomes unable (ajz), the obstacle is removed, and the original option returns.
5
Conclude the ruling
Thus, after ajz, the master may choose to hand over the slave or pay the ransom. If the master had known, he would be forced to ransom because he voluntarily assumed that obligation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن جنى عبد فكاتبه سيده جاهلا بها فعجز، فالمولى بالخيار إن شاء دفع العبد إلى ولي الجناية وإن شاء فداه بأرش الجناية؛ لأنه لما كاتبه وهو لا يعلم الجناية لزمه قيمته؛ لأنه لم يصر مختارا للفداء بالمكاتبة من غير علم، وقد امتنع الدفع بفعله، فصار مختارا للفداء، فيجب الأقل من قيمته ومن الأرش كما إذا أعتقه أو دبره أو استولد الأمة أو باعه بعد ما جنى من غير علم بها إلا أن المانع من الدفع وهو الكتابة على شرف الزوال، فلم ينتقل حق ولي الجناية من العبد إلى القيمة، فإذا عجز زال المانع، فيتخير بين الدفع والفداء الذين هما الموجب الأصلي في جناية العبد. وإنما قال: "فكاتبه سيده جاهلا" احترازا عما لو كاتبه عالما بالجناية فإنه يجب عليه الفداء؛ لأنه صار مختارا للفداء. (تكملة البحر الرائق: 125/8)
And if a slave commits a crime, then his master makes him a mukatab while ignorant of it, then he becomes unable [to pay], the master has the option: if he wishes, he hands over the slave to the guardian of the crime, and if he wishes, he ransoms him with the compensation of the crime. Because when he made him a mukatab while not knowing the crime, he is liable for his value; since he did not become a chooser of ransom by kitaba without knowledge, and he prevented handing over by his action, so he becomes a chooser of ransom, so the lesser of his value and the compensation is owed, just as if he freed him, or made him mudabbar, or had a child with the slave-girl, or sold him after he committed a crime without knowledge of it. Except that the obstacle to handing over – which is the kitaba – is on the verge of vanishing, so the right of the guardian of the crime did not transfer from the slave to the value. Thus when he becomes unable, the obstacle is removed, so he has a choice between handing over and ransoming, which are the original obligation in the crime of a slave. And he said: "his master made him a mukatab while ignorant" as a precaution against the case where he made him a mukatab knowing of the crime, for then he must ransom, because he became a chooser of ransom. (Takmila al-Bahr al-Ra'iq: 125/8)
Source pages p. 214

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with reasoning by analogy. It deals with a scenario where a slave commits a crime (janiya) – such as murder or battery – and then, before any legal action, his master enters into a kitaba contract (a contract of manumission) with him without knowing about the crime. The slave later becomes unable to pay the kitaba ('ajz). The question is: what are the master's obligations regarding the crime? The ruling states that the master has a choice: either hand over the slave to the victim's guardian (or the injured party) to be killed or enslaved, or pay the ransom (arj – compensation) for the crime. The reasoning is given: when the master made the kitaba while ignorant, he is liable for the slave's value (since he prevented handover by his own action), and he is obliged to pay the lesser of the slave's value or the ransom. This is analogous to cases where the master performs other acts after the crime without knowledge (like freeing, making mudabbar, having a child with an umm walad, or selling). However, since the kitaba is a temporary obstacle that can be removed by ajz, the right of the victim's guardian did not transfer from the slave to the value; it remained attached to the slave. Once ajz occurs, the obstacle is removed, and the original option returns. The author emphasizes that if the master knew of the crime when making the kitaba, then he must ransom (no choice), because he voluntarily assumed that obligation. This is an expansion of the matn with qiyas.

Key Terms
جناية (janiya): A crime committed by a slave against a third party, requiring compensation or retaliation.
فداء (fida'): Ransom; payment of compensation (arj) to free the slave from liability.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a master makes a slave mukatab knowing he committed a crime, what is the ruling?
A) The master has the option to hand over or ransom
B) The master must ransom (pay compensation)
C) The slave is freed from the crime
D) The victim may take the slave directly

Key Takeaway
The master's option to hand over or ransom a slave who committed a crime before kitaba exists only if he was ignorant of the crime at the time of the kitaba; knowledge obligates ransom.

Ruling on a Mukatab's Crime Before and After Court Judgment
Distinguishes ruling on crime of mukatab before and after court judgment.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the ruling on a mukatab's crime before and after court judgment, and explain the Hanafi position with its proof and the intra-Hanafi dispute.

Core Idea
A mukatab's crime shifts from master's option before judgment to a debt sale after judgment; the three imams agree, but Zufar dissents.

This passage addresses a nuanced question in Hanafi slave law: when a mukatab (a slave under a contract of manumission) commits a crime against a third party, how does the liability differ depending on whether a court judgment has been issued? The ruling changes dramatically: before judgment, the master has an option; after judgment, the liability becomes a debt that compels the sale of the mukatab. An intra-Hanafi dispute adds depth: Zufar and early Abu Yusuf dissent from the majority view. We will unpack the scenarios, the evidence, and the practical consequences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
The passage presents two distinct cases: (1) crime before judgment, then 'ajz (inability to pay kitaba); (2) crime after judgment, then 'ajz. Note the difference in ruling.
The key variable is whether the court has ruled on the crime before the mukatab becomes unable.
2
Analyze the first scenario (before judgment)
The master has the option to hand over the mukatab or pay the ransom. Before 'ajz, the mukatab himself is liable for the lesser of his value or the compensation. Understand the reasoning: after 'ajz, he reverts to being a regular slave; before 'ajz, handing over is impossible due to kitaba.
The analogy to mudabbar and umm walad clarifies the rule: the master pays the lesser amount.
3
Analyze the second scenario (after judgment)
If judgment is passed before 'ajz, the liability becomes a debt (dayn) for which the mukatab is sold. This is the position of the three imams (Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, Muhammad).
The analogy to an absconding stolen slave supports this ruling.
4
Examine the intra-Hanafi dispute
Zufar and early Abu Yusuf argue that the kitaba obstacle remains, so no sale occurs – the value is due but the mukatab is not sold. Understand their dalil and the majority's response.
The majority argues that the obstacle is uncertain (kitaba may dissolve), so the original rule of handing over can be abandoned only by judgment.
5
Apply the rule to a concrete case
Given a fact pattern, determine the outcome: before judgment the master chooses; after judgment the mukatab is sold to pay the debt.
The mufta bihi is the majority view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكذا إن جنى مكاتب ولم يقض به فعجز، يخير المولى بين الدفع والفداء؛ لأنه لما عجز صار قنا، وجناية القن يخير فيها المولى. وقبل أن يعجز يجب على المكاتب الأقل من قيمته ومن الأرش؛ لأن دفعه متعذر، وهو أحق بكسبه من المولى، وموجب الجناية عند تعذر الدفع يجب على من له الكسب. ألا ترى أن جناية المدبر وأم الولد توجب على المولى الأقل من القيمة ومن الأرش؛ لأنه أحق بكسبهما. فإن قضى به عليه في كتابته فعجز، فهو دين عليه يباع فيه؛ لأن الحق انتقل من الرقبة إلى القيمة بالقضاء، وهذا عند علمائنا الثلاثة. وقال زفر: تجب عليه قيمته ولا يباع، وهو قول أبي يوسف أولا؛ لأن المانع من الدفع وقت الجناية موجود، وهو الكتابة، فلا تتغير كجناية المدبر وأم الولد. ولنا: أن الأصل في جناية العبد الدفع، وإنما يصار إلى القيمة عند تعذر الدفع، والمانع هنا متردد؛ لاحتمال انفساخ الكتابة، فلا يثبت الانتقال عن الموجب الأصلي إلا بالقضاء، والصلح عن الرضاء، وبالموت عن الوفاء، وهو نظير المغصوب إذا أبق لا يجب عليه القيمة إلا بالقضاء، حتى لو رجع قبل القضاء، يكون لمولاه، ولو رجع بعد القضاء يكون للغاصب. (تكملة البحر الرائق: 125/8، 126/8)
Similarly, if a mukatab commits a crime and it is not judged upon him, then he becomes unable, the master is given a choice between handing over and ransom; because when he became unable, he becomes a slave, and in the crime of a slave the master has a choice. And before he becomes unable, the mukatab is liable for the lesser of his value and the compensation; because handing him over is impossible, and he (the mukatab) has more right to his earnings than the master, and the obligation for the crime when handing over is impossible falls on the one who has the earnings. Don't you see that the crime of a mudabbar and umm walad obligates the master the lesser of value and compensation, because he has more right to their earnings? And if a judgment is passed against him while he is in kitaba, then he becomes unable, it becomes a debt for which he is sold; because the right transfers from the neck to the value by judgment, and this is according to our three imams. Zufar said: His value is required and he is not sold, and this was the first opinion of Abu Yusuf; because the obstacle to handing over at the time of crime exists, namely the kitaba, so it does not change like the crime of mudabbar and umm walad. Our proof: that the original in the crime of a slave is handing over, and recourse to value is only when handing over is impossible, and here the obstacle is uncertain due to the possibility of the kitaba being dissolved, so transfer from the original obligation is not established except by judgment, or by amicable settlement, or by death while solvent. It is analogous to the case of a stolen slave who absconds: value is not required except by judgment; if he returns before judgment, he belongs to the owner; if after judgment, he belongs to the thief. (Takmila al-Bahr al-Ra'iq: 125/8, 126/8)
Source pages p. 215

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with reasoning and includes an intra-Hanafi dispute. It covers two scenarios regarding a mukatab (a slave under kitaba) who commits a crime (janiya) against a third party. First scenario: the crime occurs during the kitaba, but no court judgment has been passed, and then the mukatab becomes unable to pay ('ajz). In this case, the master has the option to either hand over the mukatab (to be subjected to the crime's punishment or compensation) or pay the ransom (compensation). The reasoning: after 'ajz, the mukatab reverts to being a regular slave (qann), and for a slave's crime the master has an option. Before 'ajz, the mukatab himself is liable for the lesser of his value or the compensation, because handing him over is not possible (due to the kitaba right) and he has priority over his earnings. This is analogous to the case of a mudabbar (slave to be freed upon master's death) or umm walad (slave concubine who bore a child): their master is liable for the lesser of value or compensation. Second scenario: if a judgment is passed against the mukatab for the crime (determining the liability) while he is still in kitaba, and then he becomes unable, the liability becomes a debt (dayn) for which the mukatab can be sold. The reason is that the right of the victim transferred from the slave's neck to the value (qimah) by the judgment. This is the position of the three imams (Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, Muhammad). Zufar disagreed, holding that his value is required but he is not sold (like the first opinion of Abu Yusuf), because the obstacle (kitaba) existed at the time of crime and does not change. The Hanafi response: the original rule for a slave's crime is handing over; recourse to value is only when handing over is impossible. Here the obstacle is uncertain (since kitaba may dissolve), so transfer from the original obligation occurs only by judgment, settlement, or death with solvency. An analogy is made with an absconding stolen slave.

Key Terms
قضاء (qada'): A court judgment against the mukatab for the crime, determining liability.
مدبر (mudabbar): A slave who is promised freedom upon the master's death.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the three imams, what happens if a judgment is passed against a mukatab for a crime and then he becomes unable to pay the kitaba?
A) The master has the option to hand over or ransom
B) The liability becomes a debt and the mukatab is sold
C) The crime is waived entirely
D) The victim must forgive the debt

Key Takeaway
A mukatab's crime liability changes from a master's option before judgment to a mandatory debt after judgment, per the dominant Hanafi view.

Effect of Compulsion on Contracts: Option to Annul
Ruling that a coerced contract is fasid (corrupt) with option to annul or confirm

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why a coerced contract is fasid (corrupt) in the Hanafi school and identify the coerced party's option to annul or confirm.

Core Idea
A contract made under duress is corrupt but curable; the coerced has the right to annul or ratify.

This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq addresses a common dilemma: what happens when someone is forced to sell, buy, or lease? The Hanafi school provides a nuanced ruling that balances the need for consent with the possibility of later ratification. We will explore the reasoning behind this and the disagreement with Zufar, who considered the contract suspended rather than corrupt.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What contract is being considered (sale/purchase/acknowledgment/lease)? What is the legal classification (fasid)?
The passage covers multiple transactions under duress.
2
Understand the condition of consent
Why is consent (rida) a condition for validity? How does duress negate it?
Both overwhelming and non-overwhelming duress equally nullify consent in contracts.
3
Examine the Hanafi proof
The proof is rational: the pillar (offer/acceptance) is valid, but the condition is missing, causing corruption (fasād) not suspension (tawaqquf).
Contrast with Zufar's view that the contract is suspended.
4
Analyze the intra-Hanafi dispute
Zufar argues it is mawquf because ratification makes it valid. The majority respond that ratification removes the defect, like other corrupt sales.
This is a legitimate ikhtilaf within the school.
5
Determine the operative ruling
The muftā bihi is that the contract is fasid and the coerced has the option (khiyār) to annul or confirm after duress ends.
Ownership transfers upon possession but is subject to rescission.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أكره على بيع إلخ [ماله بأن قال له: إن لم تبع عبدك قتلتك أو ضربتك أو حبستك هذا شروع في تفصيل صور الإكراه وبيان أحكامها، ولما كان الإكراه تارة يقع في حقوق العباد وأخرى في حقوق الله تعالى، وحق العبد مقدم لحاجة العبد إليه قدمه، ولما كان البيع والشراء والإقرار والإجارة مما يحتاج إلى الرضا، والإكراه بكلا نوعيه: الملجئ وغير الملجئ، يفسد الرضا الذي هو شرط لصحة هذه التصرفات ذكرها المصنف أولا، وجمع بين نوعي الإكراه، فقال: "بقتل أو ضرب"؛ ليعلم أن حكمهما في هذه التصرفات سواء، وإنما قال: "بضرب شديد أو حبس مديد"؛ لأنه لو قال: "أضربك سوطا أو سوطين" أو "أحبسك يوما أو يومين" لا يكون إكراها إلا إذا قال: "لأضر على رأسك أو عينك أو مذاكيرك"؛ لأنه قد يفضي إلى التلف وإلا إذا كان المكره صاحب عز ومرتبة يعلم أنه يستضر بضرب سوط أو حبس يوم؛ لما قلنا أنه يختلف باختلاف أحوال الناس. ملتقط من التكملة والفتح وغيرهما) إلخ لو أكره على بيع وشراء وإقرار وإجارة بإكراه ملجئ بأن قال: إن لم تبع هذا العبد مثلا، أو إن لم تشتر أو إن لم تقر لي بألف أو إن لم تؤجرني دارك قتلتك أو أكره على هذه الأشياء بإكراه غير ملجئ بأن قال: إن لم تفعل ضربتك ضربا شديدا أو حبستك حبسا مديدا، فباع أو اشترى ثم زال الإكراه، خير المكره بفتح الراء بين أن يمضي البيع أو يفسخه؛ لأن صحة هذه العقود تعتمد التراضي والإكراه يعدمه، فيفوت شرط صحة العقد، فيفسد، وكذلك الإقرار؛ لأنه حجة مرجحة بجانب الصدق على الكذب، فإذا أكره احتمل أنه كذب في إقراره دفعا لضرر الإكراه، فلا يثبت قوله. وقال زفر: لا يثبت الملك؛ لأنه بيع موقوف، وليس بفاسد، ألا ترى أنه لو أجاز بعد زوال الإكراه جاز، ولو كان فاسدا لما جاز، ولنا: أن ركن البيع وهو الإيجاب والقبول صدر من أهله مضافا إلى محله الفاسد؛ لعدم شرطه وهو التراضي، وفوات الشرط تأثيره في فساد العقد لا التوقف، وإنما نفذ بالإجازة؛ لارتفاع المفسد عدم التراضي، فصار كسائر البياعات الفاسدة.
He was compelled to sell etc. [His property, by saying to him: 'If you do not sell your slave, I will kill you, beat you, or imprison you.' This is the start of detailing the cases of compulsion and explaining their rulings. Since compulsion sometimes occurs in rights of people and sometimes in rights of Allah, and the right of the servant is given precedence due to the servant's need, he presented it first. Since sale, purchase, acknowledgment, and lease require consent, and compulsion of both types (overwhelming and non-overwhelming) spoils consent which is a condition for the validity of these transactions, the author mentioned them first and combined the two types of compulsion saying 'by killing or beating' to indicate that their ruling in these transactions is the same. He only said 'by severe beating or prolonged imprisonment' because if he said 'I will beat you with one or two lashes' or 'imprison you for a day or two', it is not compulsion unless he said 'I will harm your head, eye, or private parts' because that may lead to destruction; except if the coerced is a person of honor and status who is harmed by a single lash or a day's imprisonment—as we said, it varies according to people's conditions. (Compiled from al-Takmila, al-Fath, etc.)] So, if he is compelled to a sale, purchase, acknowledgment, or lease by overwhelming duress, e.g., he says 'If you do not sell this slave, or do not buy, or do not acknowledge a thousand for me, or do not lease me your house, I will kill you', or by non-overwhelming duress, e.g., 'I will beat you severely or imprison you for long', then after the duress ends, the coerced has the choice between executing the sale or annulling it, because the validity of these contracts depends on mutual consent, and compulsion negates it, so the condition of validity is lost, hence the contract is corrupt. Similarly for acknowledgment because it is a proof inclined towards truth over falsehood; if coerced, it may be false to avoid harm, so his statement is not established. Zufar said: ownership is not established because it is a suspended sale, not corrupt; do you not see that if he ratifies after the duress ends, it becomes valid—if it were corrupt, it would not become valid. Our proof: the pillar of sale, offer and acceptance, issued from a qualified person directed to a valid subject, but corruptly due to missing the condition of consent; the absence of condition causes corruption not suspension. It only becomes effective by ratification because the source of corruption—lack of consent—is removed, so it becomes like other corrupt sales.
Source pages p. 226

Commentary
This passage explains the ruling on contracts made under duress. It first categorizes compulsion into rights of people (like sale) and rights of Allah (like prayer), and because people's needs are prioritized, the author discusses financial transactions first. The key point is that both overwhelming and non-overwhelming duress invalidate consent, which is a condition for these contracts. The commentary clarifies that only severe threats (killing, maiming, severe beating, long imprisonment) constitute duress; minor threats do not, unless the person is of high status to whom even a small humiliation is severe. After duress ends, the coerced has the option to confirm or annul the contract. The text then presents an intra-Hanafi dispute: Imam Zufar argues that the contract is suspended (mawquf) rather than corrupt (fasid), because ratification can validate it. The Hanafi majority respond that the contract is indeed fasid because the missing condition (consent) renders it defective; ratification only removes the defect, making it like other corrupt sales that can be rectified.

Key Terms
إكراه ملجئ وغير ملجئ (ikrah mulji' wa ghayr mulji'): Overwhelming duress (threat of death/maiming) and non-overwhelming duress (threats of severe beating, long imprisonment).
بيع فاسد (bay' fasid): A corrupt sale that has all pillars but misses a condition (like consent). It is not void ab initio but can be validated.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi majority, what is the legal status of a sale contract made under overwhelming duress (threat of death) if the coerced person does not ratify it?
A) The contract is batil (void) from the start and no transfer of ownership occurs.
B) The contract is sahih (valid) because the offer and acceptance were made.
C) The contract is fasid (corrupt); the coerced has the option to annul or confirm it.
D) The contract is mawquf (suspended) until the coercer's threat is removed.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, a contract made under duress is fasid (corrupt), giving the coerced party the option to annul or confirm; ratification cures the defect.

Liability and Recourse in Corrupt Sales with Coercion
Ruling with proof via qiyas to ghasib

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the liability rules when goods perish in a coerced sale and apply the rule of recourse against the buyer or coercer.

Core Idea
Possession under a fasid sale creates liability; the coerced seller can choose to sue the buyer or the coercer.

This lesson examines a nuanced scenario in corrupted (fasid) sales: when a seller is coerced into selling, and the goods perish in the buyer's hands. Hanafi fiqh provides specific rules on who bears the loss and what recourse the coerced seller has. The ruling is built on the principle that possession under a corrupt contract is a liability (daman), and the coercer is treated analogously to a usurper (ghasib).

Guided Path
1
Identify the core scenario
A seller is coerced into a sale; the buyer takes possession voluntarily (non-coerced) and the goods perish in his hands.
The condition 'buyer not coerced' is critical; if the buyer is also coerced, the rule changes.
2
State the primary ruling
The buyer is liable to pay the value of the goods to the seller. This is because possession under a fasid contract is a liability (daman).
The liability is for the value (qimah) not the substitute (mithl), though the commentator suggests 'substitute' would be more comprehensive.
3
Understand the alternative recourse
The coerced seller may instead sue the coercer (the one who forced the sale). This is analogous to the case of a usurper (ghasib) and a sub-usurper.
The coercer is treated as the instrument of destruction and can be held liable.
4
Analyze the legal consequences of recourse
If the seller sues the coercer, the coercer can then seek reimbursement from the buyer. If the seller sues the buyer, the buyer cannot recourse to the coercer because his ownership is established subject to the seller's right of rescission.
This balances the rights and prevents double recovery.
5
Apply the analogy to ghasib
The Hanafi position uses qiyas: the coercer is like a usurper who takes property; the buyer is like a subsequent usurper. The owner (here the coerced seller) can choose whom to hold liable.
This is a clear example of istihsan and qiyas in Hanafi fiqh.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقبض الثمن طوعا إجازة أي بالبيع ونحوه مكرها أي قبض المبيع أي قبض البائع المكره أي حال كونه طائعاً كالتسليم طائعا، وإن هلك المبيع في يد المشتري وهو غير مكره، والبائع مكره، ضمن قيمته للبائع، وللمكره أن يضمن المكره، أي قيمة المبيع بفتح الراء وهو بفتح الراء أي المشتري: إذا هلك المبيع في يده والحال أنه لم يكرهه أحد، والبائع مكره بفتح الراء بالبيع والتسليم؛ لأنه قبضه بحكم عقد فاسد فكان مضمونا عليه بالقيمة، وقيد بقوله "والمشتري غير مكره"؛ لأنه لو كان مكرها، وهلك المبيع في يده من غير تعد لا يضمن ويهلك أمانة. ولو قال المصنف: "ضمن بدله" لكان أولى؛ لأنه يشمل المثلي والقيمي. وللمكره أن يضمن إلخ أي في الصورة المذكورة وهي هلاك المبيع في يد المشتري وهو غير مكره، والبائع مكره يضمن المشتري قيمته للبائع، وإن شاء البائع المكره بفتح الراء يضمن المكره بكسر الراء؛ لأن المكره آلة للمكره بكسر الراء فيما رجع إلى الإتلاف وإن لم يكن له آلة في حق التكلم لعدم الصلاحية؛ لأن التكلم بلسان الغير لا يمكن فصار كأنه دفع مال البائع إلى المشتري، فيضمن أيهما شاء كالغاصب وغاصب الغاصب، فإن ضمن البائع المكره بكسر الراء رجع هو على المشتري بالقيمة؛ لأنه بأداء الضمان ملكه، فقام مقام المالك المكره، فيكون مالكا له من وقت وجود السبب بالاستناد ولو ضمن المشتري ثبت ملك المشتري فيه، ولا يرجع على المكره بكسر الراء؛ لأنه ملكه بالشراء والقبض، غير أنه توقف نفوذه على سقوط حق المكره من الفسخ، فإذا.
And receiving the price voluntarily is ratification. i.e., the coerced seller receiving the price while being voluntary is like delivering the goods voluntarily. If the sold item perishes in the hands of the buyer while he is not coerced and the seller is coerced, the buyer is liable for its value to the seller. And the coerced person (with fatha on ra) may hold liable the coercer (with kasra on ra). i.e., the buyer: if the sold item perishes in his hands while no one coerced him and the seller is coerced (with fatha on ra) in the sale and delivery, because he took possession under a corrupt contract, so it is a liability upon him for the value. He restricted by saying 'the buyer is not coerced' because if he were coerced, and the item perishes without transgression, he is not liable; it perishes as a trust. If the author had said 'liable for its substitute' it would be better because it includes fungible and non-fungible. 'And the coerced may hold liable etc.' i.e., in the mentioned case: the item perishes while the buyer is not coerced, the buyer is liable to the seller for the value. If the coerced seller (with fatha on ra) wishes, he may hold the coercer (with kasra on ra) liable, because the coerced is an instrument for the coercer regarding destruction, though not for speech because speaking through another's tongue is impossible; so it is as if he paid the seller's property to the buyer. He may hold liable whichever he wishes, like the case of a usurper and the usurper's usurper. If the coerced (with fatha) holds the coercer (with kasra) liable, then the coercer can recourse to the buyer for the value, because by paying the liability he owns the item, so he stands in place of the coerced owner, becoming owner from the time of cause retroactively. If the buyer pays the value, he owns the item and cannot recourse to the coercer, because he purchased and took possession, but its effectiveness depends on the lapse of the coerced's right to rescind.
Source pages p. 227

Commentary
This passage covers the liability and recourse when a coerced sale's goods are destroyed. It begins by stating that if the coerced seller voluntarily receives the price or delivers the goods, this constitutes ratification of the sale. Then it addresses the scenario: if the buyer (who is not coerced) takes possession and the goods perish, he is liable to the seller for the value, because possession under a corrupt contract is a liability. But if the buyer is also coerced, he is not liable unless he transgresses. The coerced seller has the option to claim the value from the buyer or from the coercer. This is analogous to the case of a usurper (ghasib) and a sub-usurper. If the seller claims from the coercer, the coercer can then recourse to the buyer. If the buyer pays the value, he becomes owner and cannot claim from the coercer, because his ownership is established but subject to the seller's right of rescission until it lapses.

Key Terms
قبض (qabd): Taking possession of the sold item. Here, possession under a fasid contract creates liability.
ضمان (daman): Liability to pay compensation, here the value of the destroyed item.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the scenario where a coerced sale's goods perish in the hands of a non-coerced buyer, from whom can the coerced seller recover the value?
A) Only from the buyer.
B) Only from the coercer.
C) From either the buyer or the coercer, at his choice.
D) From both the buyer and the coercer jointly.

Key Takeaway
The coerced seller can demand compensation for destroyed goods from either the buyer or the coercer, mirroring the rule of a usurper and sub-usurper.

Buyu — Sales and Trade

Coerced Sale: Fasid Nature and Proof
Ruling on coerced sale and intra-Hanafi dispute.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on coerced sale as fasid and explain the proof by distinguishing between pillars and conditions.

Core Idea
A coerced sale is fasid because the condition of mutual consent is missing; the pillars remain intact.

Contracts require mutual consent. When coercion invalidates consent, the sale becomes defective but not void. The Hanafi school holds it as fasid (corrupt) with ownership passing on possession, unlike Imam Zufar who considered it suspended. This lesson unpacks the reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is a sale conducted under coercion (ikrah). The Hanafi ruling is that it is fasid (corrupt), not batil (void) and not mawquf (suspended).
Fasid means the pillars are sound but a condition is missing.
2
Locate the dalil
The proof is an aqli (rational) argument: the pillar of sale (ijab and qabul) is present from the proper parties, but the condition of mutual consent (taradi) is absent.
The text states: 'ana rukn al-bay'... 'li'adam shartihi wa huwa al-taradi'.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The absence of a condition leads to fasad (corruption) of the contract, not ta'liq (suspension). This is by analogy with other contracts where missing a condition results in fasad.
The condition is essential for validity; its absence corrupts the contract but does not nullify it ab initio.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
Imam Zufar (within the Hanafi school) held that the sale is mawquf (suspended) because it can become valid through ratification, which would be impossible if it were fasid.
Zufar's argument: 'law kana fasidan lama jaza' (if it were fasid, ratification would not validate it).
5
Evaluate the tarjih
The majority (Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, Muhammad) hold the fasid view, and it is the mufta bihi (operative for fatwa). Ratification is possible because the defect (lack of consent) is removed, as in other fasid contracts.
The Hanafi response: 'innama nafadha bi al-ijazah; li-irtifa' al-mufsid' (it becomes effective by ratification because the corrupting factor is removed).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أحكام الإكراه كتاب الإكراه البيع أو يفسخه ويثبت به الملك عند القبض للفساد، وقبض الثمن طوعا إجازة أي بالبيع ونحوه مكرها أي قبض المبيع أي قبض البائع المكره أي حال كونه طائعاً كالتسليم طائعا، وإن هلك المبيع في يد المشتري وهو غير مكره، والبائع مكره، كتسليم البائع المكره المبيع ضمن قيمته للبائع، وللمكره أن يضمن المكره، أي قيمة المبيع بفتح الراء بكسر الراء بفتح الراء وهو ويثبت به الملك إلخ : أي يثبت بالشراء الملك للمشتري بعد القبض؛ لكونه فاسدا ومقتضى العقد الفاسد ثبوت الملك عند القبض، وقال زفر : لا يثبت الملك؛ لأنه بيع موقوف، وليس بفاسد، ألا ترى أنه لو أجاز بعد زوال الإكراه جاز، ولو كان فاسدا لما جاز، ولنا: أن ركن البيع وهو الإيجاب والقبول صدر من أهله مضافا إلى محله الفاسد؛ لعدم شرطه وهو التراضي، وفوات الشرط تأثيره في فساد العقد لا التوقف، وإنما نفذ بالإجازة؛ لارتفاع المفسد عدم التراضي، فصار كسائر البياعات الفاسدة إلا أنه لا ينقطع به حق استرداد المبيع؛ وإن تداولته الأيدي، بخلاف سائر البياعات الفاسدة؛ لأن الفساد فيها لحق الله تعالى، وقد تعلق بالبيع الثاني حق العبد، وحقه مقدم لحاجته بإذنه، أما هنا الرد لحق العبد، وهما سواء، فلا يبطل حق الأول بحق الثاني. [رمز الحقائق: ٣٠٨/٢] وقبض الثمن إلخ: أي قبض البائع المكره الثمن برضاه أو تسليمه المبيع إلى المشتري طائعا إجازة للبيع؛ لأن القبض والتسليم طائعا دليل الرضا، وهو الشرط، بخلاف ما إذا أكره على الهبة دون التسليم وسلم، حيث لا يكون إجازة ولو سلم طائعا؛ لأن غرض المكره إنما هو استحقاق الموهوب له لا مجرد لفظ الهبة، والاستحقاق لا يثبت فيها بدون التسليم فكان التسليم فيها داخلا في الإكراه، والإكراه في البيع يثبت بنفس العقد، فلم يكن التسليم فيه داخلا في الإكراه فافترقا رمز الحقائق: ٣٠٩/٢] وإن هلك المبيع إلخ أي يضمن المشتري إذا هلك المبيع في يده والحال أنه لم يكرهه أحد، والبائع مكره بفتح الراء بالبيع والتسليم؛ لأنه قبضه بحكم عقد فاسد فكان مضمونا عليه بالقيمة، وقيد بقوله "والمشتري غير مكره"؛ لأنه لو كان مكرها، وهلك المبيع في يده من غير تعد لا يضمن ويهلك أمانة ولو قال المصنف: "ضمن بدله" لكان أولى؛ لأنه يشمل المثلي والقيمي. (تكملة، ملا مسكين) وللمكره أن يضمن إلخ أي في الصورة المذكورة وهي هلاك المبيع في يد المشتري وهو غير مكره، والبائع مكره يضمن المشتري قيمته للبائع، وإن شاء البائع المكره بفتح الراء يضمن المكره بكسر الراء؛ لأن المكره آلة للمكره بكسر الراء فيما رجع إلى الإتلاف وإن لم يكن له آلة في حق التكلم لعدم الصلاحية؛ لأن التكلم بلسان الغير لا يمكن فصار كأنه دفع مال البائع إلى المشتري، فيضمن أيهما شاء كالغاصب وغاصب الغاصب، فإن ضمن البائع المكره بكسر الراء رجع هو على المشتري بالقيمة؛ لأنه بأداء الضمان ملكه، فقام مقام المالك المكره، فيكون مالكا له من وقت وجود السبب بالاستناد ولو ضمن المشتري ثبت ملك المشتري فيه، ولا يرجع على المكره بكسر الراء؛ لأنه ملكه بالشراء والقبض، غير أنه توقف نفوذه على سقوط حق المكره من الفسخ، فإذا = ضمنه قيمته نفذ ملكه فيه كسائر البياعات الفاسدة تكملة البحر الرائق: ١٤٤/٨] ولو كان المشتري باعه من آخر وباع الآخر من آخر حتى تداولته البياعات الفاسدة نفذ الكل بتضمين الأول، وله أن يضمن من شاء المشتريين، وجازت البياعات التي بعد وبطل ما قبله بخلاف ما إذا أجاز المكره أحد من هذه البياعات حيث يجوز الكل ما قبله وما بعده ويأخذ الثمن من المشتري الأول، ووجه الفرق بين الإجازة والتضمين مذكور في "الفتح" فليطالع. [رمز الحقائق: ٣١٠/٢]
Rulings of coercion: The book of coercion. The sale or its annulment, and ownership is established by it upon possession due to its corrupt nature, and receiving the price willingly is ratification. That is, by the sale and similar, being coerced, i.e., receipt of the sold item, i.e., the receipt of the coerced seller, i.e., while being willing, like delivery willingly. And if the sold item perishes in the hand of the buyer while he is not coerced, and the seller is coerced, like the delivery of the coerced seller of the sold item, he guarantees its value to the seller, and the coerced [seller] may hold liable the coercer, i.e., the value of the sold item. (grammatical marks) And ownership is established etc.: i.e., ownership is established for the buyer through purchase after possession because it is corrupt (fasid), and the requirement of a corrupt contract is establishment of ownership upon possession. Zufar said: ownership is not established because it is a suspended sale, not corrupt. Do you not see that if he ratifies after the coercion ceases, it is valid, and if it were corrupt it would not be valid. And for us: the pillar of sale, which is offer and acceptance, issued from its proper party, related to its place, but it is corrupt due to the absence of its condition, which is mutual consent, and the lack of the condition affects the corruption of the contract, not suspension. It is only effective upon ratification because the corrupting factor—absence of consent—is removed, so it becomes like other corrupt sales, except that the right to reclaim the sold item is not cut off even if it passes through many hands, unlike other corrupt sales because the corruption there is for the right of Allah, and the right of the servant (buyer) is attached to the second sale, and his right is given priority due to his need with his permission. Here, the return is for the right of the servant, and both are equal, so the first right is not invalidated by the second. [Ramooz al-Haqaiq 2:308] And receipt of the price etc.: i.e., the coerced seller's receipt of the price willingly or his delivery of the sold item to the buyer willingly is ratification of the sale because receipt and delivery willingly are evidence of consent, which is the condition. Unlike if he is coerced into a gift without delivery, and he delivers, where it is not ratification even if he delivers willingly, because the purpose of the coerced person is only entitlement of the donee, not the mere wording of gift, and entitlement is not established without delivery, so delivery becomes included in the coercion. In sale, coercion is established by the contract itself, so delivery is not included in it, so they differ. [Ramooz al-Haqaiq 2:309] And if the sold item perishes etc.: i.e., the buyer is liable if the sold item perishes in his hand while he was not coerced, and the seller is coerced (with fatha on ra) by the sale and delivery, because he took possession by virtue of a corrupt contract, so it is a guarantee against him by value. And the qualification "and the buyer is not coerced" because if he were coerced and the item perished in his hand without transgression, he is not liable and it perishes as a trust. If the author had said "guarantees its substitute" it would have been better because it includes fungible and non-fungible. (Takmilah, Mulla Miskin) And the coerced may hold liable etc.: i.e., in the mentioned case of the perish of sold item in the buyer's hand while he is not coerced and the seller is coerced (with fatha ra), the buyer guarantees its value to the seller. And if the coerced seller (with fatha ra) wishes, he may hold the coercer (with kasra ra) liable because the coerced is a tool for the coercer regarding destruction, even though he is not a tool for speech due to inability, because speaking by another's tongue is impossible, so it is as if he handed over the seller's property to the buyer. So he may hold liable whichever he wishes, like the usurper and the usurper's usurper. If the coerced seller (with kasra ra) pays the guarantee, he recourses upon the buyer for the value because by paying the guarantee he becomes owner, and he becomes like the coerced owner, so he is owner from the time of the cause. If the buyer guarantees, the buyer's ownership is established, and he does not recourse upon the coercer (with kasra ra) because he owns it by purchase and possession, except that its effectiveness is suspended until the right of the coercer to annul falls. Then if he guarantees its value, his ownership takes effect like in other corrupt sales. [Takmilah al-Bahr al-Raiq 8:144] And if the buyer sold it to another, and that other sold it to another until multiple corrupt sales occurred, all are effective by the first's payment of guarantee, and he may hold liable whichever of the buyers he wishes, and the later sales are valid and what preceded is void, unlike if the coerced person ratified one of these sales, where all become valid—the preceding and following—and he takes the price from the first buyer. The distinction between ratification and guarantee is mentioned in al-Fath, so consult it. [Ramooz al-Haqaiq 2:310]
Source pages p. 227

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on rulings of coerced sale from the book Kanz al-Daqaiq. The main ruling is that a sale conducted under coercion (ikrah) is fasid (corrupt/voidable), not batil (void). Unlike the position of Imam Zufar who considered it a suspended sale (mawquf) pending ratification, the Hanafi school (Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, Muhammad) holds that the pillars (ijab and qabul) are present but the condition of mutual consent is missing, leading to fasad. Ownership vests in the buyer upon possession (qabd) because that is the characteristic of a fasid contract. The coerced seller has the right to annul the sale or can ratify it by willingly receiving the price or delivering the item, which signals consent. If the purchased item perishes in the buyer’s hand while the buyer is not coerced, the buyer must pay the value to the seller. The coerced seller also has the option to hold the coercer (the one who forced him) liable instead, treating the coercer as the effective cause of the loss. The discussion includes detailed comparisons with other corrupt sales and the difference between ratification and guarantee. The passage provides a full legal analysis within the Hanafi framework, referencing the authority of the school.

Key Terms
إكراه (ikrah): Coercion: compelling someone to do something against their will, which invalidates consent.
فاسد (fasid): Corrupt: a contract that is valid in pillars but deficient in conditions, making it voidable rather than void.
مكره (mukrah): The coerced person (with فتح on ra): one who is forced to act.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, a sale under coercion is:
A) Batil (void) and cannot be ratified.
B) Fasid (voidable), and ownership vests upon possession.
C) Sahih but with an option to annul.
D) Mawquf (suspended) pending ratification.

Key Takeaway
A coerced sale is fasid; ownership vests upon possession, and the seller retains the option to annul unless ratification occurs.

Buyu — Sales and Trade

Effects of Coerced Sale: Ratification, Guarantee, and Liability
Expansion on effects of coerced sale.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the effects of a coerced sale including ratification by willing receipt/delivery, guarantee upon perishing, and the option to hold the coercer liable.

Core Idea
Ratification cures the defect; the buyer is liable for value if the item perishes; the seller can hold the coercer liable.

Once a coerced sale occurs, several legal consequences arise. The seller can ratify the sale by willingly accepting payment or delivering the item. The buyer becomes liable if the item perishes without coercion. The seller may also seek compensation from the coercer. This lesson examines these effects in detail.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act of ratification
Ratification occurs when the coerced seller willingly receives the price or willingly delivers the sold item to the buyer. This signals consent and cures the defect.
The text states: 'qabd al-thaman tau'an ijazah' (receiving the price willingly is ratification).
2
Distinguish from gift (hiba)
In the case of a coerced gift, delivery under coercion does not constitute ratification because the purpose of the gift is entitlement, which requires delivery. In sale, coercion is established by the contract itself, so delivery is not part of the coercion.
The text explains: 'al-ikrah fi al-bay' yathbutu bi nafs al-'aqd, fa lam yakun al-taslim fihi dakhilan fi al-ikrah'.
3
Understand liability for perishing
If the sold item perishes in the hands of the buyer while he is not coerced, the buyer is liable for its value. If the buyer is also coerced, he is not liable (it is a trust).
Liability arises because the buyer took possession under a fasid contract, which is a guarantee upon the buyer.
4
Understand the option to hold the coercer liable
The coerced seller can choose to hold either the buyer (if he paid) or the coercer (the one who forced him) liable for the value. If the seller pays the buyer's guarantee, he can recover from the coercer. This is by analogy to the case of a usurper and his victim.
The text says: 'fa yadman ayyahuma sha'a ka al-ghasib wa ghasil al-ghasib'.

Source Text

[رمز الحقائق: ٣٠٨/٢] وقبض الثمن إلخ: أي قبض البائع المكره الثمن برضاه أو تسليمه المبيع إلى المشتري طائعا إجازة للبيع؛ لأن القبض والتسليم طائعا دليل الرضا، وهو الشرط، بخلاف ما إذا أكره على الهبة دون التسليم وسلم، حيث لا يكون إجازة ولو سلم طائعا؛ لأن غرض المكره إنما هو استحقاق الموهوب له لا مجرد لفظ الهبة، والاستحقاق لا يثبت فيها بدون التسليم فكان التسليم فيها داخلا في الإكراه، والإكراه في البيع يثبت بنفس العقد، فلم يكن التسليم فيه داخلا في الإكراه فافترقا رمز الحقائق: ٣٠٩/٢] وإن هلك المبيع إلخ أي يضمن المشتري إذا هلك المبيع في يده والحال أنه لم يكرهه أحد، والبائع مكره بفتح الراء بالبيع والتسليم؛ لأنه قبضه بحكم عقد فاسد فكان مضمونا عليه بالقيمة، وقيد بقوله "والمشتري غير مكره"؛ لأنه لو كان مكرها، وهلك المبيع في يده من غير تعد لا يضمن ويهلك أمانة ولو قال المصنف: "ضمن بدله" لكان أولى؛ لأنه يشمل المثلي والقيمي. Invalid Sales Under Coercion and Chain Liability
Ruling on chain of fasid sales and liability

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi ruling on a chain of invalid (fasid) sales under coercion to determine liability and ownership.

Core Idea
In a fasid sale, ownership passes with liability; in a chain, the first buyer bears the loss unless the coerced ratifies.

This lesson examines a specific scenario: a person is coerced into selling an item, and that item is subsequently resold multiple times through invalid (fasid) sales. The Hanafi school provides clear rules on liability and the validity of subsequent transactions, distinguishing between the original owner's right to claim compensation (tadmin) and his ratification (ijaza). The passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq illustrates this with the example of carrion-for-blood, but the principle applies to any coerced sale.

Guided Path
1
Identify the nature of the sale under coercion
The sale is fasid (invalid) because it lacks free consent, but it is not batil (void). In Hanafi fiqh, a fasid sale transfers ownership upon possession with liability for the price.
Distinguish fasid from batil: batil has no legal effects.
2
Trace the chain of fasid sales
The coerced seller (al-mukrah) sells to a first buyer, who then resells to a second buyer, and so on. Each sale is fasid due to the original defect (coercion).
3
Determine liability (tadmin)
The original owner may hold liable whichever buyer he wishes in the chain. However, the first buyer is ultimately responsible for the price, as his purchase was the first fasid sale.
4
Ascertain the effect on subsequent sales
The sales after the first are valid (jaza) and the first sale becomes void (batal) once liability is enforced. This means the later buyers keep the item, but the first buyer must compensate.
Contrast: if the coerced ratifies (ijaza) one sale, all sales become valid and he takes the price from the first buyer.
5
Contrast tadmin with ijaza (ratification)
If the coerced party ratifies any sale in the chain, all sales become fully valid, and the price flows from the first buyer. Under tadmin, only the first buyer is liable and later sales stand.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أكل ميتة على أكل دم = ضمنه قيمته نفذ ملكه فيه كسائر البياعات الفاسدة تكملة البحر الرائق: ١٤٤/٨] إقدامه على ذلك ولو كان المشتري باعه من آخر وباع الآخر من آخر حتى تداولته البياعات الفاسدة نفذ الكل بتضمين الأول، وله أن يضمن من شاء المشتريين، وجازت البياعات التي بعد وبطل ما قبله بخلاف ما إذا أجاز المكره أحد من هذه البياعات حيث يجوز الكل ما قبله وما بعده ويأخذ الثمن من المشتري الأول، ووجه الفرق بين الإجازة والتضمين مذكور في "الفتح" فليطالع. [رمز الحقائق: ٣١٠/٢]
Eating carrion in exchange for blood: he is liable for its value, his ownership is executed in it like all other invalid sales. Takmilat al-Bahr al-Ra'iq: 8/144. Undertaking that: If the buyer sold it to another, and that other sold to another, until the invalid sales circulate, all are executed with the first being liable. The original owner may hold liable whichever buyer he wishes. The subsequent sales are valid and what preceded is invalid, unlike if the coerced person permits one of these sales, in which case all sales before and after are valid, and he takes the price from the first buyer. The difference between permission and liability is mentioned in al-Fath; refer to it.
Source pages p. 228

Commentary
This is a ruling on a specific case: a person is coerced into selling an item (here it mentions eating carrion in exchange for blood, but the principle applies generally). The sale is fasid (invalid) because it involves a coerced party. If the coerced seller (al-mukrah) goes along, the sale is invalid but ownership transfers? The text says: 'Eating carrion in exchange for blood: he is liable for its value, his ownership is executed in it like all other invalid sales.' This is a rule: in a fasid sale, the buyer obtains ownership by taking possession (qabd) with liability to pay the price. If the first buyer then sells to another, and that one to another, each sale is also fasid. The original owner (the coerced) may claim compensation from any of the buyers in the chain. However, the subsequent sales (after the first) are valid (jaza) and the previous sales become void (batal). The difference between this and the case where the coerced gives permission (ijaza) is explained: in ijaza, all sales become valid and the coerced gets the price from the first buyer. This is an important Hanafi rule distinguishing between tacit acceptance (ijaza) and forced liability (tadmin).

Key Terms
بيعة فاسدة (bay' fasid): An invalid sale that is defective in some condition but not fundamentally void; ownership transfers with possession and liability for price.
تضمين (tadmin): Holding someone liable for compensation.
إجازة (ijaza): Permission or ratification of a contract by the coerced party.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In a chain of fasid sales under coercion, what happens to the first sale when the original owner enforces liability (tadmin) against the first buyer?
A) It becomes valid
B) It becomes void (batal)
C) It remains fasid
D) It becomes sahih

Key Takeaway
Under Hanafi law, a coerced sale is fasid, not batil; in a chain, the first buyer is liable, later sales are valid, and ratification makes all sales valid.

Owner's Right to Compensation from the Coercer (Mukrih)
Ruling on liability for property destroyed under duress.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on liability when property is destroyed under coercion and identify who bears the loss.

Core Idea
The coercer (mukrih) is liable, not the coerced (mukrah), because the coerced is a mere tool.

Imagine someone is forced at gunpoint to break a store window. Who should pay for the damage? In Hanafi fiqh, the owner of the property may claim compensation directly from the coercer (the one applying duress), not from the coerced person. This ruling is based on the principle that the coercer is the true cause of the destruction, while the coerced acts as an involuntary instrument.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Parties
Distinguish between the owner, the coerced (mukrah, with fatha), and the coercer (mukrih, with kasra). The coerced is the one who physically destroys the property under threat, and the coercer is the one who applies duress.
Arabic vowel markers are critical to avoid confusion.
2
Understand the Act of Destruction
The coerced does not patiently endure the threat and actually destroys the property. This act is the subject of the ruling.
If the coerced endures the threat, no destruction occurs and liability does not arise.
3
Apply the Hanafi Ruling
The owner may hold the coercer liable (tadmin) because the coercer is considered the direct destroyer (mutlif). The coerced is treated as a tool (ala) used by the coercer.
This is based on the principle that the doer under overwhelming duress is a mere instrument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وللمالك إلخ : أي إذا لم يصبر المكره على ما هدد به وأتلف المال فللمالك أن يضمن المكره بكسر الراء؛ لأنه هو المتلف لماله والمكره بالفتح آلة له فيما يصلح آلة. تكملة البحر الرائق: ١٤٧/٨]
And the owner [etc.]: meaning if the coerced does not patiently endure what he is threatened with and destroys the property, then the owner may hold liable the coercer (mukrih with kasr of ra), because he is the one who destroyed his property, and the coerced (mukrah with fatha) is a tool for him in what is suitable as a tool.
Source pages p. 229

Commentary
This is a brief but important ruling: when a person is coerced into destroying someone else's property (e.g., breaking a window), the owner of the property has the right to claim compensation (tadmin) directly from the coercer (al-mukrih), not from the coerced (al-mukrah). The reason given is that the coercer is the actual destroyer (mutlif) — he initiated the harm — while the coerced is merely a tool (ala) in his hands, similar to an inanimate object. This is based on the Hanafi principle that the doer under coercion is considered a mere instrument when the coercion is overwhelming. The mukrah (with fatha) is like a tool, and the liability falls on the one who wields the tool (the mukrih).

Key Terms
مكره (بالكسر) (mukrih): The one who coerces (the person applying duress).
مكره (بالفتح) (mukrah): The one coerced (the person under duress).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi ruling from the passage, who is considered the direct destroyer (mutlif) of property destroyed under coercion?
A) The coerced (mukrah)
B) The coercer (mukrih)
C) Both the coerced and the coercer
D) The owner of the property

Key Takeaway
The coercer (mukrih) is liable for property destroyed under his coercion; the coerced (mukrah) is treated as a tool.

Buyu — Sales and Trade
beginner
