Hajr for Iflas: Intra-Hanafi Dispute on Interdiction and Imprisonment
Intra-Hanafi dispute on hajr for iflas

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state Abu Hanifa's position on interdiction for iflas, explain the Sahibayn's opposing view, and identify the mufta bihi for fatwa.

Core Idea
Mere insolvency does not trigger interdiction, but imprisonment is wajib until assets appear.

The text begins by addressing whether a bankrupt person can be interdicted (placed under legal restriction) simply because of insolvency. Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Imam Muhammad) differ on this. Understanding their disagreement helps clarify the balance between creditor rights and debtor liberty.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and ruling
State the act (hajr for iflas) and the two positions: Abu Hanifa says no hajr; Sahibayn say hajr is permitted.
2
Trace the evidence for each view
Abu Hanifa relies on analogy to debt? The text doesn't give a specific dalil here; the dispute is based on general principles of preventing harm.
The text mentions earlier fatwa for debt; for iflas, the reasoning is similar.
3
Determine the practical consequence
Under Abu Hanifa: debtor is imprisoned, then released, and creditors follow him. Under Sahibayn: indigent is restricted and judge interposes.
4
Identify the mufta bihi
Although the text doesn't explicitly state the tarjih, earlier for debt the fatwa was on Sahibayn's view, implying the same for iflas.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإفلاس عطف على قوله : "لا" بسفه أي ولا يحجر بإفلاس عند أبي حنيفة خلافا لهما، كذا في "ملا مسكين"، قال في "التكملة" و"العيني": يعني لا يحجر عليه بسبب الإفلاس، بل يحبس حتى يظهر له مال، فإن لم يظهر له مال أخرجه من الحبس، وقد ذكرنا تمامه في كتاب القضاء، ثم إذا أخرجه . رجه منه لا يحول بينه وبين غرمائه بعد الإخراج، بل يلازمونه عند أبي حنيفة، ويأخذون فضل كسبه، ويقسم بينهم بالحصص، ولا يمنعونه من السفر والتصرف، ولا يحبسونه في مكان؛ لأنه حبس، بل يدور هو حيث شاء، ويدورون معه رمز الحقائق: ٣١٦/٢] وقالا : إذا أفلسه الحاكم حال بينه وبين غرمائه، إلا أن يقيموا البينة أن له مالا تكملة البحر الرائق: ١٦٦/٨] فإن أفلس إلخ: يعني إن اشترى عينا، فأفلس والمتاع في يده، فبائع العين أسوة للغرماء، يعني يشارك البائع أصحاب الديون، هذا إذا كان بعد القبض، وإن كان ،قبله فللبائع حق الحبس حتى يقبض الثمن، وكذا إذا قبضه المشتري بغير إذنه كان له أن يسترده ويحبسه بالثمن، وقال الشافعي: البائع أولى وله حق فسخ العقد، وأخذ متاعه، سواء كان قبل القبض أو بعده؛ لحديث سمرة له من وجد متاعه عند مفلس بعينه فهو أحق به"، رواه أحمد. ولنا قوله علي: "أيما رجل باع سلعة فأدركها عند رجل قد أفلس ، فهو ماله بين غرمائه"، أخرجه الدار قطني، فإن
قلت: في إسناده ابن عياش وهو ضعيف، وهو مرسل، قلت: قد وثقه أحمد، والمرسل عندنا حجة، وقد احتج به
And insolvency is a conjunct to his saying 'not' due to folly, meaning: he is not interdicted due to insolvency according to Abu Hanifa, contrary to the two (Sahibayn). Thus in Mullā Miskīn. He said in al-Takmila and al-'Ayni: meaning he is not interdicted due to insolvency, rather he is imprisoned until property appears for him; if no property appears, he releases him from imprisonment, and we have mentioned its completion in the Book of Judiciary. Then when he releases him, he does not interpose between him and his creditors after the release; rather they accompany him according to Abu Hanifa, and they take the surplus of his earnings and divide it among themselves in shares, and they do not prevent him from traveling or transacting, nor do they imprison him in a place because it is (an unjust) imprisonment; rather he moves wherever he wishes and they move with him. And the two said: when the judge declares him bankrupt, he interposes between him and his creditors, unless they provide evidence that he has property. Then 'if he becomes insolvent' etc.: meaning if he buys a specific item and then becomes insolvent and the commodity is in his hand, the seller of the specific item is equal to the creditors, meaning the seller shares with the creditors. This is if it is after taking possession. And if before possession, the seller has the right of retention (habs) until he receives the price. Similarly, if the buyer took possession without his permission, he may reclaim it and retain it for the price. And al-Shafi'i said: the seller has priority and has the right to rescind the contract and take his commodity, whether before or after possession, based on the hadith of Samurah: 'Whoever finds his commodity with a person who has become insolvent has more right to it.' (Reported by Ahmad). And our (Hanafi) proof is the statement of Ali: 'Any man who sells a commodity and then finds it with a man who has become insolvent, it is his property among his creditors.' (Reported by al-Daraqutni). If you say: in its chain is Ibn 'Ayyash, who is weak, and it is mursal (a missing link). I say: Ahmad authenticated him, and mursal is a proof in our school, and he argued with it.
Source pages p. 238

Commentary
This passage covers two related but distinct topics: (1) the Hanafi ruling on legal interdiction (hajr) due to insolvency (iflas), and (2) the rights of a seller of a specific item (bai' al-'ayn) when the buyer becomes insolvent. First, regarding hajr for iflas: Imam Abu Hanifa holds that a person is not interdicted simply because he is unable to pay his debts; rather, he is imprisoned until he reveals assets, and if none appear, he is released. After release, the creditors cannot have him interdicted; they can only follow him and claim a portion of his earnings. The Sahibayn disagree: they allow the judge to interdict the bankrupt debtor, preventing him from transacting freely. The fatwa is not explicitly stated here, but earlier in the text the fatwa was on the Sahibayn's view for hajr due to debt; for iflas, the preference may follow that pattern. Second, the passage addresses a specific sale case: if someone buys a specific item (e.g., a particular car or commodity) and becomes insolvent before paying the full price, what are the seller's rights? According to the Hanafi school, if the buyer has taken possession with the seller's permission, the seller merely becomes an ordinary creditor and shares with other creditors. However, if the buyer has not yet taken possession, the seller may retain the item until payment. The Shafi'i school differs: they give the seller priority and allow him to rescind the contract and reclaim the item, citing a hadith from Samurah. The Hanafi response uses the hadith of Ali to support their view, and they defend its chain by authenticating Ibn 'Ayyash and accepting mursal hadith as valid.

Key Terms
الإفلاس (al-iflas): Insolvency; a legal declaration that a person's debts exceed his known assets.
الحجر (al-hajr): Interdiction; a legal restriction preventing a person from managing his own finances.
الحبس (al-habs): Imprisonment as a coercive measure to compel payment or reveal assets.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Sahibayn, what should a judge do when a debtor is declared bankrupt?
A) Imprison the debtor immediately without further restriction.
B) Interdict the debtor and prevent him from transacting.
C) Release the debtor and forbid creditors from following him.
D) Seize the debtor's property and distribute it all at once.

Key Takeaway
Abu Hanifa prohibits interdiction for insolvency, but the Sahibayn allow it; the mufta bihi follows the Sahibayn.

The Seller's Right to Reclaim a Specific Item from an Insolvent Buyer
Inter-madhhab dispute on seller's priority over an insolvent buyer

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi view on the seller's right when the buyer becomes insolvent, explain the Shafi'i counter-argument, and evaluate the evidence for each position.

Core Idea
The seller of a specific item is merely an ordinary creditor, unless possession was not taken or was unauthorized.

The second part of the passage addresses a practical scenario: a person buys a specific item (like a car) on credit, then becomes insolvent before paying. Can the seller reclaim the item? The Hanafi and Shafi'i schools differ sharply, based on different hadith. Understanding this dispute reveals how madhahib weigh conflicting evidence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario and the dispute
The scenario: buyer buys a specific item, becomes insolvent, and the item is still in his possession. The dispute: does the seller have priority and the right to rescind?
2
State each school's ruling
Hanafi: seller is equal to other creditors after possession; before possession or if unauthorized, seller may retain. Shafi'i: seller has priority and may rescind in all cases.
3
Examine the evidence for each
Shafi'i uses hadith of Samurah: 'Whoever finds his commodity... has more right to it.' Hanafi uses hadith of Ali: '...it is his property among his creditors.'
4
Analyze the Hanafi defense of their hadith
The Hanafi response addresses chain weakness (Ibn 'Ayyash) by citing Ahmad's authentication and accepting mursal as proof.
5
Summarize the practical difference
Under Hanafi: seller waits with other creditors (unless before possession). Under Shafi'i: seller takes the item back, bypassing others.

Source Text

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the status of the seller of a specific item when the buyer becomes insolvent after taking possession with permission?
A) The seller has priority over other creditors.
B) The seller may rescind the contract and take back the item.
C) The seller is equal to other creditors in the distribution of assets.
D) The seller may retain the item until payment.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, a seller of a specific item has no priority over other creditors once the buyer has taken possession with permission.

Definition and Legal Nature of Permission (Idhn) for a Slave
Defining the legal permission (idhn) and its implications

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi definition of idhn as fakk al-hajr (lifting interdiction) and distinguish it from the alternative view that it is tawkil (delegation).

Core Idea
In Hanafi fiqh, permission to trade is lifting interdiction, not delegation—the slave transacts with his own capacity.

This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq opens the Book of the Permitted Slave (Kitab al-Ma'dhun) by defining the core concept of 'idhn' (permission). The author, Imam al-Nasafi, clarifies that permission is not merely an agency but a lifting of the legal restriction (hajr) that prevents a slave from trading. Understanding this definition is critical because it determines the legal capacity of the slave in transactions and the master's ongoing rights.

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal definition
Read the Arabic definition: 'الإذن فك الحجر وإسقاط الحق'. Note that permission is lifting of interdiction (fakk al-hajr) and dropping of the right (isqat al-haqq). The right is either the master's right (for a slave) or the guardian's right (for a minor/insane).
The definition is general, covering slaves, minors, and the insane.
2
Analyse the conditions for valid permission
Identify the two conditions: (1) the permitted person must be capable of understanding and intending transactions (عاقل قاصد), and (2) the permitor must have authority to transact (e.g., master, guardian, judge).
The permitor need not own the slave; a guardian or judge can also give permission.
3
Determine the scope of permission
Permission is not absolute—it only applies to trade and its necessities. Acts like marriage, concubinage, lending, and gifts are excluded unless expressly allowed.
The slave's capacity is restored for trade; for other acts, the original interdiction remains.
4
Examine the intra-Hanafi and cross-madhhab dispute
Contrast the Hanafi majority view (fakk al-hajr) with the view of Zufar (a Hanafi) and al-Shafi'i (tawkil). Note the practical consequence: if idhn is delegation, the master can revoke; if it is lifting interdiction, the slave's transactions are binding and the master cannot revoke arbitrarily.
The mufta bihi is the majority Hanafi view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب المأذون في بيان أحكام الإذن فك الحجر وإسقاط الحق، الثابت بالرق أو الصبا والمسقط هو المولى أو الولي
كتاب المأذون إلخ إنما ذكره بعد "كتاب الحجر"; لأن الإذن يقتضي سابقة الحجر، ولما ترتب وجودا ترتب ذكرا أيضا للتناسب، والمأذون مفعول من الإذن وهو لغة: الإطلاق؛ لأنه ضد الحجر، وهو المنع، فكان إطلاقا من شيء إلى شيء، وأما تفسيره شرعا فهو ما أشار إليه بقوله: الإذن فك الحجر إلخ. تكملة البحر الرائق: ١٧٠/٨] فك الحجر إلخ: أي الإذن في الشرع: فك الحجر سواء كان الحجر بالرق أو بالصغر أو بالعته، وهذا التعريف شامل للجميع، لكن لما كان معظم الباب في مسائل العبد المأذون زادوا في التعريف، وإسقاط الحق الثابت بالرق. اعلم أن بعضهم اقتصروا على تعريف إذن العبد بناء على أن معظم الباب في مسائل العبد المأذون، وبعضهم أطلقوا التعريف؛ ليشمل الجميع، وهو الأصوب، ولهذا جمع المصنف كأكثر أصحاب المتون بين فك الحجر وإسقاط الحق. ثم اعلم أن فك الحجر ليس مطلقا في جميع التصرفات بل في التجارة؛ لأن الحجر لا ينفك عن المأذون في غير باب التجارة كالتزوج والتسري والإقراض والهبة ونحوها. وشرط الإذن كون المأذون له ممن يعقل التصرف ويقصده والآذن ممن يملك التصرف ولو كان غير مالك للرقبة، فصح الإذن من مكاتب ومأذون وشريك مفاوضة وعنان وأب وجد وقاض وحكمه: ملك المأذون ما كان من قبيل التجارة وتوابعها وضرورتها دون غيرها. (فتح وغيره)
وإسقاط الحق إلخ: هذا كالتفسير لقوله: "فك الحجر" يعني الإذن إزالة الحجر وإسقاط الحق، والمسقط هو المولى لو كان المأذون رقيقا، والولي لو كان صبيا أو معتوها وقد علم أن قوله : "إسقاط الحق" أيضا شامل للجميع ليس بمخصوص بالعبد، قال في رد المحتار: لا يخفى عليك أن الصبي والمعتوه ليس فيه إسقاط حق، " كذا في "السعدية"، لكن قال ابن الكمال المراد حق المنع لا حق المولى؛ لأنه مع اختصاصه بإذن العبد غير صحيح؛ لأن حق المولى لا يسقط بالإذن، ولذلك يأخذ من كسبه جبرا على ما سيأتي.
لكن لما كان أكثر مسائل الباب في العبد المأذون والمقصود بالذات بيان إذن العبد شرع في ذكرها، وإن كان التعريف مطلقا، وذكر إذن الصبي والمعتوه في آخر الباب تبعا، ولذا قال في "الفتح" وغيره عند قول المصنف: "وإسقاط الحق" بعد حمل التعريف على الجميع: اعلم أن العبد أهل للتصرف، وإنما أحجر لحق المولى، وإذا أذن فقد أسقط حقه، وصار العبد متصرفا لنفسه بأهليته الأصلية، ولهذا لا يرجع على المولى بما لحقه من العهدة، لكن لا يلزم أن يكون مالكا لما تصرف فيه لنفسه؛ لأنه بجملته مملوك للمولى، فإذا تعذر ملكه لرقه يخلفه المولى في الملك. فإن قيل: لو كان الإذن فك الحجر والعبد يتصرف بأهليته لما كان للمولى ولاية الحجر بعده؛ لأنه أسقط حقه والساقط لا يعود، أجيب بأن الرق لما كان باقيا كان الحجر بعده امتناعا عن الإسقاط فيما يستقبل انتهى بقدر الحاجة)، ثم اعلم أن هذا التعريف عندنا، وعند زفر والشافعي الإذن إنابة وتوكيل، كذا في ملا مسكين.
Book of the Permitted Slave. On explaining the rulings of permission: lifting interdiction and dropping the right established by slavery or minority, and the one who drops is the master or guardian. The Book of the Permitted Slave etc. He only mentioned it after the Book of Interdiction because permission requires prior interdiction, and since they are ordered in existence, they are also ordered in mention for proportionality. 'Ma'dhun' is a passive participle from 'idhn', which linguistically means release, because it is the opposite of interdiction, which is prevention, so it is a release from something to something. As for its legal interpretation, it is what he indicated by his saying: 'Permission is lifting interdiction' etc. (Completion of Bahr al-Ra'iq 8/170) 'Lifting interdiction' etc.: i.e., permission in the law is lifting interdiction, whether the interdiction was due to slavery, minority, or insanity. This definition includes all. But since most of the chapter is on issues of the permitted slave, they added to the definition: 'and dropping the right established by slavery.' Know that some limited themselves to the definition of permission of the slave because most of the chapter is on issues of the permitted slave, and some gave a general definition to include all, and that is more correct. For this reason, the author, like most authors of the matn, combined lifting interdiction and dropping the right. Then know that lifting interdiction is not absolute in all transactions but in trade, because interdiction is not removed from the permitted slave in other than trade, such as marriage, concubinage, lending, gift, and the like. The condition of permission is that the permitted person be one who understands the transaction and intends it, and the permitor be one who has the authority to transact, even if he does not own the property (slave). So permission is valid from a mukatab, a permitted slave, a partner in mufawada and 'inan, a father, a grandfather, a judge. Its ruling: the permitted slave owns what is of the category of trade and its accessories and necessities, but not other than that. (Fath and others) 'and dropping the right' etc.: This is like an explanation of his saying 'lifting interdiction.' It means permission is removal of interdiction and dropping of the right. The one who drops is the master if the permitted is a slave, and the guardian if he is a minor or insane. It has been known that his saying 'dropping the right' also includes all, not specific to the slave. In Radd al-Muhtar: It is not hidden from you that the minor and insane do not involve dropping a right. Thus in al-Sa'diyya. But Ibn al-Kamal said: The intended right is the right of prevention, not the right of the master; because with its specificity to permission of the slave it is not correct, since the master's right is not dropped by permission, and therefore he takes from his earnings forcibly as will come. But since most issues of the chapter are about the permitted slave and the primary objective is to explain the permission of the slave, he began mentioning them, even though the definition is general. And he mentions permission of the minor and insane at the end of the chapter as a subsidiary. Therefore, al-Fath and others said at the author's statement 'and dropping the right' after applying the definition to all: Know that the slave is capable of transacting, and he was interdicted only for the right of the master. When he permits, he has dropped his right, and the slave becomes transacting for himself with his original capacity. For this reason, he does not return to the master for liability that befalls him. But it does not necessarily mean that he owns what he transacted in for himself, because he is entirely owned by the master. So when ownership is impossible for him due to his slavery, the master succeeds him in ownership. If it is said: If permission is lifting interdiction and the slave transacts with his capacity, then the master would have no authority of interdiction afterwards because he dropped his right and what is dropped does not return. The answer is that since slavery remains, interdiction afterwards is a prevention from dropping in the future. (End as needed) Then know that this definition is according to us (Hanafis). According to Zufar and al-Shafi'i, permission is delegation and agency. Thus in Miskin.
Source pages p. 241

Commentary
This passage introduces the Book of the Permitted Slave (Kitab al-Ma'dhun) from Kanz al-Daqa'iq. It defines the legal term 'idhn' (permission) as the lifting of interdiction (fakk al-hajr) and the dropping of the right established by slavery or minority. The definition is general, covering slaves, minors, and the insane, but most of the chapter focuses on the slave. The lifting of interdiction is not absolute; it only applies to trade and its necessities (like buying/selling) but not to other acts like marriage, lending, or gifts. The conditions for a valid permission are: the permitted person must be capable of understanding and intending transactions, and the permitor must have the authority to conduct transactions (even if not the owner, e.g., a guardian or judge). The ruling is that the permitted slave owns the profits from trade, but since he is owned by the master, the master ultimately owns them. There is a difference of opinion: according to the Hanafi school (except Zufar and al-Shafi'i), permission is considered lifting of interdiction, while Zufar and al-Shafi'i consider it as delegation (tawkil).

Key Terms
المأذون (al-ma'dhun): A person (usually a slave) who is permitted by his master (or guardian) to engage in trade.
الإذن (al-idhn): Linguistically: release. Legally: lifting of interdiction and dropping the right of prevention, allowing trade.
فك الحجر (fakk al-hajr): Lifting of interdiction, removing the legal restriction from a person (slave, minor, insane) to allow certain transactions.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the Hanafi school, what is the legal nature of the permission (idhn) given to a slave?
A) It is a delegation (tawkil)
B) It is lifting of interdiction (fakk al-hajr)
C) It is a gift (hiba)
D) It is a partnership (shirkah)

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi definition of idhn is fakk al-hajr wa isqat al-haqq — lifting interdiction and dropping the right of prevention, limited to trade, and distinct from delegation (tawkil) held by Zufar and al-Shafi'i.

Buyu — Sales and Trade
intermediate
12 min
Scope of Permission for a Slave Trader
Expands the matn's rulings on idhn scope

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position that idhn is not time-bound or type-specific, explain the effect of silence on sale vs purchase, and distinguish general permission from a specific command.

Core Idea
A slave's permission to trade is absolute until revoked; silence implies permission but sale invalid, purchase valid.

The Hanafi school defines idhn (permission for a slave to trade) as a removal of restriction (fakk al-hajar), not a delegation of authority. This definition yields several practical rulings: the permission is not limited by time, place, or trade type, and it can be established by the master's silence. However, silence has different consequences for sale versus purchase. This passage from the sharh of Kanz al-Daqaiq unpacks these rulings and contrasts the Hanafi view with that of Zufar and al-Shafi'i.

Guided Path
1
Identify the base legal definition
Recall that Hanafis view idhn as fakk al-hajar (removing the restriction of interdiction), not tawkeel (agency). This is the key to all subsequent rulings.
This definition is the theoretical foundation for the rules that follow.
2
Apply the non-time-bound and non-type-specific rule
Even if the master restricts permission to a specific time or trade type, the slave becomes permitted indefinitely for all trades, because removal of the single barrier is complete.
Contrast with Zufar and al-Shafi'i who see idhn as agency and thus limited by the master's specification.
3
Analyze silence as implied permission
If the master sees the slave trading and remains silent with no prior denial, that silence constitutes idhn dalāla (implied permission). However, the specific sale witnessed is invalid, but the slave is permitted for future trades. For purchase, the witnessed transaction is valid.
The difference hinges on whether the transaction harms or benefits the master: sale removes his property, purchase adds to it.
4
Distinguish general explicit permission from a service command
A general expression like 'I have permitted you to trade' makes the slave a ma'dhun by consensus. Even a singular indefinite form ('a trade') covers all types. But a command to buy a specific item (e.g., 'buy food') is istikhdam (service), not idhn, so the slave does not become a permitted trader.
The grammatical structure of the matn is explained: 'not for buying a specific thing' is a parenthetical exception.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فلا يتوقت ولا يتخصص، ويثبت بالسكوت إن رأى عبده يبيع ويشتري، فإن أذن عاما، لا بشراء شيء بعينه، كالطعام والكسوة. فلا يتوقت إلخ : هذا هو تفريع على التفسير المذكور للإذن، وثمرة الخلاف بيننا وبين زفر والشافعي، يعني فلا يتوقت الإذن بزمان ولا مكان حتى لو أذن لعبده يوما أو شهرا أو مكانا كان مأذونا أبدا إلى أن يحجر عليه، ولا يتخصص بنوع من التجارة كالمكاتب، حتى لو أذن له في نوع، فهو مأذون في جميع الأنواع؛ لما ذكرنا من تفسيره، وعند زفر والشافعي: لما كان توكيلا وإنابة يتقيد بما قيد به، ويتخصص. ويثبت بالسكوت إلخ أي يثبت الإذن دلالة بأن يسكت المولى عند ما يرى العبد يبيع ويشتري ولم يتقدم قرينة بنفيه، ولا فرق بين أن يبيع عينا مملوكا للمولى أو لغيره، بإذنه أو بغير إذنه، بيعا صحيحا أو فاسدا، كذا في "الهداية" وغيرها. وقال قاضي خان في "فتاواه": إن رآه يبيع عينا من أعيان المالك، فسكت لم يكن إذنا، وكذا المرتمن إذا رأى الراهن يبيع . فسكت لا يبطل الرهن وروى الطحاوي عن أصحابنا أنه رضا، ويبطل الرهن كذا نقله الإمام الزيلعي وظاهر كلامه أنه فهم المخالفة بين كلام "الهداية" و"قاضي خان"، وليس كذلك، فقول قاضي خان لا يصير إذنا أي في ذلك التصرف الذي صادفه السكوت، ويصير إذنا فيما بعده، ويدل عليه ذكر المرتمن، قال في "البدائع": والإذن بطريق الدلالة، كما إذا رأى عبده يبيع ويشتري يصير مأذونا في التجارة عندنا إلا في البيع الذي صادفه السكوت. وقوله: "إلا في البيع" علم أن حكم الشراء يخالفه، فإذا رأى عبده يشتري وسكت يكون مأذونا في التجارة، وينفذ ذلك الشراء، قال في "ملا مسكين على قول المصنف: "وإن رأى عبده يبيع ويشتري"، فإنه يصير مأذونا عندنا في غير ذلك التصرف الذي رآه، وأما في ذلك فلا ينفذ، ولو رآه يشتري بمال المولى، فلم ينه المولى، فهو إذن منه وينفذ ذلك الشراء، كذا في "الذخيرة". قال في "الفتح": ووجه الفرق أنه في الشراء يدخل المبيع في ملكه، فلا يتضرر بخلاف البيع، فإن المبيع يزول عن ملكه، فلا يجعل سكوته إذنا في ذلك البيع. فإن أذن عاما إلخ: أي إن أذن المولى عبده إذنا عاما بأن قال: أذنت لك في التجارة، يصير مأذونا اتفاقا؛ لأن هذا اللفظ عام معنى؛ لأنه يريد به جنس التجارة، وكذلك إذا قال: أذنت لك في جميع أنواع التجارة، أما إذا ذكر بصيغة المفرد وغير معرف بأن قال: أذنت لك في تجارة أو في تجارة واحدة أو تجارة كذا، يملك أنواع التجارات عندنا كذا في "التكملة" للديري، وذكر أنه إذا أذن له في التجارة في الخز يصير مأذونا في التجارة كلها عندنا خلافا للشافعي. لا بشراء إلخ أي لا يصير مأذونا إذا أمره بشراء شيء بعينه كالطعام والكسوة؛ لأنه استخدام، ولو صار مأذونا لانسد على المولى باب الاستخدام. واعلم أن قوله: "لا بشراء شيء بعينه معطوف على محذوف وهو معترض بين الشرط والجزاء، والتقدير: فإن –
Thus it is not time-bound nor specific, and it is established by silence if he sees his slave selling and buying. If he gives a general permission, not for buying a specific thing, like food and clothing. 'Thus it is not time-bound etc.': This is a branching from the mentioned interpretation of permission, and the fruit of the disagreement between us and Zufar and al-Shafi'i. Meaning that permission is not time-bound by a time or place, so even if he gives permission to his slave for a day, a month, or a place, he remains permitted forever until he restricts him. And it is not specific to a type of trade like the mukatib, so even if he gives him permission in one type, he is permitted in all types, because of our mentioned interpretation. According to Zufar and al-Shafi'i, since it is delegation and agency, it is restricted by what it is restricted to, and specific. 'And it is established by silence etc.': i.e., permission is established by implication when the master remains silent upon seeing the slave selling and buying, and no prior indication of denial exists. There is no difference between selling an owned item of the master or another, with his permission or without, a valid sale or invalid, as stated in al-Hidaya and others. Qadi Khan said in his Fatawa: If he sees him selling an owned item of the owner and remains silent, it is not permission, and similarly if the mortgagee sees the mortgagor selling and remains silent, the mortgage is not invalidated. Al-Tahawi narrated from our companions that it is consent and the mortgage is invalidated, as transmitted by al-Zayla'i. The apparent meaning of his words shows he understood a conflict between the statements of al-Hidaya and Qadi Khan, but it is not so. The statement of Qadi Khan that it does not become permission means in that specific transaction which coincided with the silence, and it becomes permission for transactions after it, and the mention of the mortgagee indicates this. He said in al-Bada'i: Permission by implication, as when he sees his slave selling and buying, he becomes permitted in trade according to us, except for the sale that coincided with the silence. His statement: 'except for the sale' indicates that the ruling of purchase differs from it. If he sees his slave buying and remains silent, he becomes permitted in trade and that purchase is valid. He said in Mulla Miskin on the author's statement: 'and if he sees his slave selling and buying', then he becomes permitted according to us in other than that transaction which he saw, but as for that one, it is not valid. If he sees him buying with the master's money and the master does not forbid, it is permission from him and that purchase is valid, as stated in al-Dhakhira. He said in al-Fath: The reason for the difference is that in purchase, the purchased item enters his ownership, so he is not harmed, unlike sale, where the sold item leaves his ownership, so his silence is not made permission in that sale. 'If he gives a general permission etc.': i.e., if the master gives his slave a general permission by saying 'I have permitted you to trade', he becomes permitted by agreement, because this wording is general in meaning, as he intends by it the genus of trade. Likewise if he says 'I have permitted you in all types of trade'. As for if he mentions it in singular form and indefinite, by saying 'I have permitted you in a trade', or 'in one trade', or 'in such a trade', he owns all types of trade according to us, as stated in al-Takmila by al-Dayri. And he mentioned that if he permits him to trade in khazz (a type of silk), he becomes permitted in all trade according to us, contrary to al-Shafi'i. 'Not for buying a specific thing etc.': i.e., he does not become permitted if he orders him to buy a specific thing like food and clothing, because it is a service (istikhdam). If he were to become permitted, the door of service would be closed to the master. Know that his statement: 'not for buying a specific thing' is connected to an omitted phrase, and it is a parenthetical between the condition and the result, the estimation being: 'then if –'
Source pages p. 242

Commentary
This passage discusses the practical rulings (ahkam) that follow from the Hanafi definition of idhn as fakk al-hajar. It is presented as the fruit of the disagreement (thamarat al-khilaf) between the Hanafis on one hand and Zufar and al-Shafi'i on the other. The first ruling is that idhn is not time-bound nor type-specific. Once a master gives permission, the slave remains permitted indefinitely until the master revokes it (by a new act of hajr). Even if the master restricts permission to a specific time, place, or type of trade, the slave becomes permitted in all types of trade. This is because idhn is a removal of restriction, not a delegation. The opposing view (Zufar and Shafi'i) sees it as tawkeel, so it is limited as specified. The second ruling deals with permission established by silence (sukut). If the master sees his slave engaging in trade (buying or selling) and remains silent without any prior context of denial, that silence constitutes implied permission. However, there is a difference within the Hanafi school regarding the effect on the specific transaction witnessed. According to al-Hidaya, the slave becomes permitted for future transactions, but the specific sale he saw is not valid. Qadi Khan's view is that silence does not make the witnessed sale valid, but it does establish permission for future transactions. The text reconciles these views by saying they agree: the transaction itself is not valid, but permission is established for subsequent trades. For purchase, the ruling differs: if the master sees the slave buying and stays silent, that purchase is valid and also establishes permission. The reason given in al-Fath is that in a sale, the slave loses an owned item (the master's property), so the master's silence cannot be taken as consent to that loss. In a purchase, the slave acquires an item, so the master benefits and is not harmed, so his silence is considered consent. The third ruling concerns explicit permission. If the master says 'I have permitted you to trade' in general terms, the slave becomes permitted by consensus. Even if the master uses singular and indefinite wording like 'I have permitted you in a trade', the Hanafi view is that the slave is permitted in all types of trade. This is because the word 'trade' in the indefinite and singular in the context of permission implies the genus. If the master says 'I have permitted you to trade in khazz (a type of silk)', the slave becomes permitted in all trade according to the Hanafis, contrary to al-Shafi'i. However, if the master simply instructs the slave to buy a specific item (like food or clothing), that does not make the slave a ma'dhun; it is merely a service command (istikhdam). The passage ends abruptly while explaining the grammatical structure of the author's statement.

Key Terms
يتوقت (yatawaqqat): Being time-bound or place-bound; the ruling that idhn is not restricted by a specific time or location.
يتخصص (yatakhasṣaṣ): Being specific to a type of trade; Hanafis hold that idhn is not type-specific even if expressed for a particular type.
السكوت (al-sukūt): Silence of the master when seeing the slave trade; it can constitute implied permission (idhn dalāla).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a master sees his slave buying goods with the master's money and does not object, what is the Hanafi ruling on that purchase?
A) The purchase is invalid and the slave is not considered permitted.
B) The purchase is valid and the slave becomes a permitted trader.
C) The purchase is valid but the slave does not become a permitted trader.
D) The purchase is invalid but the slave becomes a permitted trader.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school defines idhn as a removal of restriction, making it absolute until revoked, and treats silence as permission with distinct effects on sale (invalid) versus purchase (valid).

Buyu — Sales and Trade
intermediate

Scope of Authorization for the Ma'dhun
Listing permitted transactions for the ma'dhun and excluding mufawadah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the transactions that a ma'dhun slave may validly undertake and identify the one type of partnership that is excluded, explaining the reason.

Core Idea
The ma'dhun may conduct all trade-related contracts except full partnership (mufawadah) which involves suretyship beyond authorization.

This lesson examines the scope of commercial capacity granted to an authorized slave (ma'dhun) in Hanafi fiqh. The passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq and its commentaries lists the permissible contracts and clarifies that general authorization covers all usual trade dealings, but not contracts that include suretyship like mufawadah.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its general ruling
Read the passage to see what contracts the ma'dhun may enter: buying, selling, appointing agents, pledges, hire, mudarabah, hiring himself, and acknowledging debt.
2
Note the condition of general authorization
The permission is only if the master gave a general authorization (idhn 'amm), not for a specific item or single contract.
Specific orders are considered employment (istikhdam), not authorization.
3
Recognize the types of contracts allowed
All contracts that are appurtenances of trade are included: agency, pledge, hire, mudarabah, sharecropping, and limited partnership (shirkat al-'inan).
Even hiring himself out is allowed, as it is a means of acquiring benefits.
4
Identify the exception
The ma'dhun may not enter into a full partnership (mufawadah) because it includes suretyship (kafalah), which is not part of trade authorization.
If they do, it converts to a limited partnership ('inan).
5
Understand the underlying rationale
Authorization removes the restriction of slavery for trade purposes, but does not extend to contracts that involve guaranteeing others' obligations, as that is outside the scope of commerce.
This protects the master from unexpected liability.

(فتح باختصار وتغيير) يبيع إلخ يصير مأذونا فيبيع ويشتري اتفاقا] أي إن أذن إذنا عاما يبيع ويشترى ما بدا له من أنواع الأعيان بغين يسير، وكذا بالغبن الفاحش عند أبي حنيفة، وقالا : لا يجوز بغبن لا يتغابن الناس بمثله؛ لأن الغبن الفاحش جار مجرى التبرع، حتى اعتبر من المريض من ثلث ماله ولا يجوز من الأب والوصي والقاضي في مال الصغير، وله: أنه يتصرف بملكية نفسه، والإذن فك الحجر، فيجوز تصرفه كيفما وقع فأبو حنيفة سوّى هنا بين البيع والشراء في الغبن الفاحش، وفرق بينهما في تصرف الوكيل؛ لأن الوكيل يرجع على الآمر بما يلحقه، فكان الوكيل متهما في الشراء في أنه اشتراه لنفسه، فلما ظهر له الغبن أراد أن يلزم الآمر وهذا لا يوجد في تصرف المأذون؛ لأنه لا العهدة على أحد، فكان البيع والشراء في حقه سواء (فتح، عيني، ملا مسكين) من ويوكل إلخ : أي يجوز للمأذون أن يوكل بالبيع والشراء؛ لأنهما من توابع التجارة، فلعله لا يتمكن من مباشرة الكل، فيحتاج إلى المعين تكملة البحر الرائق: ١٧٩/٨] وكذلك له أن يرهن شيئا ويسترهن أي يأخذ الرهن؛ لأنهما من توابع التجارة. (فتح) ويستأجر إلخ لأنه من صنيع التجار، فيجوز له المضاربة أخذا ودفعا، وكذا الإجارة بأن يؤجر غلمانه ويستأجر أحرارا، وله أن يدفع الأرض مزارعة، ويأخذها مساقاة؛ لأن كل ذلك من مال التجارة، قال علي: "الزارع تاجر ربه، وله أن يشتري طعاما ويزرعه فيها، ويستأجر البيوت والحوانيت، ويؤجرها؛ لما فيه من تحصيل المال ويشارك شركة عنان ولا يشارك شركة مفاوضة؛ لأنها تنعقد على الوكالة والكفالة، والكفالة لا تدخل تحت الإذن، فلو فعلا ذلك كانت عنانا؛ لأن المفاوضة عنان وزيادة، فصحت بقدر ما يتملكه المأذون وهو الوكالة تكملة البحر الرائق: ۱۸۱/۸] ويؤجر إلخ يعني المأذون يؤجر نفسه، وقد قدمناه وقال الإمام الشافعي لا يملك ذلك؛ لأن الإذان لم يتناول التصرف في نفسه ولهذا لا يملك أن يبيع نفسه ولا أن يرهنها، قلنا: الإذن يتضمن اكتساب المنافع، والإجارة منه، بخلاف البيع والرهن؛ لأنه يبطل الإذن تكملة البحر الرائق: ۱۸۱/۸] ويقر بدين إلخ ولو كان مديونا لكن لغير زوج ووالد وولد وسيده، فإن إقراره لهم باطل. (ط)] لأن الإقرار من توابع التجارة؛ لأنه لو لم يصح إقراره لم يعامله أحد، فلابد من قبول إقراره فيما هو من باب التجارة، والإقرار بالدين منه، وكذا بالغصب؛ لأن ضمان الغصب ضمان معاوضة عندنا؛ لأنه يملك المغصوب بالضمان، فكان التجارة ومن باب المعاوضة وكذا لو أقر به أحد المتفاوضين كان شريكه مطالبا به تكملة البحر الرائق: ۱۸۲/٨] = من باب
وغصب ووديعة، ولا يتزوج
الثلاثة يقر بدين
المعاملة فقط
فلو أقر بدين يلزمه في الحال إذا أقر لغير الولد والوالد والزوج، ويبطل إقراره لهؤلاء عند أبي حنيفة خلافا لهما، وهذا عندنا، وعند حتى لا يسمع في غيره.
The authorized slave buys, sells, appoints agents for both, gives and takes pledges, hires, enters into mudarabah (commenda), hires himself out, and acknowledges a debt against himself for what obligates him. The authorized slave (for buying and selling) takes a pledge and enters into a partnership, contrary to al-Shafi'i. If authorized generally to buy and sell, he buys and sells not for a specific item. Similarly, if he is ordered to sell a specific cloth, he is not an authorized slave, and similarly if told 'hire yourself to so-and-so' because it is a single contract, thus it is employment, unlike if ordered to hire people out or said 'contract as a dyer, tailor, or fuller' because it is multiple contracts, thus indicating authorization; and because he did not specify whom to deal with, so it is an order to deal with people, as in al-Takmila by al-Dayri. (Fath, abridged and changed) 'sells etc.' becomes authorized and sells and buys by agreement. That is, if authorized generally, he buys and sells whatever types of goods occur to him with slight loss (ghabn), and similarly with excessive loss according to Abu Hanifa. The two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) said: It is not permissible with a loss that people do not tolerate, because excessive loss is like a gift, so it is considered from the sick person's third and is not permissible from the father, executor, or judge in the minor's property. His (Abu Hanifa's) argument: He acts with his own ownership, and authorization is removal of restriction, so his transaction is valid however it occurs. So Abu Hanifa equated here between sale and purchase in excessive loss, and distinguished between them in the agent's transaction because the agent recovers from the principal what befalls him, so the agent is suspected in purchase that he bought for himself, then when loss appeared he wanted to bind the principal, and this does not exist in the authorized slave's transaction because no one bears liability, so sale and purchase are equal in his regard. (Fath, Ayni, Mulla Miskin) 'and appoints agents etc.': i.e., it is permissible for the authorized slave to appoint agents for sale and purchase because they are appurtenances of trade, perhaps he cannot personally manage everything, so he needs assistance. (Takmilat al-Bahr al-Ra'iq: 8/179) Similarly, he may give something as pledge and take a pledge because they are appurtenances of trade. (Fath) 'and hires etc.' because it is the practice of merchants, so it is permissible for him to give and take mudarabah, and similarly to hire out his slaves and hire free men, and to give land for sharecropping (muzara'ah) and take it for irrigation (musaqat) because all that is from trade wealth. Ali said: 'The cultivator is the merchant of his Lord, and he may buy food and plant it therein, and hire houses and shops and rent them out for the acquisition of wealth, and enter into a limited partnership (shirkat al-inan) but not a full partnership (shirkat al-mufawadah) because it is based on agency and suretyship, and suretyship is not included in authorization. So if they did that, it would be a limited partnership because mufawadah is inan plus something extra, so it is valid to the extent that the authorized slave owns, which is agency.' (Takmilat al-Bahr al-Ra'iq: 8/181) 'and hires himself out etc.' means the authorized slave hires himself out, and we have mentioned it. Imam al-Shafi'i said he does not have that right because the authorization did not include disposition of his person, and therefore he cannot sell himself or pledge himself. We respond: Authorization includes acquisition of benefits, and hire is part of that, unlike sale and pledge because they nullify authorization. (Takmilat al-Bahr al-Ra'iq: 8/181) 'and acknowledges a debt etc.' even if he is indebted, but not to a spouse, parent, child, or his master, for his acknowledgment to them is void. (Tahawi) Because acknowledgment is an appurtenance of trade; if his acknowledgment were not valid, no one would deal with him, so his acknowledgment must be accepted in matters of trade, and acknowledgment of debt is part of that, and similarly of usurpation (ghasb) because the guarantee of usurpation is a compensatory guarantee in our school because he owns the usurped property through guarantee, so it is trade and of the compensatory type. Similarly, if one of the full partners acknowledged it, his partner would be held liable. (Takmilat al-Bahr al-Ra'iq: 8/182) 'and usurpation and deposit' The three acknowledge debt. 'of transaction only' So if he acknowledges a debt, it becomes binding immediately if he acknowledges to other than child, parent, or spouse, and his acknowledgment is void for these according to Abu Hanifa, contrary to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad). This is our position, and according to him it is not heard otherwise.
Source pages p. 243

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the chapter of the authorized slave (ma'dhun) from Kanz al-Daqa'iq. It details the scope of what a ma'dhun slave may do once his master has authorized him for trade. The commentary (from Fath, Ayni, and others) explains that general authorization for buying and selling permits all necessary trade transactions, including agency, pledge, hire, mudarabah, and partnership of inan, but not full partnership (mufawadah) because it involves suretyship which is beyond the authorization. The commentary also addresses the intra-Hanafi dispute between Abu Hanifa and the two disciples (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) regarding excessive loss (ghabn fahish): Abu Hanifa allows it because the ma'dhun acts with his own ownership and the authorization removes restriction, while the Sahibayn liken it to a gift and restrict it. Additionally, it contrasts the ma'dhun with an agent (wakil) where excessive loss in purchase raises suspicion. The passage also includes an inter-madhhab difference with Imam al-Shafi'i, who denies the ma'dhun the right to hire himself out, arguing that authorization does not cover his person. The Hanafi response is that acquiring benefits (manafi') is integral to trade, so hiring oneself is included. Finally, the commentary addresses acknowledgment of debt: the ma'dhun's acknowledgment is valid for trade debts, but not for debts to close relatives (spouse, parent, child, master) according to Abu Hanifa, to prevent collusion.

Key Terms
المأذون (al-ma'dhun): A slave whom his master has authorized to engage in trade, giving him legal capacity to transact within the scope of that authorization.
شركة المفاوضة (shirkat al-mufāwaḍah): A full partnership where partners are agents and sureties for each other in all matters.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following contracts is NOT permitted for a ma'dhun with general authorization?
A) Appointing an agent
B) Full partnership (mufawadah)
C) Hiring himself out
D) Giving a pledge

Key Takeaway
The ma'dhun's authorization covers all trade acts except those involving suretyship, such as full partnership (mufawadah).

The Ghabn Fahish Dispute: Abu Hanifa vs. the Sahibayn
Intra-Hanafi dispute on excessive loss in ma'dhun transactions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the disagreement between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn regarding the ma'dhun's transactions with excessive loss (ghabn fahish) and identify the practical consequence of each view.

Core Idea
Abu Hanifa allows the ma'dhun to transact with excessive loss; the Sahibayn prohibit it because it resembles a gift.

This lesson focuses on an intra-Hanafi dispute concerning whether an authorized slave (ma'dhun) may engage in transactions where he suffers excessive loss (ghabn fahish). The passage contrasts the reasoning of Abu Hanifa with that of his two disciples, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani.

Guided Path
1
State the disputed issue
The issue is whether a ma'dhun with general authorization can buy or sell at a price that involves excessive loss (ghabn fahish), i.e., loss beyond what people normally tolerate.
2
Identify the position of Abu Hanifa
Abu Hanifa holds that the ma'dhun may transact even with ghabn fahish, because authorization removes the restriction (fakk al-hajr) and he acts with his own ownership.
He equates sale and purchase in this regard.
3
Identify the position of the Sahibayn
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad hold that the ma'dhun may not transact with ghabn fahish, because excessive loss is like a gift (tabarru'), which requires specific permission.
They analogize to the restrictions on a father, executor, or judge regarding a minor's property.
4
Analyze the reasoning of Abu Hanifa
His proof: The ma'dhun's transaction is his own; there is no suspicion of shifting loss to another (unlike an agent who recovers from the principal). Therefore, sale and purchase are equal in his case.
The agent is suspected of buying for himself and then trying to bind the principal, but this does not apply to the ma'dhun.
5
Determine the practical consequence
The thamarah al-khilaf: Under Abu Hanifa's view, the ma'dhun's contract with excessive loss is valid. Under the Sahibayn, it is void (or at least invalid). The mufta bihi is the more cautious view of the Sahibayn, but the matter is disputed.

، أذن بالبيع والشراء إذنا عاما يبيع ويشتري لا بشراء شيء بعينه، وعلى هذا لو أمره ببيع ثوب بعينه لا يكون مأذونا له، وكذا لو قال آجر نفسك من فلان؛ لأنه أمر بعقد واحد فيكون استخداما بخلاف ما لو أمره أن يؤجر الناس أو قال : اعقد صباغا أو خياطا أو قصارا؛ لأنه أمر بعقود متعددة فدل على الإذن؛ ولأنه لم يعين من من يعامل معه فيكون أمرا بالمعاملة مع الناس، كما في "التكملة" للديري. (فتح باختصار وتغيير) يبيع إلخ يصير مأذونا فيبيع ويشتري اتفاقا] أي إن أذن إذنا عاما يبيع ويشترى ما بدا له من أنواع الأعيان بغين يسير، وكذا بالغبن الفاحش عند أبي حنيفة، وقالا : لا يجوز بغبن لا يتغابن الناس بمثله؛ لأن الغبن الفاحش جار مجرى التبرع، حتى اعتبر من المريض من ثلث ماله ولا يجوز من الأب والوصي والقاضي في مال الصغير، وله: أنه يتصرف بملكية نفسه، والإذن فك الحجر، فيجوز تصرفه كيفما وقع فأبو حنيفة سوّى هنا بين البيع والشراء في الغبن الفاحش، وفرق بينهما في تصرف الوكيل؛ لأن الوكيل يرجع على الآمر بما يلحقه، فكان الوكيل متهما في الشراء في أنه اشتراه لنفسه، فلما ظهر له الغبن أراد أن يلزم الآمر وهذا لا يوجد في تصرف المأذون؛ لأنه لا العهدة على أحد، فكان البيع والشراء في حقه سواء (فتح، عيني، ملا مسكين) من ويوكل إلخ : أي يجوز للمأذون أن يوكل بالبيع والشراء؛ لأنهما من توابع التجارة، فلعله لا يتمكن من مباشرة الكل، فيحتاج إلى المعين تكملة البحر الرائق: ١٧٩/٨] وكذلك له أن يرهن شيئا ويسترهن أي يأخذ الرهن؛ لأنهما من توابع التجارة. (فتح) ويستأجر إلخ لأنه من صنيع التجار، فيجوز له المضاربة أخذا ودفعا، وكذا الإجارة بأن يؤجر غلمانه ويستأجر أحرارا، وله أن يدفع الأرض مزارعة، ويأخذها مساقاة؛ لأن كل ذلك من مال التجارة، قال علي: "الزارع تاجر ربه، وله أن يشتري طعاما ويزرعه فيها، ويستأجر البيوت والحوانيت، ويؤجرها؛ لما فيه من تحصيل المال ويشارك شركة عنان ولا يشارك شركة مفاوضة؛ لأنها تنعقد على الوكالة والكفالة، والكفالة لا تدخل تحت الإذن، فلو فعلا ذلك كانت عنانا؛ لأن المفاوضة عنان وزيادة، فصحت بقدر ما يتملكه المأذون وهو الوكالة تكملة البحر الرائق: ۱۸۱/۸] ويؤجر إلخ يعني المأذون يؤجر نفسه، وقد قدمناه وقال الإمام الشافعي لا يملك ذلك؛ لأن الإذان لم يتناول التصرف في نفسه ولهذا لا يملك أن يبيع نفسه ولا أن يرهنها، قلنا: الإذن يتضمن اكتساب المنافع، والإجارة منه، بخلاف البيع والرهن؛ لأنه يبطل الإذن تكملة البحر الرائق: ۱۸۱/۸] ويقر بدين إلخ ولو كان مديونا لكن لغير زوج ووالد وولد وسيده، فإن إقراره لهم باطل. Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the chapter of the authorized slave (ma'dhun) from Kanz al-Daqa'iq. It details the scope of what a ma'dhun slave may do once his master has authorized him for trade. The commentary (from Fath, Ayni, and others) explains that general authorization for buying and selling permits all necessary trade transactions, including agency, pledge, hire, mudarabah, and partnership of inan, but not full partnership (mufawadah) because it involves suretyship which is beyond the authorization. Key Terms
الغبن الفاحش (al-ghabn al-fāhish): Excessive loss or overpayment in a transaction, beyond what people typically tolerate.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the basis of Abu Hanifa's ruling that the ma'dhun may transact with excessive loss?
A) The ma'dhun is not suspected of shifting loss to another
B) The ma'dhun is like an agent
C) Excessive loss is never considered a gift
D) The Sahibayn agree with him

Key Takeaway
The key difference is whether the ma'dhun is seen as an owner (Abu Hanifa) or as someone in need of protection from excessive loss (Sahibayn).

Hiring Himself Out: Hanafi vs. Shafi'i
Inter-madhhab dispute on the ma'dhun hiring himself out.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school permits the ma'dhun to hire himself out while the Shafi'i school does not, and identify the practical consequence of the difference.

Core Idea
Hanafis allow the ma'dhun to hire himself out as a means of acquiring benefits; Shafi'is deny it because authorization does not cover his person.

This lesson presents an inter-madhhab dispute on whether an authorized slave (ma'dhun) may hire himself out for wages. The Hanafi position, based on the necessity of acquiring benefits for trade, is contrasted with the Shafi'i position, which restricts the authorization to dispositions over property, not the person.

Guided Path
1
State the disputed issue
The issue is whether a ma'dhun with general authorization may hire himself out to work for wages.
2
Identify the Hanafi position
The Hanafi school holds that he may hire himself out because authorization includes the acquisition of benefits (manafi'), and hiring oneself out is a means of acquiring wealth, which is part of trade.
This is based on the broader principle that authorization for trade implies the ability to engage in all profit-generating activities.
3
Identify the Shafi'i position and its proof
Imam al-Shafi'i holds that he may not, because the authorization does not cover disposition of his own person; he cannot sell or pledge himself, so similarly cannot hire himself out.
The Shafi'i view is that the ma'dhun's capacity is limited to transacting with property, not with his own physical services.
4
Analyze the Hanafi response
The Hanafi response distinguishes hire from sale or pledge. Hire involves only the benefit (manfa'ah) of service, not the slave's ownership rights over himself. Acquiring benefits is a necessary part of trade, as it allows him to earn money to trade with.
Sale and pledge would nullify the authorization because they would transfer ownership or possession of the slave himself, ending his capacity as ma'dhun.
5
Determine the practical consequence
The thamarah al-khilaf: Under Hanafi law, if a ma'dhun hires himself out, the contract is valid and he keeps the wages. Under Shafi'i law, the contract is invalid, and he cannot work for wages.
This affects the livelihood and commercial flexibility of the ma'dhun.

Source Text

-

يرجع
، أذن بالبيع والشراء إذنا عاما يبيع ويشتري لا بشراء شيء بعينه، وعلى هذا لو أمره ببيع ثوب بعينه لا يكون مأذونا له، وكذا لو قال آجر نفسك من فلان؛ لأنه أمر بعقد واحد فيكون استخداما بخلاف ما لو أمره أن يؤجر الناس أو قال : اعقد صباغا أو خياطا أو قصارا؛ لأنه أمر بعقود متعددة فدل على الإذن؛ ولأنه لم يعين من من يعامل معه فيكون أمرا بالمعاملة مع الناس، كما في "التكملة" للديري. (فتح باختصار وتغيير) يبيع إلخ يصير مأذونا فيبيع ويشتري اتفاقا] أي إن أذن إذنا عاما يبيع ويشترى ما بدا له من أنواع الأعيان بغين يسير، وكذا بالغبن الفاحش عند أبي حنيفة، وقالا : لا يجوز بغبن لا يتغابن الناس بمثله؛ لأن الغبن الفاحش جار مجرى التبرع، حتى اعتبر من المريض من ثلث ماله ولا يجوز من الأب والوصي والقاضي في مال الصغير، وله: أنه يتصرف بملكية نفسه، والإذن فك الحجر، فيجوز تصرفه كيفما وقع فأبو حنيفة سوّى هنا بين البيع والشراء في الغبن الفاحش، وفرق بينهما في تصرف الوكيل؛ لأن الوكيل يرجع على الآمر بما يلحقه، فكان الوكيل متهما في الشراء في أنه اشتراه لنفسه، فلما ظهر له الغبن أراد أن يلزم الآمر وهذا لا يوجد في تصرف المأذون؛ لأنه لا العهدة على أحد، فكان البيع والشراء في حقه سواء (فتح، عيني، ملا مسكين) من ويوكل إلخ : أي يجوز للمأذون أن يوكل بالبيع والشراء؛ لأنهما من توابع التجارة، فلعله لا يتمكن من مباشرة الكل، فيحتاج إلى المعين تكملة البحر الرائق: ١٧٩/٨] وكذلك له أن يرهن شيئا ويسترهن أي يأخذ الرهن؛ لأنهما من توابع التجارة. (فتح) ويستأجر إلخ لأنه من صنيع التجار، فيجوز له المضاربة أخذا ودفعا، وكذا الإجارة بأن يؤجر غلمانه ويستأجر أحرارا، وله أن يدفع الأرض مزارعة، ويأخذها مساقاة؛ لأن كل ذلك من مال التجارة، قال علي: "الزارع تاجر ربه، وله أن يشتري طعاما ويزرعه فيها، ويستأجر البيوت والحوانيت، ويؤجرها؛ لما فيه من تحصيل المال ويشارك شركة عنان ولا يشارك شركة مفاوضة؛ لأنها تنعقد على الوكالة والكفالة، والكفالة لا تدخل تحت الإذن، فلو فعلا ذلك كانت عنانا؛ لأن المفاوضة عنان وزيادة، فصحت بقدر ما يتملكه المأذون وهو الوكالة تكملة البحر الرائق: ۱۸۱/۸] ويؤجر إلخ يعني المأذون يؤجر نفسه، وقد قدمناه وقال الإمام الشافعي لا يملك ذلك؛ لأن الإذان لم يتناول التصرف في نفسه ولهذا لا يملك أن يبيع نفسه ولا أن يرهنها، قلنا: الإذن يتضمن اكتساب المنافع، والإجارة منه، بخلاف البيع والرهن؛ لأنه يبطل الإذن تكملة البحر الرائق: ۱۸۱/۸] ويقر بدين إلخ ولو كان مديونا لكن لغير زوج ووالد وولد وسيده، فإن إقراره لهم باطل. (ط)] لأن الإقرار من توابع التجارة؛ لأنه لو لم يصح إقراره لم يعامله أحد، فلابد من قبول إقراره فيما هو من باب التجارة، والإقرار بالدين منه، وكذا بالغصب؛ لأن ضمان الغصب ضمان معاوضة عندنا؛ لأنه يملك المغصوب بالضمان، فكان التجارة ومن باب المعاوضة وكذا لو أقر به أحد المتفاوضين كان شريكه مطالبا به تكملة البحر الرائق: ۱۸۲/٨] = من باب
وغصب ووديعة، ولا يتزوج
الثلاثة يقر بدين
المعاملة فقط
فلو أقر بدين يلزمه في الحال إذا أقر لغير الولد والوالد والزوج، ويبطل إقراره لهؤلاء عند أبي حنيفة خلافا لهما، وهذا عندنا، وعند حتى لا يسمع في غيره.
The authorized slave buys, sells, appoints agents for both, gives and takes pledges, hires, enters into mudarabah (commenda), hires himself out, and acknowledges a debt against himself for what obligates him. The authorized slave (for buying and selling) takes a pledge and enters into a partnership, contrary to al-Shafi'i. If authorized generally to buy and sell, he buys and sells not for a specific item. Similarly, if he is ordered to sell a specific cloth, he is not an authorized slave, and similarly if told 'hire yourself to so-and-so' because it is a single contract, thus it is employment, unlike if ordered to hire people out or said 'contract as a dyer, tailor, or fuller' because it is multiple contracts, thus indicating authorization; and because he did not specify whom to deal with, so it is an order to deal with people, as in al-Takmila by al-Dayri. (Fath, abridged and changed) 'sells etc.' becomes authorized and sells and buys by agreement. That is, if authorized generally, he buys and sells whatever types of goods occur to him with slight loss (ghabn), and similarly with excessive loss according to Abu Hanifa. The two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) said: It is not permissible with a loss that people do not tolerate, because excessive loss is like a gift, so it is considered from the sick person's third and is not permissible from the father, executor, or judge in the minor's property. His (Abu Hanifa's) argument: He acts with his own ownership, and authorization is removal of restriction, so his transaction is valid however it occurs. So Abu Hanifa equated here between sale and purchase in excessive loss, and distinguished between them in the agent's transaction because the agent recovers from the principal what befalls him, so the agent is suspected in purchase that he bought for himself, then when loss appeared he wanted to bind the principal, and this does not exist in the authorized slave's transaction because no one bears liability, so sale and purchase are equal in his regard. (Fath, Ayni, Mulla Miskin) 'and appoints agents etc.': i.e., it is permissible for the authorized slave to appoint agents for sale and purchase because they are appurtenances of trade, perhaps he cannot personally manage everything, so he needs assistance. (Takmilat al-Bahr al-Ra'iq: 8/179) Similarly, he may give something as pledge and take a pledge because they are appurtenances of trade. (Fath) 'and hires etc.' because it is the practice of merchants, so it is permissible for him to give and take mudarabah, and similarly to hire out his slaves and hire free men, and to give land for sharecropping (muzara'ah) and take it for irrigation (musaqat) because all that is from trade wealth. Ali said: 'The cultivator is the merchant of his Lord, and he may buy food and plant it therein, and hire houses and shops and rent them out for the acquisition of wealth, and enter into a limited partnership (shirkat al-inan) but not a full partnership (shirkat al-mufawadah) because it is based on agency and suretyship, and suretyship is not included in authorization. So if they did that, it would be a limited partnership because mufawadah is inan plus something extra, so it is valid to the extent that the authorized slave owns, which is agency.' (Takmilat al-Bahr al-Ra'iq: 8/181) 'and hires himself out etc.' means the authorized slave hires himself out, and we have mentioned it. Imam al-Shafi'i said he does not have that right because the authorization did not include disposition of his person, and therefore he cannot sell himself or pledge himself. We respond: Authorization includes acquisition of benefits, and hire is part of that, unlike sale and pledge because they nullify authorization. (Takmilat al-Bahr al-Ra'iq: 8/181) 'and acknowledges a debt etc.' even if he is indebted, but not to a spouse, parent, child, or his master, for his acknowledgment to them is void. (Tahawi) Because acknowledgment is an appurtenance of trade; if his acknowledgment were not valid, no one would deal with him, so his acknowledgment must be accepted in matters of trade, and acknowledgment of debt is part of that, and similarly of usurpation (ghasb) because the guarantee of usurpation is a compensatory guarantee in our school because he owns the usurped property through guarantee, so it is trade and of the compensatory type. Similarly, if one of the full partners acknowledged it, his partner would be held liable. (Takmilat al-Bahr al-Ra'iq: 8/182) 'and usurpation and deposit' The three acknowledge debt. 'of transaction only' So if he acknowledges a debt, it becomes binding immediately if he acknowledges to other than child, parent, or spouse, and his acknowledgment is void for these according to Abu Hanifa, contrary to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad). This is our position, and according to him it is not heard otherwise.
Source pages p. 243

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the chapter of the authorized slave (ma'dhun) from Kanz al-Daqa'iq. It details the scope of what a ma'dhun slave may do once his master has authorized him for trade. The commentary (from Fath, Ayni, and others) explains that general authorization for buying and selling permits all necessary trade transactions, including agency, pledge, hire, mudarabah, and partnership of inan, but not full partnership (mufawadah) because it involves suretyship which is beyond the authorization. The commentary also addresses the intra-Hanafi dispute between Abu Hanifa and the two disciples (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) regarding excessive loss (ghabn fahish): Abu Hanifa allows it because the ma'dhun acts with his own ownership and the authorization removes restriction, while the Sahibayn liken it to a gift and restrict it. Additionally, it contrasts the ma'dhun with an agent (wakil) where excessive loss in purchase raises suspicion. The passage also includes an inter-madhhab difference with Imam al-Shafi'i, who denies the ma'dhun the right to hire himself out, arguing that authorization does not cover his person. The Hanafi response is that acquiring benefits (manafi') is integral to trade, so hiring oneself is included. Finally, the commentary addresses acknowledgment of debt: the ma'dhun's acknowledgment is valid for trade debts, but not for debts to close relatives (spouse, parent, child, master) according to Abu Hanifa, to prevent collusion.

Key Terms
المأذون (al-ma'dhun): A slave whom his master has authorized to engage in trade, giving him legal capacity to transact within the scope of that authorization.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the main reason the Hanafi school gives for allowing the ma'dhun to hire himself out?
A) Authorization includes disposition of the person
B) Authorization includes acquisition of benefits
C) The master's permission is implied for any work
D) The ma'dhun is not a slave

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school permits the ma'dhun to hire himself out because it falls under the acquisition of benefits, which is integral to trade.

Buyu — Sales and Trade
intermediate
8 min
Prohibited Acts for the Authorized Slave (Ma'dhun)
Hanafi rulings on what the ma'dhun slave may not do

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the acts prohibited for the authorized slave (ma’dhun) and identify the exceptions based on the master's permission and trade custom.

Core Idea
The ma’dhun is bound to trade; marriage, gifts over a dirham, and manumission exceed his permit.

The authorized slave (ma'dhun) has permission to trade, but this permission does not extend to all acts. This passage delineates the boundaries: certain actions harm the master's interests or are not part of trade, and thus are prohibited unless the master specifically permits and there is no debt. We will explore the prohibited acts, the rationale, and the exceptions recognized in trade practice.

Guided Path
1
List the prohibited acts
Identify the acts that the ma'dhun cannot perform: marriage (for himself), marrying off his slave, kitabah, manumission, lending, taking a concubine, and giving gifts (above a dirham).
Each act is analyzed separately in the text.
2
Understand the rationale for prohibition
These acts are not part of trade (tijarah) and may impose financial burdens (e.g., mahr, maintenance) or are pure donations, which the ma'dhun does not own.
Trade custom (urf al-tujjar) allows minimal hospitality and price reductions.
3
Identify exceptions and conditions
Small gifts of food (under a dirham or non-wasteful) and price reduction for defects are permitted. The master's permission and absence of debt can lift the prohibition for some acts (e.g., manumission).
Exceptions are based on trade necessity or direct master consent.
4
Note intra-Hanafi differences
Abu Yusuf allows marrying off a female slave (as a type of trade), while Abu Hanifa and Muhammad prohibit it. Also, on manumission with overwhelming debt: Abu Hanifa does not execute, but the Sahibayn permit if master agrees.
These differences affect the mufta bihi ruling.
5
Apply the condition of debt
If the ma'dhun has debts, acts like kitabah and manumission are void even with the master's permission, because debt takes precedence.
This prevents harm to creditors.

كالرغيف ونحوه

قدر ما يحط التجار
فلو أقر بدين يلزمه في الحال إذا أقر لغير الولد والوالد والزوج، ويبطل إقراره لهؤلاء عند أبي حنيفة خلافا لهما، وهذا عندنا، وعند حتى لا يسمع في غيره. (تكملة، ملا مسكين، عيني) ولا يتزوج إلخ: أي ليس للعبد المأذون أن يتزوج؛ لأنه ليس من باب التجارة، ولأنه فيه ضرر على المولى؛ لوجوب المهر، والنفقة في رقبته. [تكملة : ١٨٤/٨] وكذا ليس له أن يزوج مملوكه، سواء كان ذكرا أو أنثى عندهما، وقال أبو يوسف: يجوز تزويج الأمة؛ لأنه نوع تجارة ولهما أنه مأذون في التجارة، وهذا ليس منه، ولهذا لا يملك تزويج العبد، وعلى هذا الخلاف الصبي المأذون له والمضارب وشريك العنان والأب والوصي. [رمز الحقائق: ٣١٩/٢] ولا يكاتب إلخ أي لا يكاتب العبد المأذون؛ لأنه ليس من باب التجارة، ولأن الكتابة أقوى من الإذن؛ لأن الكتابة توجب حرية اليد في الحال وحرية الرقبة في المآل، والإذن لا يوجب شيئا من ذلك، والشيء لا يتضمن ما فوقه إلا إذا أجازها المولى، ولم يكن عليه دين وذكر في "النهاية": أنه لو كان على العبد دين، فكتابته باطلة، وإن أجازها المولى؛ لأن قيام الدين يمنعه من ذلك، قل أو كثر ولا يعتق لأنه فوق الكتابة، فكان أولى بالامتناع، وإن أجازه المولى و لم يكن عليه دين جاز، وكان قبض العوض إليه إن كان العتق على مال، وإن كان عليه دين مستغرق لا ينفذ عند أبي حنيفة خلافا لهما رمز الحقائق: ۳۱۹/۲] (فتح) ولا يقرض لأنه تبرع ،ابتداء، وهو لا يملك وينبغي أن يقال: إذا كان درهما فصاعدا، فأما ما دونه فيجوز أن يقرض كما في الهبة. ولا يهب أطلقه فشمل ما إذا كان بعوض أو لا؛ لأنه تبرع ابتداء وانتهاء، وأطلق في منع الهبة، فشمل ما قيمته درهم وما دونه، وفي "المحيط : ولا يهب، وهذا إذا بلغت قيمته درهما فصاعدا، ويجوز هبة ما دون الدرهم؛ لأنه من صنيع التجارة؛ لأنه لابد للتجار منه ليعرف ويميل قلب الناس إليه تكملة البحر الرائق: ١٨٥/٨] ويهدي طعاما إلخ أي يجوز للمأذون أن يهدي طعاما يسيرا كالرغيف ونحوه، ويضيف من يطعمه، قال في "البزازية": ويملك إهداء مأكول، وإن زاد على درهم بما لا يعد إسرافا، ويتخذ الضيافة اليسيرة لا الكثيرة، وذا بقدر المال، حتى لو كان في يده عشرة آلاف درهم فالضيافة بعشرة يسيرة، ولو في يده عشرة دراهم، فبدانق كثيرة، والتقييد بالطعام للاحتراز عما سوى المأكولات من الدراهم والدنانير والثياب، إلا أن يهب مالا يساوي درهما، وإن أجاز المولى صحت إن لم يكن عليه دين والمحجور لا يهدي، وروي عن أبي يوسف: إذا دفع للمحجور قوت يومه، فدعا بعض رفقائه للأكل معه، فلا بأس، بخلاف ما لو دفع قوت شهر؛ لأنهم إذا أكلوه يضر بحال المولى. (ملتقط من الشروح) ويحط من الثمن إلخ يعني يجوز للمأذون حط الثمن من المبيع إن ظهر فيه عيب؛ لأنه من صنيع التجار، وأطلق المصنف الحط من الثمن بالعيب فشمل ما إذا كان الحط قدر العيب أو أكثر أو أقل، والحال أن الجواز مقيد
He may not marry, nor marry off his slave, nor make a contract of kitabah (manumission by payment), nor manumit, nor lend except with permission, nor take a concubine absolutely, whether male or female, nor give a gift, but he may give a small gift of food and feed guests and reduce the price due to a defect, like a loaf of bread or similar. The three acknowledge debt only of transaction. So if he acknowledges a debt, it becomes binding immediately if he acknowledges to other than child, parent, or spouse, and his acknowledgment is void for these according to Abu Hanifa, contrary to the two. This is our position. 'Nor marry etc.' means the authorized slave may not marry because it is not from trade and because it harms the master due to the obligation of dower and maintenance on his neck. [Takmila 8/184] Similarly, he may not marry off his slave, whether male or female according to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), and Abu Yusuf said: Marrying off a female slave is permissible because it is a type of trade. 'Nor make kitabah etc.' means the authorized slave may not make a kitabah contract because it is not from trade and because kitabah is stronger than authorization; kitabah gives freedom of hand immediately and freedom of ownership eventually, while authorization gives none of that, and a thing cannot imply what is greater than it, unless the master permits and there is no debt against him. It is mentioned in al-Nihayah: If the slave has debt, his kitabah is void even if the master permits because debt prevents it, little or much. 'Nor manumit' because it is above kitabah, so it is more deserving of prohibition. If the master permits and there is no debt, it is valid and he takes the price if it is for money. If debt is overwhelming, it is not executed according to Abu Hanifa, contrary to the two. (Ramz al-Haqa'iq 2/319) (Fath) 'Nor lend' because it is a gift from the beginning, and he does not own that. It should be said: if it is a dirham or more, but less than that is permissible as in gift. 'Nor give a gift' he made it absolute, covering whether with compensation or not; it is a gift from beginning to end, and he prohibited gift absolutely, covering what is worth a dirham or less. In al-Muhit: he may not give a gift if its value reaches a dirham or more, and gift of less than a dirham is permissible because it is from trade practices; merchants need it to be known and attract people's hearts. (Takmilat al-Bahr al-Ra'iq 8/185) 'and gives food etc.' means it is permissible for the authorized slave to give a small gift of food like a loaf and invite guests to eat. It is said in al-Bazzaziyyah: He has the right to gift food even if exceeding a dirham as long as it is not wasteful, and to offer small hospitality not large, according to his means. 'and reduces the price due to a defect' means it is permissible for the authorized slave to reduce the price if a defect appears, because it is from trade practices. The author made the reduction absolute, covering whether the reduction equals the defect or more or less, whereas permissibility is conditional.
Source pages p. 244

Commentary
This passage continues the rulings on the authorized slave (ma'dhun), focusing on prohibited acts. The ma'dhun cannot marry or arrange marriage for his slave, as marriage is not a trade transaction and imposes financial burdens on the master. There is an intra-Hanafi difference: Abu Yusuf allows marrying off a female slave as it is a type of trade, but the other two (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad) forbid it. Kitabah (contract for freedom) and manumission are also prohibited because they are stronger than authorization and not trade acts; however, if the master permits and there is no debt, they may be valid. Lending money is prohibited as it is a gift, unless the amount is less than a dirham. Gifts are generally prohibited if the value is a dirham or more, but small gifts of food and hospitality are allowed as they are customary in trade to build goodwill. Reduction of price for a defect is permitted as it is a trade practice. The commentary also revisits the acknowledgment of debt, specifying that acknowledgment to close relatives is void according to Abu Hanifa, to prevent collusion.

Key Terms
الكتابة (al-kitābah): A contract between a master and his slave whereby the slave gains freedom in exchange for paying a sum of money in installments.
الهبة (al-hibah): A gift, which is a voluntary transfer of ownership without compensation.
الحط من الثمن (al-ḥaṭṭ min al-thaman): Reduction of the sale price due to a defect in the merchandise.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is the authorized slave (ma'dhun) prohibited from making a kitabah contract?
A) Because kitabah is a form of trade
B) Because kitabah is stronger than the authorization and requires the master's direct permission
C) Because the ma'dhun cannot own money to pay for kitabah
D) Because kitabah is invalid in all cases for slaves

Key Takeaway
The ma'dhun's authorization is limited to trade; acts that are not trade or are pure donations (like marriage, gifts over a dirham, manumission without compensation) are prohibited unless the master specifically permits and there is no debt.

Prohibited Non-Trade Acts of the Ma'dhun
Lists prohibited acts for the ma'dhun with commentary on each

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify which acts are prohibited for the ma'dhun without the master's permission and explain the underlying principle that only trade-related acts are permitted.

Core Idea
A ma'dhun cannot marry, manumit, lend, or give large gifts because these are not trade.

The ma'dhun (slave permitted to trade) has limited authority. This lesson examines the specific acts he may not perform without the master's consent, the reasoning behind each prohibition, and the intra-Hanafi dispute regarding marrying off a female slave.

Guided Path
1
List the Prohibited Acts
Identify each act mentioned in the text: marriage, marrying off his slave, kitaba, manumission, lending, taking a concubine, and giving gifts of significant value.
The commentary explains the reason for each.
2
Analyze the Reasoning
For each act, locate the rationale from the commentary. Usually it is because the act is not from the 'bāb al-tijāra' (type of trade) or causes harm to the master (like incurring mahr and nafaqa).
3
Examine the Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Note that Abu Yusuf permits marrying off a female slave by the ma'dhun, while Abu Hanifa and Muhammad prohibit it. Identify the evidence for each side.
The dispute stems from whether this act is considered trade.
4
Generalize the Principle
Formulate the rule: The ma'dhun's permission is limited to trade; any act outside that scope is prohibited unless specifically allowed by trade custom (e.g., small gifts).
This principle also applies to similar agents like the permitted minor, mudarib, etc.
5
Apply to Analogous Cases
Review the text's mention that the same difference of opinion applies to the ma'dhun minor, mudarib, etc. Recognize the consistent application of the principle.
