أحكام المأذون وغصب ووديعة، ولا يتزوج ولا يزوج مملوكه ولا يكاتب ولا يعتق ولا يقرض إلا بإذن ولا يتسرى مطلقا عبدا أو أمة ولا يهب، ويهدي طعاما يسيرا ويضيف من يطعمه ويحط من الثمن بعيب كالرغيف ونحوه. (تكملة، ملا مسكين، عيني) ولا يتزوج إلخ: أي ليس للعبد المأذون أن يتزوج؛ لأنه ليس من باب التجارة، ولأن فيه ضرر على المولى؛ لوجوب المهر، والنفقة في رقبته. [تكملة : ١٨٤/٨] وكذا ليس له أن يزوج مملوكه، سواء كان ذكرا أو أنثى عندهما، وقال أبو يوسف: يجوز تزويج الأمة؛ لأنه نوع تجارة ولهما أنه مأذون في التجارة، وهذا ليس منه، ولهذا لا يملك تزويج العبد، وعلى هذا الخلاف الصبي المأذون له والمضارب وشريك العنان والأب والوصي. [رمز الحقائق: ٣١٩/٢] ولا يكاتب إلخ أي لا يكاتب العبد المأذون؛ لأنه ليس من باب التجارة، ولأن الكتابة أقوى من الإذن؛ لأن الكتابة توجب حرية اليد في الحال وحرية الرقبة في المآل، والإذن لا يوجب شيئا من ذلك، والشيء لا يتضمن ما فوقه إلا إذا أجازها المولى، ولم يكن عليه دين وذكر في "النهاية": أنه لو كان على العبد دين، فكتابته باطلة، وإن أجازها المولى؛ لأن قيام الدين يمنعه من ذلك، قل أو كثر ولا يعتق لأنه فوق الكتابة، فكان أولى بالامتناع، وإن أجازه المولى و لم يكن عليه دين جاز، وكان قبض العوض إليه إن كان العتق على مال، وإن كان عليه دين مستغرق لا ينفذ عند أبي حنيفة خلافا لهما رمز الحقائق: ۳۱۹/۲] (فتح) ولا يقرض لأنه تبرع ،ابتداء، وهو لا يملك وينبغي أن يقال: إذا كان درهما فصاعدا، فأما ما دونه فيجوز أن يقرض كما في الهبة. ولا يهب أطلقه فشمل ما إذا كان بعوض أو لا؛ لأنه تبرع ابتداء وانتهاء، وأطلق في منع الهبة، فشمل ما قيمته درهم وما دونه، وفي "المحيط : ولا يهب، وهذا إذا بلغت قيمته درهما فصاعدا، ويجوز هبة ما دون الدرهم؛ لأنه من صنيع التجارة؛ لأنه لابد للتجار منه ليعرف ويميل قلب الناس إليه تكملة البحر الرائق: ١٨٥/٨] ويهدي طعاما إلخ أي يجوز للمأذون أن يهدي طعاما يسيرا كالرغيف ونحوه، ويضيف من يطعمه، قال في "البزازية": ويملك إهداء مأكول، وإن زاد على درهم بما لا يعد إسرافا، ويتخذ الضيافة اليسيرة لا الكثيرة، وذا بقدر المال، حتى لو كان في يده عشرة آلاف درهم فالضيافة بعشرة يسيرة، ولو في يده عشرة دراهم، فبدانق كثيرة، والتقييد بالطعام للاحتراز عما سوى المأكولات من الدراهم والدنانير والثياب، إلا أن يهب مالا يساوي درهما، وإن أجاز المولى صحت إن لم يكن عليه دين والمحجور لا يهدي، وروي عن أبي يوسف: إذا دفع للمحجور قوت يومه، فدعا بعض رفقائه للأكل معه، فلا بأس، بخلاف ما لو دفع قوت شهر؛ لأنهم إذا أكلوه يضر بحال المولى. (ملتقط من الشروح) ويحط من الثمن إلخ يعني يجوز للمأذون حط الثمن من المبيع إن ظهر فيه عيب؛ لأنه من صنيع التجار، وأطلق المصنف الحط من الثمن بالعيب فشمل ما إذا كان الحط قدر العيب أو أكثر أو أقل، والحال أن الجواز مقيد بما إذا كان قدر العيب فلو قال: "بقدره لكان أولى ، فلو حط من غير عيب، أو حط أكثر من العادة لا يجوز؛ لأنه تبرع محض بعد تمام العقد، وهو ليس من صنيع التجار، فلا ضرورة إليه، بخلاف المحاباة ابتداء؛ لأنه قد يحتاج إليه التاجر. تكملة البحر الرائق: ١٨٥/٨]
Rulings on the permitted slave: and usurpation and deposit, and he may not marry, nor marry off his slave, nor make a kitaba contract, nor manumit, nor lend except with permission, nor take a concubine absolutely, slave male or female, nor give a gift, and he may give a small food gift and host one who eats with him and deduct from the price due to a defect like a loaf of bread and similar. Commentary: He may not marry... until end of discount ruling.
Source pages p. 244

Commentary
This passage states the general rule: a ma'dhun (slave permitted to trade) is restricted in actions that are not part of trade. The commentary explains each restriction: marriage is prohibited because it is not trade and harms the master due to obligatory mahr and nafaqa. Similarly, he cannot marry off his slave; Abu Yusuf permits marrying off a female slave as it is a type of trade, but Abu Hanifa and Muhammad disagree. Kitaba (contract of manumission) is not allowed because it is stronger than permission and leads to freedom. Manumission is also prohibited. Lending is forbidden as it is a pure donation. Gifts are forbidden if value reaches one dirham; below that is allowed as it is trade practice. Small food gifts and hospitality are permitted in moderation. Discount for defect is allowed as trade practice, but only to the extent of the defect; discounting without defect or beyond custom is not allowed because it is pure donation after contract completion.

Key Terms
المأذون (al-ma'dhun): A slave who has been given permission by his master to engage in trade.
التبرع (al-tabarru'): A voluntary donation without compensation, which the ma'dhun cannot do without permission.
المحاباة (al-muhabah): A transaction where the seller gives a favor to the buyer, e.g., reducing price as a gift.
الكتابة (al-kitaba): A contract whereby a slave is promised freedom in exchange for payments.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school (majority view), which of the following acts by a ma'dhun is considered void without the master's permission?
A) Giving a small food gift worth half a dirham
B) Deducting the price due to a defect in the goods
C) Lending a dirham to another trader
D) Hosting a friend with a small meal

Key Takeaway
The ma'dhun's permission is strictly limited to trade; any non-trade act, especially those involving donation or personal status, is void without the master's explicit consent.

Buyu — Sales and Trade

Permitted Trade-Related Acts: Small Gifts and Discount for Defect
Lists permitted acts and their conditions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why small gifts and discounts for defect are permitted for the ma'dhun and identify the conditions that must be met.

Core Idea
Small gifts and defect-related discounts are trade practices allowed to the ma'dhun within limits.

While the ma'dhun is prohibited from making large donations, he is allowed to give small food gifts, host guests moderately, and deduct from the price when a defect is discovered. These acts are considered part of trade custom (ṣunʿ al-tijāra) and are thus within his authority. This lesson examines the conditions and limits of these permissions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Permitted Acts
List the acts explicitly permitted: giving small food gifts, hosting, and deducting the price due to a defect.
2
Analyze the Rationale: Trade Practice
Note that these acts are permitted because they are part of 'ṣunʿ al-tijāra' (trade practice). The commentary explains that traders necessarily give small gifts and make discounts to attract customers.
The permission is not based on the act being trade itself, but on custom.
3
Determine the Conditions and Limits
For gifts: only food of small quantity, not extravagant. For hosting: moderate, relative to the capital. For discount: only for a defect, and only to the amount of the defect (not more).
The text gives examples: a loaf of bread for gift; hosting with a tenth of capital for 10,000 dirhams is small; discount must be exactly the defect value.
4
Distinguish from Prohibited Donations
Understand why these are not considered pure tabarru' (donation). The small gift is an inducement for trade, the hosting is a social necessity, and the discount is a correction of the price. They are not free gifts.
Discount without defect or beyond defect is pure tabarru' and thus prohibited.
5
Apply to Analogous Situations
Consider that the same principle would apply to other small trade incentives, like giving free samples or offering a small discount for prompt payment, as long as they are customary and not excessive.
The modern analogy: a sales agent may offer small promotional items within reason.

Key Terms
الصنيع (al-ṣunʿ): Trade practice or custom; the basis for permitting small gifts and discounts.
المحاباة (al-muhabah): A favor in price at the time of initial sale; distinguished from post-sale discount.
الضيافة (al-diyāfah): Hosting; permitted moderately for the ma'dhun.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A ma'dhun deducts 5 dirhams from the price of a defective item where the defect actually reduces the value by 3 dirhams. According to the Hanafi school, this deduction is:
A) Permissible, because deduction is allowed for any defect.
B) Permissible, as long as the deduction is less than 10 dirhams.
C) Not permissible, because it exceeds the amount of the defect.
D) Not permissible, because no deduction is allowed after contract completion.

Key Takeaway
The ma'dhun may engage in small trade practices like giving food gifts and discounting for defects, but these are limited by custom and proportionality.

The Ma'dhun's Confession of Debt to Close Relatives
Intra-Hanafi dispute on the validity of ma'dhun's confession to relatives

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the ma'dhun's confession of debt to close relatives and identify the intra-madhhab difference between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn.

Core Idea
Abu Hanifa: confession to child/parent/spouse invalid; Sahibayn: valid. The mufta bihi is Abu Hanifa's view.

This lesson examines a specific ruling within the Hanafi school concerning a ma'dhun (slave permitted to trade) who confesses to a debt. While his confession to a stranger binds him immediately, a dispute arises when the confession is made to his child, parent, or spouse. Understanding this dispute reveals the school's concern for preventing collusion and the authority of the founding imams.

Guided Path
1
Identify the parties and the act
A ma'dhun is a slave given permission to trade. He can incur debts in the course of trade. His confession (iqrar) of a debt normally makes it binding immediately.
The ma'dhun's capacity is limited to trade-related transactions.
2
State the exception according to Abu Hanifa
Abu Hanifa rules that if the ma'dhun confesses to a debt to his child, parent, or spouse, the confession is invalid (batil) and not binding.
These three are considered likely to collude with the ma'dhun to harm the owner.
3
Contrast with the Sahibayn
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (the Sahibayn) hold that the confession is valid and binding immediately, just as with any other person.
They see no reason to distinguish relatives; the ma'dhun's confession is a strong proof anyway.
4
Identify the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference: under Abu Hanifa, if the ma'dhun confesses to his son, the son cannot collect from the ma'dhun's owner; under the Sahibayn, he can.
This affects creditors' rights and the owner's liability.
5
Determine the tarjih
The text sides with Abu Hanifa, saying 'وهذا عندنا' (this is according to us), indicating the mufta bihi position is his.
Later Hanafi works generally adopt Abu Hanifa's view as the relied-upon position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثلاثة يقر بدين المعاملة فقط قدر ما يحط التجار فلو أقر بدين يلزمه في الحال إذا أقر لغير الولد والوالد والزوج، ويبطل إقراره لهؤلاء عند أبي حنيفة خلافا لهما، وهذا عندنا، وعند حتى لا يسمع في غيره.
The three [cases]? He confessed to debt, the transaction only, as much as the merchants discount. So if he confessed to a debt, it becomes binding immediately if he confessed to other than child, parent, and spouse, and his confession is invalid for these according to Abu Hanifa, contrary to the two. And this according to us, and so that it is not heard in other [cases].
Source pages p. 244

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the ma'dhun's confession (iqrar) of debt. The ruling: if the ma'dhun confesses to a debt, it becomes binding immediately, except if he confesses to his child, parent, or spouse. In those cases, Abu Hanifa holds that the confession is invalid, while Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (the Sahibayn) hold it is valid. The text indicates that the relied-upon position is Abu Hanifa's, saying 'this is according to us.'

Key Terms
إقرار (iqrar): A legal confession or admission of a right or debt.
الولد والوالد والزوج (al-walad wa al-walid wa al-zawj): The child, parent, and spouse of the ma'dhun; confession to them is invalid according to Abu Hanifa.
مأذون (ma'dhun): A slave permitted by his owner to engage in trade.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the mufta bihi position, to which of the following does the ma'dhun's confession of debt NOT bind him immediately?
A) A stranger
B) His child
C) His business partner
D) His neighbour

Key Takeaway
Within the Hanafi school, the ma'dhun's confession of debt to his child, parent, or spouse is invalid according to the relied-upon view of Abu Hanifa.

Debt Attachment to the Ma'dhun's Person (Raqaba)
First part of the passage: ruling and dispute on debt attachment to raqaba

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position that the ma'dhun's trade debt is attached to his raqaba and explain the opposing views of Zufar and al-Shafi'i along with the practical consequence.

Core Idea
The ma'dhun can be sold for his trade debt because the debt attaches to his person, not just his earnings.

The Hanafi school uniquely holds that when a ma'dhun slave incurs debt in trade, the debt attaches to his person (raqaba). If the master does not pay, the slave can be sold. This ruling is based on the need to protect creditors and the analogy to consumption debts. Other schools limit the debt to earnings.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Hanafi ruling
State that the debt of the ma'dhun is attached to his raqaba, so he can be sold if the master does not redeem him.
2
Understand the Hanafi proof
The debt appears against the master due to permission; the closest source is the slave's person, analogous to a debt of consumption.
The common factor is repelling harm from people.
3
Contrast with Zufar's view
Zufar held that debt attaches only to earnings because the person was not included in the permission.
Al-Shafi'i and Malik agreed.
4
Examine the practical difference
Hanafi: slave can be sold; other schools: only earnings used, so creditor might wait longer.
This is the thamarah al-khilaf.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ودينه متعلق برقبته يباع به إن لم يفده سيّده، وقسم ثمنه بالحصص، وما بقي الذي وجب عليه بتجارة طولب به بعد عتقه، وينحجر. أي العبد ما بقي أي المأذون بين الغرماء بما إذا كان قدر العيب فلو قال: "بقدره لكان أولى ، فلو حط من غير عيب، أو حط أكثر من العادة لا يجوز؛ لأنه تبرع محض بعد تمام العقد، وهو ليس من صنيع التجار، فلا ضرورة إليه، بخلاف المحاباة ابتداء؛ لأنه قد يحتاج إليه التاجر. تكملة البحر الرائق: ١٨٥/٨]( بزيادة) ودينه إلخ أي الدين الذي يركب المأذون متعلق برقبته يباع المأذون بسببه إن لم يفده سيده بقضاء الدين، وقال زفر: يتعلق بالكسب لا بالرقبة، فلا تباع رقبته؛ لأن الرقبة لم تدخل في الإذن فلا يتعلق دين التجارة بها، بخلاف الكسب؛ لأنه دخل في الإذن وبه قال الشافعي ومالك وعن أحمد يتعلق بذمة سيده، ولنا: أنه ظاهر في حق المولى بسبب الإذن، وكل دين يظهر في حقه فهو متعلق بالرقبة؛ لأنه لابد من محل يستوفى منه، وأقرب المحال إليه نفسه، فصار كدين الاستهلاك، والجامع دفع ضرر الناس [رمز الحقائق: ٣١٩/٢] وقسم ثمنه إلخ : أي إذا لم يفد السيد العبد المأذون المديون وبيع في دينه قسم ثمنه بين الغرماء بالحصص؛ لأن ديونهم متعلقة ب برقبته، فيتحاصصون من الاستيفاء وفاء من البدل كما في التركة وإن لم يكن بالثمن وفاء يضرب كل غريم في الثمن بقدر حقه كالتركة إذا ضاقت فإن فضل شيء من ديونه طولب به بعد الحرية، ولا يباع ثانيا كي لا يمتنع الناس عن شراء المأذون تكملة البحر الرائق: ۱۸۸/۸] (بتوضيح من المحشي) طولب به إلخ أي ما بقي من الدين الذي بيع العبد فيه طولب به بعد عتقه، ولا يطالب به للحال؛ لأن دينهم ثابت في ذمته ولم يستوفوا الكل منه؛ لأن رقبته لم تف به فبقي دينهم على حاله في ذمته، فيستوفونه إذا قدر على أدائه، ولا يقدر إلا بعد العتق؛ لأنه لا يمكن بيعه ثانيا. [ رمز الحقائق (۳۱۹/۲ ، ۳۲۰] لأن المشتري يمتنع حينئذ عن شرائه، فيؤدي إلى امتناع البيع بالكلية، فيتضرر الغرماء، ولا استسعاؤه؛ لأن من هو مخير بين شيئين أو أشياء فاختار أحدهما بطل خياره في غيره؛ ولو اشتراه بعد ذلك مولاه الذي باعه للغرماء لم يكن لهم على العبد تعلق؛ لأن هذا ملك جديد، بسبب جديد وتبدل الملك كتبدل العين حكما، فصار كأنه عبد آخر، وفي نفقة الزوجة يباع مرارا؛ لأن المنفعة تتجدد ساعة فساعة فيكون دينا حادثا بعد البيع. (عيني، تكملة، فتح) وينحجر إلخ أي ينحجر العبد المأذون بحجر المولى إن علم بالحجر أكثر أهل السوق، وقال الشافعي: الحجر صحيح وإن لم يعلم به أحد من أهل سوقه، ولنا أن حجره لو صح بدون علمهم لتضرروا به؛ لأنه إذا اكتسب شيئا، فالمولى يأخذه فيتأخر حقهم إلى ما بعد العتق، وهو موهوم؛ لأنه قد يعتق، وقد لا يعتق، وقيد بالأكثر وهو الاستحسان؛ لأن إعلام الكل متعذر، أو متعسر، ويشترط علم العبد أيضا، وبقي مأذونا له حتى يعلم بالحجر. تكملة البحر الرائق: ١٩٠/٨]
And his debt is attached to his person; he is sold for it if his master does not save him. And his price is divided proportionally. And what remains of what was owed by him due to trade is demanded after his emancipation. And he is interdicted. That is the slave, what remains, the ma'dhun among the creditors... (commentary continues with disputes and details).
Source pages p. 245

Commentary
This passage covers the enforcement of debts against the ma'dhun. The key rulings: (1) The debt incurred by the ma'dhun in trade is attached to his person (raqaba), so if the master does not pay, the slave can be sold. Zufar, Shafi'i, and Malik disagreed, saying it attaches only to earnings. The Hanafi reasoning is that since the debt appears against the master due to the permission, the closest source is the slave's own person, like a debt of consumption. (2) When sold, the price is divided among creditors proportionally, like in an estate. (3) If there is a remainder of debt after sale, the creditor can pursue it after the slave becomes free; he cannot be sold again to avoid discouraging buyers. (4) The master can interdict (hajr) the ma'dhun, but only if most market people know about it (and the slave knows); otherwise, the slave remains ma'dhun until he learns. Shafi'i held that hijr is valid without anyone knowing. The Hanafi position is based on istihsan to protect creditors.

Key Terms
الرقبة (al-raqaba): The person or body of the slave, which can be sold to satisfy debts.
الحجر (al-hajr): Legal interdiction by the master preventing the ma'dhun from trading.
الغرماء (al-ghurama'): The creditors who have a claim against the ma'dhun; they share the sale price proportionally.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In Hanafi fiqh, the ma'dhun's trade debt is attached to which?
A) his earnings only
B) his person (raqaba)
C) his master's wealth
D) his future freedom

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school permits selling the ma'dhun for his trade debts because the debt is attached to his person.

Conditions for Validity of the Master's Interdiction (Hajr)
Second part: ruling on hijr and dispute with Shafi'i

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the Hanafi condition for the validity of the master's interdiction of the ma'dhun and explain the rationale based on istihsan.

Core Idea
The master's hijr of the ma'dhun is valid only if most market people and the slave know about it — to protect creditors.

The master can interdict his ma'dhun slave, preventing him from trading. But the Hanafi school imposes a condition: most market people must be aware of the interdiction. Otherwise, the slave remains ma'dhun until he learns. This lesson examines the dispute with al-Shafi'i and the Hanafi proof based on istihsan.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position on hijr
The hijr is valid only if most of the market people know about it and the slave himself knows.
2
Understand the Hanafi proof
If hijr were valid without knowledge, creditors would be harmed because the master could take the slave's earnings, delaying their rights.
Condition of knowledge of most is istihsan because informing everyone is difficult.
3
Contrast with Shafi'i view
Al-Shafi'i holds that hijr is valid even if no one knows.
4
Identify the practical difference
Hanafi: slave remains ma'dhun until he learns of hijr. Shafi'i: hijr effective immediately.
Creditors' rights are protected under Hanafi.

Buyu — Sales and Trade
intermediate
8 min
Debt Attachment of the Authorized Slave
Ruling on debt attachment to slave's person and sale for repayment

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position that an authorized slave's trade debt attaches to his person, explain the Hanafi proof by qiyas to debt of destruction, and compare it with the views of Zufar, Shafi'i, and Malik.

Core Idea
An authorized slave's trade debt attaches to his person, not just his earnings – a Hanafi rule to protect creditors.

When a slave authorized to trade incurs debt, creditors need a way to recover their money. The Hanafi school holds that such debt attaches to the slave's person (رقبة), allowing the slave to be sold if the master does not pay. However, some early Hanafi jurists and other madhhabs disagree. This lesson examines the dispute, its proofs, and its practical consequences.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify the ruling: the authorized slave's trade debt is attached to his person (رقبة). If the master does not pay, the slave can be sold to satisfy the debt.
This is the relied-upon (mufta bihi) position in the school.
2
Identify the opposing views
Zufar (intra-Hanafi) and the Shafi'i/Maliki schools hold that the debt attaches only to earnings. Ahmad (Hanbali) holds it attaches to the master's liability.
Note the different schools and their positions.
3
Analyze the Hanafi proof (qiyas)
The Hanafi proof analogizes the trade debt to a debt of destruction (istihlak). Both require a tangible source for recovery; the common illa is repelling harm from creditors. The closest source is the slave's own person.
The proof is qiyas khafi (subtle analogy) based on necessity.
4
Examine the thamarah al-khilaf
Under the Hanafi view, the creditor can demand sale of the slave. Under Zufar/Shafi'i/Malik, only earnings can be taken. Under Ahmad, the master must pay.
This difference has practical impact on debt recovery.
5
Apply the distribution and secondary rules
If sold, the price is divided proportionally among creditors. Remaining debt is collectible after emancipation. No second sale (to avoid deterring buyers) except for wife's maintenance.
These rules flow from the attachment principle.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ودينه متعلق برقبته يباع به إن لم يفده سيّده، وقسم ثمنه بالحصص، وما بقي الذي وجب عليه بتجارة طولب به بعد عتقه، وينحجر. أي العبد ما بقي بين الغرماء بما إذا كان قدر العيب فلو قال: "بقدره لكان أولى"، فلو حط من غير عيب، أو حط أكثر من العادة لا يجوز؛ لأنه تبرع محض بعد تمام العقد، وهو ليس من صنيع التجار، فلا ضرورة إليه، بخلاف المحاباة ابتداء؛ لأنه قد يحتاج إليه التاجر. تكملة البحر الرائق: ١٨٥/٨]( بزيادة) ودينه إلخ أي الدين الذي يركب المأذون متعلق برقبته يباع المأذون بسببه إن لم يفده سيده بقضاء الدين، وقال زفر: يتعلق بالكسب لا بالرقبة، فلا تباع رقبته؛ لأن الرقبة لم تدخل في الإذن فلا يتعلق دين التجارة بها، بخلاف الكسب؛ لأنه دخل في الإذن وبه قال الشافعي ومالك وعن أحمد يتعلق بذمة سيده، ولنا: أنه ظاهر في حق المولى بسبب الإذن، وكل دين يظهر في حقه فهو متعلق بالرقبة؛ لأنه لابد من محل يستوفى منه، وأقرب المحال إليه نفسه، فصار كدين الاستهلاك، والجامع دفع ضرر الناس [رمز الحقائق: ٣١٩/٢] وقسم ثمنه إلخ : أي إذا لم يفد السيد العبد المأذون المديون وبيع في دينه قسم ثمنه بين الغرماء بالحصص؛ لأن ديونهم متعلقة برقبته، فيتحاصصون من الاستيفاء وفاء من البدل كما في التركة وإن لم يكن بالثمن وفاء يضرب كل غريم في الثمن بقدر حقه كالتركة إذا ضاقت فإن فضل شيء من ديونه طولب به بعد الحرية، ولا يباع ثانيا كي لا يمتنع الناس عن شراء المأذون تكملة البحر الرائق: ۱۸۸/۸] (بتوضيح من المحشي) طولب به إلخ أي ما بقي من الدين الذي بيع العبد فيه طولب به بعد عتقه، ولا يطالب به للحال؛ لأن دينهم ثابت في ذمته ولم يستوفوا الكل منه؛ لأن رقبته لم تف به فبقي دينهم على حاله في ذمته، فيستوفونه إذا قدر على أدائه، ولا يقدر إلا بعد العتق؛ لأنه لا يمكن بيعه ثانيا. [ رمز الحقائق (۳۱۹/۲ ، ۳۲۰] لأن المشتري يمتنع حينئذ عن شرائه، فيؤدي إلى امتناع البيع بالكلية، فيتضرر الغرماء، ولا استسعاؤه؛ لأن من هو مخير بين شيئين أو أشياء فاختار أحدهما بطل خياره في غيره؛ ولو اشتراه بعد ذلك مولاه الذي باعه للغرماء لم يكن لهم على العبد تعلق؛ لأن هذا ملك جديد، بسبب جديد وتبدل الملك كتبدل العين حكما، فصار كأنه عبد آخر، وفي نفقة الزوجة يباع مرارا؛ لأن المنفعة تتجدد ساعة فساعة فيكون دينا حادثا بعد البيع. (عيني، تكملة، فتح)
And his debt is attached to his person; he is sold for it if his master does not pay it. And his sale price is divided proportionally. And what remains of the debt incurred through trade is demanded after his emancipation, and he is interdicted—i.e., the slave remains among the creditors—when it is equal to the defect. If he said 'by its amount' it would be better. So if he abates without defect, or abates more than customary, it is not permissible, because it is pure charity after the contract's completion, and it is not the practice of merchants, so there is no necessity for it. Unlike abatement at the beginning, because the merchant may need it. (Completion of al-Bahr al-Ra'iq 8:185) (Addition:) 'And his debt etc.' i.e., the debt that the authorized slave incurs is attached to his person; the authorized slave is sold for it if his master does not pay the debt. Zufar said: It is attached to earnings, not the person, so his person is not sold because the person did not enter into the authorization, so trade debt does not attach to it, unlike earnings because they entered the authorization. Al-Shafi'i and Malik said likewise. From Ahmad, it is attached to the master's liability. Our proof: It is apparent against the master due to the authorization, and every debt that appears against him is attached to the person because there must be a source from which it is recovered, and the closest source is his own person, so it becomes like the debt of destruction, and the common factor is repelling harm from people. (Rumuz al-Haqa'iq 2:319) 'And the division of his price etc.': i.e., if the master does not ransom the indebted authorized slave and he is sold for his debt, his price is divided among the creditors proportionally because their debts are attached to his person, so they share in recovery from the substitute as in the estate. If the price is insufficient, each creditor strikes the price according to his right, like the estate when it is insufficient. If something remains of his debts, it is demanded after freedom, and he is not sold a second time so that people are not deterred from buying the authorized slave. (Completion of al-Bahr al-Ra'iq 8:188) (Clarified by the glossator:) 'Demanded after it etc.': i.e., what remains of the debt for which the slave was sold is demanded after his emancipation, and he is not demanded for it immediately because their debt is established in his liability, and they have not recovered all of it because his person did not suffice for it, so their debt remains as is in his liability; they recover it when he is able to pay, and he is not able except after emancipation because he cannot be sold a second time (Rumuz al-Haqa'iq 2:319-320) because the buyer would then refrain from purchasing him, leading to the complete prevention of sale, harming the creditors. And he is not made to earn (istis'aa') because one who is given a choice between two or more things and chooses one, his choice in the other is void. And if thereafter his master who sold him to the creditors buys him, they have no claim against the slave, because this is a new ownership, due to a new cause, and change of ownership is like change of the thing in ruling, so he becomes like another slave. And in the wife's maintenance, he is sold repeatedly because the benefit is renewed hour by hour, so it becomes a debt arising after the sale. (Ayni, Completion, Fath)
Source pages p. 245

Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab dispute concerning the legal status of an authorized slave's trade debt. The Hanafi position, supported by Abu Hanifa and the majority of the school, is that the debt is attached to the slave's person (رقبة), allowing the slave to be sold to satisfy creditors if the master does not pay. Zufar (a Hanafi dissenter) and the Shafi'i and Maliki schools hold that the debt attaches only to the slave's earnings, not his person. Ahmad ibn Hanbal is reported to hold that it attaches to the master's liability. The Hanafi proof is based on the necessity of a tangible source for recovery and the principle of repelling harm from people (دفع ضرر الناس). The commentary then elaborates on distribution of sale proceeds: creditors share proportionally, and any remaining debt is collectible after the slave's emancipation. The slave cannot be sold twice to prevent deterring buyers. If the master buys the slave back after the initial sale, it is a new ownership, and past debts do not transfer. An exception is made for wife's maintenance, where the slave can be sold repeatedly because the obligation is renewed continuously.

Key Terms
المأذون (al-ma'dhun): A slave authorized by his master to engage in trade.
الرقبة (al-raqabah): The physical person of the slave, including his body and being.
زفر (Zufar): Zufar ibn al-Hudhayl, a prominent early Hanafi jurist who dissented on some rulings.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, to what is an authorized slave's trade debt primarily attached?
A) The slave's earnings only
B) The master's liability
C) The slave's person (رقبة)
D) The slave's family

Key Takeaway
An authorized slave's trade debt attaches to his person, allowing sale for repayment; this Hanafi position is based on qiyas to debt of destruction to protect creditors.

Conditions for Effective Hajr on a Ma'dhun: Public Knowledge and Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Conditions for hajr validity based on permission scope

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the three scenarios of hajr validity based on the scope of permission and distinguish the positions of Imam Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn regarding witness requirements.

Core Idea
Restriction must match permission's scope; private permission requires private restriction.

This passage from al-Hidayah commentaries explains when a master's legal restriction (hajr) on a ma'dhun is effective. It classifies permission into three levels of publicity and ties the restriction's validity to that same level. The text also addresses a key intra-Hanafi dispute: how many witnesses are needed if the slave denies being informed of the restriction.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three permission scenarios
Permission is either publicly known (most of the market), known to a specific few, or known only to the slave. Your task is to recognize each category from the text.
The text explicitly lists these three cases.
2
Match restriction to permission scope
For each scenario, note the required condition for the restriction to be effective: public knowledge, presence of the few, or presence of the slave. The principle is that permission does not accept specification (takhsis).
A general permission cannot be withdrawn by a private restriction.
3
Understand the intra-Hanafi dispute
When the slave denies being informed, Imam Abu Hanifa requires multiple witnesses or one just witness. Abu Yusuf and Muhammad suffice with one informant absolutely. Locate this ruling in the text.
The dispute only arises when the slave denies; if he confirms, there is consensus.
4
Apply the criterion of shuyu'
The ultimate criterion is the spread (shuyu') of knowledge about the restriction, not the location of its pronouncement. For example, restricting in the market with few people present is ineffective.
This is summarized at the end of the passage.
5
Synthesize the overall rule
Combine the three scenarios and the dispute to articulate the complete ruling: the restriction must reach the same level of publicity as the original permission, and if the slave denies, the proof of notification follows the Sahibayn's easier view in practice.
The mufta bihi is the Sahibayn's position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بحجره إن علم به أكثر أهل سوقه، وبموت سيّده وجنونه ولحوقه مرتدا أي حجر المولى بالحجر
بالحجر
ر أيضاً ينحجر
بحجره إلخ بأن يقول : حجرتك عن التصرف، أو وصله خبر ،حجره، كذا في "الدرر"، وفي الإخبار لابد من العدد أو العدالة عند الإمام واكتفيا بالواحد مطلقا، وهذا إذا كذبه العبد، فإن صدقه يصير محجورا اتفاقا، ولو كان المخبر رسولا ينحجر في الوجهين اتفاقا؛ لأن عبارة الرسول كعبارة المرسل هذا في الإخبار بحجره، وأما الإخبار بإذنه فيكتفى فيه بالواحد اتفاقا. (فتح)
إن علم به إلخ : اعلم أن الإذن إما أن يكون علم به أهل السوق عامة، أو علم به رجل أو رجلان أو ثلاثة، أو لم يعلم به إلا العبد ففي الأول يشترط علم أهل السوق بالحجر أيضا، فلو حجر عليه في هذه الصورة بحضرة الأقل، لم ينحجر، حتى لو باعه من علم منهم ومن لم يعلم جاز؛ لأنه لما بقي مأذونا في حق من لم يعلم، فكذا في حق من علم أيضا؛ لأن الإذن لا يقبل التخصيص، وفي الثاني يشترط أن يكون الحجر بمحضر هؤلاء، ولا يصح بما دونه بأن يحجره في بيته، وفي الثالث يكون الحجر بمحضر من العبد، ولا يصح إذا كان الحجر في بيته بغير محضر منه. قال في "المحيط": وأصله أن الحجر الخاص لا يرد على الإذن العام ويرد على الإذن الخاص بأن أذن له بمحضر رجل أو رجلين أو ثلاثة فحجر بمحضر هؤلاء يصح، ولو رآه المولى يبيع ويشتري بعد ما حجر عليه قبل أن يعلم العبد، فلم ينهه، ثم علم العبد بالحجر يبقى مأذونا استحسانا ، ثم العبرة لشيوع الحجر واشتهاره، لا للحجر في السوق، حتى لو حجر في السوق، وليس فيه إلا رجل أو رجلان لا ينحجر، ولو باعوه جاز وإن باعه الذي علم بحجره، وإن حجر في بيته بمحضر من أهل السوق ينحجر، هذا حاصل ما في الشروح والحواشي.
By his placing a legal restriction [on the slave], if most of the people of his market know about it. And by the death of his master, his insanity, and his joining [Dar al-Harb] as an apostate. That is, the master's placing of a restriction: by the restriction, also [the slave] becomes restricted. [The master's] placing a restriction, etc., by saying: 'I place a restriction on you from transactions,' or news of his restriction reaches him. This is as in al-Durar. Regarding informing [the slave], a specific number or uprightness is required according to Imam [Abu Hanifa], while the two [Abu Yusuf and Muhammad] suffice with a single person absolutely. This is if the slave denies it. If he confirms it, he becomes restricted by agreement. And if the informant is a messenger, he becomes restricted in both views by agreement, because the statement of the messenger is like the statement of the sender. This is regarding informing about his restriction. As for informing about his permission, a single person suffices by agreement. (Fath) 'If he knows about it, etc.': Know that permission is either known to the people of the market generally, or known to one, two, or three men, or only the slave knows. In the first case, the knowledge of the people of the market of the restriction is also required. So if he places a restriction on him in this situation in the presence of a few, he does not become restricted. Even if he sells to one who knows and one who does not know, it is valid, because since he remains permitted in the right of the one who does not know, likewise in the right of the one who knows, because permission does not accept specification. In the second case, it is required that the restriction be in the presence of those [few], and it is not valid with less than that, such as if he restricts him in his house. In the third case, the restriction is in the presence of the slave, and it is not valid if the restriction is in his house without his presence. He said in al-Muhit: The principle is that a specific restriction does not override general permission, but it does override specific permission. For example, if he gave permission in the presence of one, two, or three men, then restricts in their presence, it is valid. And if the master sees him buying and selling after he placed a restriction before the slave knew, and he does not forbid him, then the slave knows of the restriction, he remains permitted by istihsan. Then the criterion is the spread and notoriety of the restriction, not the restriction in the market. So even if he restricts in the market, and there is only one or two men, he does not become restricted. And if they [those few] sell, it is valid even if the seller is the one who knows of his restriction. And if he restricts in his house in the presence of the people of the market, he becomes restricted. This is the summary of what is in the commentaries and glosses.
Source pages p. 246

Commentary
This passage is a detailed explanation of the conditions for the effectiveness of legal restriction (hajr) on a permitted slave (ma'dhun). The author distinguishes three scenarios based on how widely the original permission was known. If the permission was public knowledge (known to most of the market), then the restriction must also be publicly known to be effective; a private restriction does not work because permission cannot be selectively withdrawn. If the permission was known only to a specific few (one or two men), then the restriction must be enacted in their presence. If only the slave knew of the permission, then the restriction must be in his presence. The text also discusses the dispute between Imam Abu Hanifa and the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) regarding the number of witnesses needed when informing the slave of the restriction: Imam requires multiple witnesses or a single just witness, while the two suffice with one witness regardless. If the slave confirms the restriction, it is effective by consensus. The ultimate criterion is the spread and notoriety of the restriction, not the location of its pronouncement.

Key Terms
حجر (hajr): Legal restriction placed by the master on a permitted slave, prohibiting him from engaging in transactions.
مأذون (ma'dhun): A slave who has been given general permission by his master to engage in trade and transactions.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The restriction must be announced publicly in the market.
B) The restriction must be made in the presence of those specific men.
C) The restriction must be made in the presence of the slave alone.
D) The restriction is effective regardless of how it is made.

Key Takeaway
The effectiveness of a legal restriction on a permitted slave depends on matching the publicity of the original permission; an intra‑Hanafi dispute exists on witness requirements but the Sahibayn's view is preferred for ease.

Automatic Restriction of a Permitted Slave
Rulings on events that automatically restrict a permitted slave

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three events that automatically restrict a permitted slave and explain why the slave's knowledge is not required.

Core Idea
Death, continuous insanity, and apostasy automatically end a permitted slave's trading permission.

In Hanafi fiqh, a permitted slave (ma'dhun) enjoys a general permission to trade. However, certain events automatically terminate that permission, even if the slave is unaware. This lesson examines those events and the principle of constructive restriction (hajr hukmi).

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal concept
Recognize that 'hajr hukmi' (constructive restriction) operates automatically by law, not by the master's explicit action.
2
List the three causes
Memorize: (1) death of the master, (2) continuous insanity (of master or slave), (3) joining Dar al-Harb as an apostate.
Each cause removes either ownership or legal capacity.
3
Understand the automatic nature
Grasp that knowledge by the slave is irrelevant—restriction occurs instantly upon the event.
Compare with an agent's dismissal: the agent's authority ends upon the principal's death even without notice.
4
Clarify the condition for insanity
Note that only 'continuous' insanity (mutbiq) causes restriction; temporary illness or unconsciousness does not.
Continuous means lasting more than a year or without lucid intervals.
5
Apply to both master and slave
Recognize that the slave's own continuous insanity or apostasy also restricts him, and permission does not return.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبموت إلخ أي ينحجر المأذون أيضا بموت سيده وجنونه ولحوقه بدار الحرب حال كونه مرتدا، أما الموت؛ فلأنه يزيل الملك، وأما الجنون؛ فلأنه يزيل الأهلية، وأما اللحاق ؛ فلأنه موت حكما رمز الحقائق: ٣٢٠/٢] سواء علم العبد بذلك أو لم يعلم؛ لأنه حجر حكمي فلا يكون العلم من شرطه كانعزال الوكيل بهذه الأشياء، وكما لو أخرجه المولى عن ملكه وكالمفاوضة تبطل بملك أحدهما ما تصح فيه الشركة تصير عنانا وإن لم يملك أحدهما إبطالها، وفي الظهرية وموت الأب حجر على الصبي كموت وصي وعزله، ولو عزل قاض أذن لصبي ومأذون كانا على إذنهما. (من العيني والفتح) وجنونه إلخ : أي جنون المولى ويجوز جعل الضمير فيه للعبد ففي "البدائع": جنون العبد مطبقا ولحوقه بدار الحرب مرتدا من أسباب الانحجار؛ ولهذا قال عربي زادة لو قال وموت أحدهما وجنونه مطبقا لكان أتم وأخصر، وفي منية المفتي: وينحجر أيضا بجنون نفسه ولحوقه مرتدا ولا يعود الإذن فيه، ثم يشترط في الجنون أن يكون مطبقا، وإن لم يكن مطبقا كالمرض فلا ينحجر به وما دون السنة غير مطبق، كذا في "الذخيرة"، ولو أغمي عليه لا يصير محجورا من الفتح وملا (مسكين ولحوقه بدار الحرب حال كونه مرتدا، علم العبد بذلك في الصور الثلاث أو لم يعلم.
And by death, etc.: That is, the permitted slave also becomes restricted by the death of his master, his insanity, and his joining Dar al-Harb while being an apostate. As for death, because it removes ownership. As for insanity, because it removes legal capacity. As for joining [Dar al-Harb], because it is constructive death. (Ramz al-Haqa'iq 320/2) Whether the slave knows of that or not, because it is a constructive restriction, so knowledge is not a condition for it, just as an agent is removed by these things. And as if the master removed him from his ownership, and as in the case of partnership (mufawada) being invalidated by one of them owning something in which partnership is not valid, it becomes a limited partnership (`inan) even if neither has the right to nullify it. In al-Zahiriya: The death of a father is a restriction on the child, like the death of a guardian and his removal. And if a judge removes permission given to a child or a permitted [slave], they both remain on their permission. (From al-Ayni and al-Fath) 'And his insanity, etc.': That is, the master's insanity. And it is possible that the pronoun refers to the slave. In al-Bada'i': The continuous insanity of the slave and his joining Dar al-Harb as an apostate are among the causes of restriction. Therefore, Arabi Zada said: If he had said 'death of one of them and his continuous insanity' it would have been more complete and concise. In Munyat al-Mufti: He also becomes restricted by his own insanity and his joining [Dar al-Harb] as an apostate, and the permission does not return. Then the condition for insanity is that it be continuous. If it is not continuous, like illness, he does not become restricted by it. Less than a year is not continuous. This is in al-Dhakhira. And if he falls unconscious, he does not become restricted. From al-Fath and Mulla (Miskin). 'And his joining Dar al-Harb while being an apostate' - whether the slave knows of that in the three cases or not.
Source pages p. 246

Commentary
This passage explains the automatic causes of restriction (hajr hukmi) for a permitted slave. Three events immediately terminate the slave's permission to trade without requiring the slave's knowledge: (1) death of the master, because ownership ceases; (2) insanity of the master (or the slave, according to one view), because legal capacity (ahliyya) is removed; (3) the master's joining Dar al-Harb as an apostate, which is considered constructive death. The restriction is automatic (hukmi) and does not depend on the slave's awareness, similar to how an agent is removed by the death of the principal. There is also a discussion about the nature of insanity: it must be continuous (mutbiq) to cause restriction; a temporary illness or unconsciousness does not count. The text also notes that if the slave himself becomes insane or joins Dar al-Harb as an apostate, he becomes restricted. Once restriction occurs due to these causes, the permission does not return automatically.

Key Terms
حجر حكمي (hajr hukmi): A legal restriction that takes place by operation of law without requiring any action or knowledge by the parties.
مطبق (mutbiq): Continuous insanity, lasting for a prolonged period (more than a year, or without lucid intervals).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Death of the master
B) The master's temporary illness
C) The master's continuous insanity
D) The master's joining Dar al-Harb as an apostate

Key Takeaway
Death, continuous insanity, and apostasy (by joining Dar al-Harb) automatically restrict a permitted slave's permission to trade, even without the slave's knowledge.

Restriction by Absconding (Ibaq)
Inter-madhhab dispute on ibaq causing restriction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school holds that a permitted slave becomes constructively restricted upon absconding, and evaluate the opposing argument of Zufar and al-Shafi'i.

Core Idea
A slave's absconding indicates the master's displeasure, which constructively revokes permission.

This lesson examines the dispute over whether a permitted slave who absconds becomes restricted. The Hanafi position relies on indication (dalalah) and the weakness of istishab, while Zufar and al-Shafi'i maintain that absconding does not contradict continued permission. The key is understanding how dalalah can override explicit initial permission.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Hanafi ruling on ibaq
The Hanafi school states that a permitted slave becomes constructively restricted (mahjur alayhi hukman) upon absconding, regardless of public knowledge.
The term hukman indicates that restriction is not explicit but legally inferred.
2
Examine the opposing view
Zufar and al-Shafi'i argue that absconding does not trigger restriction because it does not contradict the initial permission; by qiyas, if initial permission to an absconding slave is valid, continuation should also be valid.
This is a qiyas jaliy (apparent analogy).
3
Trace the Hanafi proof
The Hanafi response uses istihsan: the master's displeasure with rebellion is a strong indication (dalalah) that he does not permit trade. This dalalah establishes restriction just as an explicit statement would.
Dalalah can override a weak presumption like istishab.
4
Analyze the rebuttal
The opponent objects that explicit permission initially should continue via istishab. The Hanafiyyoon reply that istishab is weak and the dalalah of displeasure outweighs it, especially since explicit permission does not guarantee continuation in the face of changed circumstances.
The ruling does not require new explicit revocation; the act of absconding is legally effective.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
Under the Hanafi view, all transactions after absconding are invalid without new permission; under the opposing view, they remain valid until the master explicitly restricts.
This practical difference affects the validity of contracts made by the absconding slave.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبالإباق والاستيلاد لا بالتدبير، وضمن بهما قيمتهما للغرماء، وإن أقر بعد .
أي إباق العبد المأذون بالجر عطف على الإباق
قيمة الأمة المستولدة والمدبرة
المأذون
وبالإباق: يعني بالإباق أيضا يصير محجورا عليه حكما علم أهل السوق بذلك أو لم يعلم، وقال زفر والشافعي: لا يصير محجورا عليه بالإباق؛ لأن الإباق لا ينافي ابتداء الإذن، ألا ترى أنه إذا أذن لعبده المحجور عليه الآبق صح، وجاز له أن طاعته عادة، إذا بلغه فلأن لا يمنع الإباق أولى ولنا أن المولى لم يرض بتصرف عبده المتمرد عن يتجر فصار محجورا عليه دلالة والحجر يثبت دلالة كالإذن تكملة البحر الرائق: ۱۹۳/۸] وهذا بخلاف ابتداء الإذن في الآبق؛ لأن الصريح يبطل الدلالة رمز الحقائق: ۳۲۰/۲] وإن قلت: إذا كان الصريح يبطل الدلالة فلا ينحجر العبد بالإباق حكما لوجود التصريح بالإذن في الابتداء. قلنا: إن وجود التصريح بالإذن في الابتداء لا يقتضي وجوده إلى حال الإباق، وإنما يعرف في البقاء باعتبار استصحاب الحال، وهي حجة ضعيفة، فيجوز أن تترجح الدلالة عليها، ولو عاد من الإباق فالصحيح أن الإذن لا يعود. ملتقط من العيني والتكملة باختصار) والاستيلاد يعني الأمة المأذون لها تصير محجورة باستيلاد المولى لها، وقال زفر لا تصير محجورا عليها به، وهو القياس؛ لأن المولى لو أذن لأم ولده ابتداء يجوز، فلأن لا يمنع الاستيلاد بقاء الإذن أولى، وبقول زفر قالت الثلاثة، وجه الاستحسان: أن العادة جرت بتحصين أمهات الأولاد، وأنه لا يرضى بخروجها، واختلاطها بالرجال في المعاملة
والتجارة، ودليل الحجر كصريحه، بخلاف ما لو أذن لأم ولده صريحا كما تقدم تكملة البحر الرائق: ١٩٤/٨] لا بالتدبير : يعني المأذون لها لا تصير محجورا عليها بالتدبير؛ لأن العادة لم تجر بتحصين المدبرة، ولم يوجد دليل الحجر، فيثبت على ما كانت؛ إذ لا تنافي بين حكمي التدبير والإذن؛ لأن حكم التدبير انعقاد حق الحرية في الحال، وحقيقة الحرية في المآل، وحق الإذن فك الحجر، وحق الحرية لا يمنع فك الحجر. تكملة البحر الرائق: ١٩٤/٨]
And by absconding and becoming an umm walad, but not by tadbir. And he [the master] is liable for their value to the creditors. 'And if he acknowledges after [restriction]...' That is, the absconding of the permitted slave (by a jar [construction] coordinating with absconding) the value of the umm walad and mudabbara of the permitted [slave]. 'And by absconding': That is, by absconding he also becomes restricted constructively, whether the people of the market know or not. Zufar and al-Shafi'i said: He does not become restricted by absconding, because absconding does not contradict the initial permission. Do you not see that if he gives permission to his restricted absconding slave, it is valid, and it is permissible for him because his obedience is customary? When it reaches him, then it is more appropriate that absconding does not prevent [continued permission]. Our [Hanafi] evidence: The master is not pleased with the transaction of his rebellious slave; thus he becomes restricted by indication (dalalah), and restriction is established by indication just like permission. (Takmilat al-Bahr al-Ra'iq 8/193) This is unlike initial permission for an absconding slave, because explicit statement invalidates indication. (Ramz al-Haqa'iq 2/320) And if you say: If explicit statement invalidates indication, then the slave should not become restricted by absconding because of the existence of explicit permission initially. We say: The existence of explicit permission initially does not necessitate its existence at the time of absconding. Rather, its continuation is known by istishab al-hal, which is a weak proof, so it is possible that the indication outweighs it. And if he returns from absconding, the correct view is that permission does not return. (Summarized from al-Ayni and al-Takmila) 'And istilad': That is, the permitted female slave becomes restricted by the master's making her an umm walad. Zufar said: She does not become restricted by it, and that is the analogy (qiyas), because if the master initially gave permission to his umm walad, it is valid, so it is more appropriate that istilad does not prevent the continuation of permission. The three [Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, Muhammad] said contrary to Zufar's statement. The basis of istihsan: Custom has established protecting mothers of children, and that the master is not pleased with her going out and mixing with men in transactions and trade. And the evidence of restriction is like its explicit statement, unlike if he explicitly gave permission to his umm walad as mentioned earlier. (Takmilat al-Bahr al-Ra'iq 8/194) 'Not by tadbir': That is, the permitted [female slave] does not become restricted by tadbir, because custom has not established protecting a mudabbara, and there is no evidence of restriction. So she remains as she was, because there is no contradiction between the rulings of tadbir and permission. The ruling of tadbir is the establishment of the right to freedom immediately, and the reality of freedom in the future; the right of permission is lifting the restriction, and the right to freedom does not prevent lifting the restriction. (Takmilat al-Bahr al-Ra'iq 8/194)
Source pages p. 247

Commentary
This passage covers three situations: absconding (ibaq), becoming an umm walad (istilad), and tadbir (declaration of freedom upon death). For absconding: The Hanafi position is that the slave becomes restricted constructively, even if the market does not know. Zufar (a Hanafi) and al-Shafi'i argue that absconding does not contradict continued permission, using qiyas that if initial permission to an absconding slave is valid, then it should remain. The Hanafi response uses istihsan (juristic preference) based on dalalah (indication): since the master is not pleased with a rebellious slave, restriction is indicated. The explicit permission initially is overridden by the indication of the master's displeasure, and istishab (presumption of continuity) is weak. Permissioin does not return upon return from absconding. For istilad: The Hanafi position (Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, Muhammad) is that the female slave becomes restricted because custom restricts umm waladat from trade. Zufar again uses qiyas to say it does not. The Hanafi response invokes istihsan based on custom. For tadbir: All Hanafis agree that tadbir does not cause restriction, because there is no custom or indication of restriction, and both rulings can coexist. The passage sets the stage for the next discussion about liability.

Key Terms
إباق (ibaq): A slave absconding from his master without permission.
دلالة (dalalah): Indication; a non-explicit sign that carries legal weight.
استصحاب الحال (istishab al-hal): Presumption of continuity of a state until a change is proven.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the effect of a permitted slave absconding?
A) The slave remains permitted until the master explicitly revokes permission.
B) The slave becomes constructively restricted because absconding indicates the master's displeasure.
C) The slave becomes restricted only if the master learns of the absconding.
D) The slave becomes free.

Key Takeaway
A permitted slave becomes constructively restricted upon absconding because dalalah of displeasure overrides the weak presumption of continued permission.

Restriction by Becoming Umm Walad (Istilad)
Inter-madhhab dispute on istilad causing restriction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school holds that a permitted female slave becomes restricted upon becoming an umm walad, and evaluate the opposing qiyas of Zufar.

Core Idea
Customary protection of umm waladat indicates restriction, overriding the qiyas of continued permission.

This lesson covers the dispute over whether a permitted female slave becomes restricted when her master makes her an umm walad (mother of his child). The Hanafi position uses istihsan based on custom, while Zufar argues by qiyas that she should remain permitted.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Hanafi ruling on istilad
The Hanafi school (Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, Muhammad) states that the permitted female slave becomes restricted upon becoming an umm walad.
This applies during the master's life; she becomes free upon his death.
2
Examine Zufar's opposing view
Zufar argues by qiyas: if initial permission to an umm walad is valid, then becoming one should not end the permission.
This is a qiyas jaliy (apparent analogy).
3
Understand the Hanafi istihsan
The Hanafi response uses istihsan based on custom (urf): it is customary to protect umm waladat from mixing with men in trade. The master is not pleased with her transacting.
This custom serves as evidence of restriction just like an explicit statement.
4
Contrast with explicit permission
If the master explicitly gives permission to his umm walad, that explicit permission overrides the implied restriction, as noted earlier.
This distinction is key: dalalah of restriction is overridden by explicit permission.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
Under Hanafi, her transactions after istilad are invalid without new permission; under Zufar, they remain valid until explicit restriction.
This protects the umm walad from exploitation.

والتجارة، ودليل الحجر كصريحه، بخلاف ما لو أذن لأم ولده صريحا كما تقدم تكملة البحر الرائق: ١٩٤/٨] لا بالتدبير : يعني المأذون لها لا تصير محجورا عليها بالتدبير؛ لأن العادة لم تجر بتحصين المدبرة، ولم يوجد دليل الحجر، فيثبت على ما كانت؛ إذ لا تنافي بين حكمي التدبير والإذن؛ لأن حكم التدبير انعقاد حق الحرية في الحال، وحقيقة الحرية في المآل، وحق الإذن فك الحجر، وحق الحرية لا يمنع فك الحجر. تكملة البحر الرائق: ١٩٤/٨]
And by absconding and becoming an umm walad, but not by tadbir. And he [the master] is liable for their value to the creditors. 'And if he acknowledges after [restriction]...' That is, the absconding of the permitted slave (by a jar [construction] coordinating with absconding) the value of the umm walad and mudabbara of the permitted [slave]. 'And by absconding': That is, by absconding he also becomes restricted constructively, whether the people of the market know or not. Zufar and al-Shafi'i said: He does not become restricted by absconding, because absconding does not contradict the initial permission. Do you not see that if he gives permission to his restricted absconding slave, it is valid, and it is permissible for him because his obedience is customary? When it reaches him, then it is more appropriate that absconding does not prevent [continued permission]. Our [Hanafi] evidence: The master is not pleased with the transaction of his rebellious slave; thus he becomes restricted by indication (dalalah), and restriction is established by indication just like permission. (Takmilat al-Bahr al-Ra'iq 8/193) This is unlike initial permission for an absconding slave, because explicit statement invalidates indication. (Ramz al-Haqa'iq 2/320) And if you say: If explicit statement invalidates indication, then the slave should not become restricted by absconding because of the existence of explicit permission initially. We say: The existence of explicit permission initially does not necessitate its existence at the time of absconding. Rather, its continuation is known by istishab al-hal, which is a weak proof, so it is possible that the indication outweighs it. And if he returns from absconding, the correct view is that permission does not return. (Summarized from al-Ayni and al-Takmila) 'And istilad': That is, the permitted female slave becomes restricted by the master's making her an umm walad. Zufar said: She does not become restricted by it, and that is the analogy (qiyas), because if the master initially gave permission to his umm walad, it is valid, so it is more appropriate that istilad does not prevent the continuation of permission. The three [Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, Muhammad] said contrary to Zufar's statement. The basis of istihsan: Custom has established protecting mothers of children, and that the master is not pleased with her going out and mixing with men in transactions and trade. (Takmilat al-Bahr al-Ra'iq 8/194) 'Not by tadbir': That is, the permitted [female slave] does not become restricted by tadbir, because custom has not established protecting a mudabbara, and there is no evidence of restriction. So she remains as she was, because there is no contradiction between the rulings of tadbir and permission. The ruling of tadbir is the establishment of the right to freedom immediately, and the reality of freedom in the future; the right of permission is lifting the restriction, and the right to freedom does not prevent lifting the restriction. (Takmilat al-Bahr al-Ra'iq 8/194)

أم ولد (umm walad): A female slave who has borne a child to her master.

No Restriction by Tadbir
Ruling on tadbir and its non-effect on permission.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school holds that tadbir (declaration of freedom upon death) does not cause restriction of a permitted slave.

Core Idea
Tadbir and permission are compatible; tadbir does not indicate restriction, so permission remains.

This lesson explains why tadbir does not restrict a permitted slave, in contrast to ibaq and istilad. The passage clarifies that there is no custom or evidence indicating restriction, and the two rulings (tadbir and permission) can coexist harmoniously.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling on tadbir
The Hanafi school states that tadbir does not cause restriction for a permitted female slave.
This is a clear ruling contrasting with ibaq and istilad.
2
Understand the reason: no custom
Custom has not established protecting mudabbarat (female slaves promised freedom) like it has for umm waladat.
Custom is the key differentiating factor.
3
Understand the reason: no evidence of restriction
There is no dalil (evidence) indicating that the master is displeased with her trading. Tadbir does not imply restriction.
Unlike ibaq where displeasure is indicated.
4
Analyze compatibility of rulings
The ruling of tadbir (right to freedom now, actual freedom later) and the ruling of permission (lifting of restriction) are not contradictory. The right to freedom does not prevent lifting restriction.
Both can coexist without conflict.
5
Contrast with istilad
In istilad, custom indicates restriction; in tadbir, it does not. Thus the slave remains permitted.
This contrast reinforces the role of custom and indication.

Master's Liability for Value of Umm Walad and Mudabbara to Creditors
Ruling on master's liability for unsellable slaves

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the master's liability when his action (tadbir or istilad) renders a slave unsellable to creditors, and identify the creditors' two options.

Core Idea
A master who makes his slave unsellable must guarantee its value to creditors, who may alternatively demand the slave work.

In this lesson, we examine a ruling from the Hanafi school concerning a master who has a slave with debts. If the master makes the slave an umm walad (by bearing his child) or gives a tadbir declaration (promising freedom after his death), the slave becomes unsellable. Since the creditors could only recover their debt through sale of the slave, the master's action effectively destroys their security. The Hanafi school holds that the master must therefore guarantee the slave's value, but the creditors have a choice: they can claim from the master, or they can require the slave to work to pay off the debt.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Act and Its Ruling
What does the master do? He makes his slave an umm walad or gives a tadbir. What is the ruling? The master becomes liable for the slave's value to the creditors. Determine the hukm level: it is wajib (obligatory) because the master caused loss.
2
Understand the Legal Cause (Ta'lil)
The text gives the reason: the master's action prevented the sale of the slave, and sale is the means by which creditors recover their debts. Thus the master 'destroyed' their right. This rationale (illah) grounds the liability.
The illah is that the master's action made the slave unsellable, which is a form of damage (itlaf) to the creditors' security.
3
List the Creditors' Options
The passage from al-Muhit states two options: (1) take the slave's value from the master – after which they have no further claim on the slave until he is freed; (2) forgo the master's guarantee and instead demand that the slave work (istis'a) to pay off the entire debt.
Istis'a means the slave is required to earn money through labour to repay the debt.
4
Note the Time of Freedom
If the creditors choose the first option and take the value from the master, they have no recourse against the slave until he is freed (at death of master for mudabbara, or at master's death for umm walad). At that point, if the slave still owes any remaining debt, he may be required to work.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وضمن بهما إلخ يعني ضمن المولى بالتدبير والاستيلاد قيمتهما للغرماء لو كان عليهما دين؛ لأنه أتلف بالتدبير والاستيلاد تعلق حق الغرماء؛ لأنه بفعله امتنع بيعهما، وبالبيع يقضي حقوقهم، قال في "المحيط": فإذا ضمن المولى القيمة لا سبيل لهم على العبد حتى يعتق، وإن شاؤوا لم يضمنوا المولى القيمة واستسعوا العبد في جميع دينهم. تكملة البحر الرائق: ١٩٥/٨]
And he is liable with them, etc.: That is, the master is liable by tadbir and istilad for their value to the creditors if there is a debt on them [the slaves], because he caused damage by tadbir and istilad, and the right of the creditors became attached, because by his action their sale became prevented, and by sale their rights are satisfied. He said in al-Muhit: If the master guarantees the value, the creditors have no recourse against the slave until he is freed. And if they wish, they may not claim the value from the master and instead demand the slave work (yusta'sa) for their entire debt. (Takmilat al-Bahr al-Ra'iq 8/195)
Source pages p. 247

Commentary
This passage explains the master's financial liability when he makes a permitted slave (who has debts) unsellable by either making her an umm walad (istilad) or by giving a tadbir declaration. By doing so, the master prevents the creditors from recovering their debts through sale of the slave. Therefore, the master becomes liable for the slave's value up to the debt amount. However, the creditors have an option: they can either take the value from the master, after which they have no claim on the slave until he is freed (at which point the slave might still owe if the value is less), or they can refuse to take the master's guarantee and instead demand that the slave work to pay off the debt (istis'a). This ruling is from al-Muhit and the Takmila.

Key Terms
استسعاء (istis'a): The process of a slave working to earn money to pay off his debts, used when the master cannot or will not pay.
تدبير (tadbir): A declaration by the master that a slave will be freed upon the master's death.
استيلاد (istilad): A female slave becoming an umm walad by bearing her master's child.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi ruling in this passage, what is the consequence if a master makes his mudabbara (who has debts) and the creditors choose to claim the value from the master?
A) The creditors can still pursue the slave for the full debt.
B) The creditors have no recourse against the slave until he is freed.
C) The slave must immediately work to pay the master back.
D) The master must also pay the slave's future wages.

Key Takeaway
A master who makes his slave unsellable (via tadbir or istilad) must guarantee its value to creditors, who may alternatively demand the slave work for the debt.

Acknowledgment by a Restricted Slave: The Intra-Hanafi Dispute on Possession (Yad)
Intra-Hanafi dispute on the validity of a restricted slave's acknowledgment

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the positions of Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn on a restricted slave's acknowledgment and identify the underlying dispute over possession (yad).

Core Idea
A restricted slave's acknowledgment is valid because possession remains real — Abu Hanifa's view vs. the Sahibayn's qiyas.

This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq delves into a subtle intra-Hanafi disagreement. When a permitted slave is later restricted, can he still make a binding acknowledgment of a debt or property? The answer depends on whether his possession (yad) is considered effective after restriction. Imam Abu Hanifa argues that it remains effective in reality, while his two disciples, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad, hold that restriction nullifies it. Understanding this ikhtilaf sharpens your ability to trace how different premises about possession yield different rulings.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
We have a permitted slave who was given permission to trade, then restricted. After restriction, he makes an acknowledgment (iqrar) of a debt, trust, or usurped property. The question: is this acknowledgment valid?
The restriction removes the master's permission, but the slave may still hold property in his hand.
2
State the two positions
Abu Hanifa says the acknowledgment is valid (sahih). Abu Yusuf and Muhammad say it is invalid (batil). The latter is described as qiyas (analogy).
3
Identify the validator
The Sahibayn argue that the validator (musahhih) of the acknowledgment was either permission (which is gone) or possession (which restriction nullifies). Abu Hanifa holds that the validator is possession, which remains in reality.
Thus the dispute centers on the effect of restriction on possession.
4
Analyze the proofs
For Abu Hanifa: possession remains haqiqa (real), and restriction only constructively nullifies it if the property exceeds the slave's needs. The acknowledgment itself proves the property exists in his hand. For the Sahibayn: restriction renders the slave's possession like that of a restricted free person—legally ineffective.
The Sahibayn's position is qiyas; Abu Hanifa's may be seen as istihsan based on reality.
5
Note the agreed exception
If the property in the slave's hand was acquired by gathering firewood (ihtitab) or similar simple acquisition, the acknowledgment is invalid by consensus. This is because such property is not considered stable possession.
This exception shows that the dispute only applies when the slave's hand contains assets from trade or similar.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن أقر بعد إلخ: أي إن أقر المأذون له بعد حجره بما في يده لأحد صح إقراره عند أبي حنيفة، سواء أقر بأنه أو غصب، أو أقر بدين، وقالا : لا يصح إقراره، وهو القياس؛ لأن مصحح إقراره إن كان الإذن فقد زال بالحجر، وإن كان اليد فالحجر أبطلها؛ لأن يد المحجور عليه غير معتبر وبه قالت الثلاثة، وله: أن المصحح هو اليد. رمز الحقائق: ٣٦٠/٢] ولهذا لا يصح إقراره قبل الحجر فيما أخذه المولى من يده، واليد باقية حقيقة، وشرط بطلانها بالحجر حكما فراغ ما في يده من الأكساب عن حاجته، أو إقراره دليل تحققها، ثم هذا الإقرار معتبر إذا لم يكن ما في يده حصل بمثل احتطاب حتى لو كان في يده مال حصل بالاحتطاب ونحوه لا يصدق في إقراره لأحد بالاتفاق ملتقط من العيني والفتح باختصار
And if he acknowledges after, etc.: That is, if the permitted [slave] after his restriction acknowledges something in his hand to someone, his acknowledgment is valid (sahih) according to Abu Hanifa, whether he acknowledges it as a trust (amana) or as usurped property (ghasb), or acknowledges a debt. The two [Abu Yusuf and Muhammad] say: His acknowledgment is not valid, and that is the analogy (qiyas), because if the validator of his acknowledgment was the permission, it has been removed by restriction; and if it was the possession (yad), then restriction invalidates it, because the possession of a restricted person is not considered. And the three [Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, Muhammad? Actually 'the three' here likely refers to the Sahibayn and another? Or 'the three' means the three imams besides Abu Hanifa? The text says 'wa bihi qaalat al-thalatha' – possibly meaning the three (Abu Yusuf, Muhammad, and Zufar or Shafi'i?) The context: 'and with it spoke the three' meaning they agree with the qiyas. But then 'wa lahu' refers to Abu Hanifa. So the dispute: Abu Hanifa vs. the others. He [Abu Hanifa] says: The validator is the possession (yad). (Ramz al-Haqa'iq 2/360) For this reason, his acknowledgment before restriction is not valid regarding what the master has taken from his hand. The possession (yad) remains in reality (haqiqa), and the condition of its invalidation by restriction constructively is that the earnings in his hand be free from his need, or his acknowledgment is evidence of its realization. Then this acknowledgment is considered if what is in his hand was not acquired by something like gathering firewood (ihtitab). So if there is wealth in his hand acquired by gathering firewood and the like, he is not believed in his acknowledgment to anyone by agreement. (Summarized from al-Ayni and al-Fath)
Source pages p. 247

Commentary
This passage deals with the validity of a financial acknowledgment (iqrar) made by a permitted slave after he has been restricted. The dispute is between Imam Abu Hanifa and the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad). The two argue that since the slave's permission has ended, his acknowledgment is invalid; also his possession (yad) is nullified by the restriction. Imam Abu Hanifa holds that the possession remains in reality (haqiqa) and is the basis for the acknowledgment. The restriction only constructively nullifies the possession if the earnings exceed his needs, but the acknowledgment itself proves that the possession is still intact. However, there is an exception: if the wealth in his hand was acquired through simple gathering (e.g., firewood), then the acknowledgment is not valid by consensus because such wealth is not considered stable possession. The passage also notes that even before restriction, an acknowledgment regarding property taken by the master is invalid, showing that the master's dominion overrides.

Key Terms
إقرار (iqrar): A legal acknowledgment of a right or debt against oneself, which is binding if made by a competent person.
يد (yad): Possession; physical control over property. In Hanafi fiqh, yad is a basis for ownership claims and valid transactions.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
The validity of a restricted slave's acknowledgment hinges on whether possession (yad) remains effective after restriction, leading to a legitimate ikhtilaf between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn.

Hijr of the Ma'dhun by Ibaq and Istilad
Ruling on hijr of the permitted slave by ibaq and istilad

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on hijr of the ma'dhun by ibaq and istilad, distinguish it from tadbir, and evaluate the proofs for each case.

Core Idea
Hijr of the ma'dhun is established by indication of the master's displeasure in ibaq and by custom in istilad, but tadbir does not conflict with permission.

This lesson examines when a ma'dhun (a slave permitted to trade) becomes legally interdicted (mahjur). The Hanafi school holds that running away (ibaq) and the master begetting a child from a female slave (istilad) cause hijr by indication or custom, while a promise of freedom after death (tadbir) does not. The passage presents rational proofs and addresses objections from Zufar and al-Shafi'i, as well as an intra-Hanafi disagreement on istilad. Understanding this ruling clarifies how implicit revocation of permission operates in Islamic commercial law.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three conditions and their rulings
Note the three scenarios: ibaq (running away), istilad (master fathering child from female slave), and tadbir (promise of freedom after death). The Hanafi ruling: hijr occurs for ibaq and istilad, but not for tadbir.
Also note the master's liability for value when hijr occurs due to ibaq or istilad.
2
Analyze the proof for hijr by ibaq
The Hanafi proof: the master's displeasure with the rebellious slave indicates he no longer consents to trading; hijr by indication is valid similar to permission by indication. Contrast with Zufar/Shafi'i who argue that ibaq does not contradict initial explicit permission.
The Hanafi rebuttal: initial permission is explicit, but its continuation relies on istishab al-hal (presumption of continuity), a weak proof that can be overridden by the stronger indication of displeasure.
3
Analyze the proof for hijr by istilad
Abu Hanifa uses istihsan based on custom that slave mothers are secluded, so the master would not consent to her trading. Zufar and the three (including the Sahibayn) follow qiyas: if initial permission to an umm walad is valid, then istilad should not prevent continuation.
The istihsan view is mufta bihi in the Hanafi school.
4
Understand why tadbir does not cause hijr
Tadbir does not conflict with permission; it grants a future right to freedom but does not indicate seclusion or displeasure. Custom does not seclude mudabbaras, and there is no indication of hijr.
The master's promise of freedom does not negate the permission to trade.
5
Master's liability and creditor options
When hijr occurs by ibaq or istilad, the master must guarantee the slave's value to creditors if there is debt. Creditors may either claim the value from the master or seek earnings from the slave until the debt is paid.
This protects creditors' rights when the slave can no longer be sold due to the master's actions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

حكما علم أهل السوق بذلك أو لم يعلم، وقال زفر والشافعي: لا يصير محجورا عليه بالإباق؛ لأن الإباق لا ينافي ابتداء الإذن، ألا ترى أنه إذا أذن لعبده المحجور عليه الآبق صح، وجاز له أن طاعته عادة، إذا بلغه فلأن لا يمنع الإباق أولى ولنا أن المولى لم يرض بتصرف عبده المتمرد عن يتجر فصار محجورا عليه دلالة والحجر يثبت دلالة كالإذن تكملة البحر الرائق: ١٩٣/٨] وهذا بخلاف ابتداء الإذن في الآبق؛ لأن الصريح يبطل تصير محجورة باستيلاد المولى لها، وقال زفر لا تصير محجورا عليها به، وهو القياس؛ لأن المولى لو أذن لأم ولده ابتداء يجوز، فلأن لا يمنع الاستيلاد بقاء الإذن أولى، وبقول زفر قالت الثلاثة، وجه الاستحسان: أن
