المأذون لها لا تصير محجورا عليها بالتدبير؛ لأن العادة لم تجر بتحصين المدبرة، ولم يوجد دليل الحجر، فيثبت على ما كانت؛ إذ لا تنافي بين حكمي التدبير والإذن؛ لأن حكم التدبير انعقاد حق الحرية في الحال، وحقيقة الحرية في المآل، وحق الإذن فك الحجر، وحق الحرية لا يمنع فك الحجر. تكملة البحر الرائق: ١٩٤/٨] وضمن بهما إلخ يعني ضمن المولى بالتدبير والاستيلاد قيمتهما للغرماء لو كان عليهما دين؛ لأنه أتلف بالتدبير والاستيلاد تعلق حق الغرماء؛ لأنه بفعله امتنع بيعهما، وبالبيع يقضي حقوقهم، قال في "المحيط": فإذا ضمن المولى القيمة لا سبيل لهم على العبد حتى يعتق، وإن شاؤوا لم يضمنوا المولى القيمة واستسعوا العبد في جميع دينهم. تكملة البحر الرائق: ١٩٥/٨]
And by running away and childbearing [hijr occurs], not by tadbir; and he [the master] guarantees their value to the creditors. And by running away: meaning by running away he also becomes interdicted legally whether the people of the market know that or not. Zufar and al-Shafi'i said: He does not become interdicted by running away because running away does not contradict initial permission. Do you not see that if he gave permission to his interdicted runaway slave it is valid, and it is permissible for him that his obedience is customary; when it reaches him, then running away not preventing is more appropriate. Our evidence: the master did not consent to the transaction of his rebel slave, so he becomes interdicted by indication, and interdiction is established by indication like permission. This differs from initial permission for the runaway because explicit statement nullifies indication. If you say: if explicit statement nullifies indication, then the slave should not be interdicted by running away due to the existence of explicit permission initially. We reply: The existence of explicit permission initially does not necessitate its existence at the time of running away; it is known in continuance by considering the continuation of the state, which is a weak proof, so it is possible for the indication to outweigh it. If he returns from running away, the correct view is that permission does not return. (Summarized from al-‘Ayni and al-Takmila) And childbearing: meaning the permitted slave woman becomes interdicted by her master's begetting a child from her. Zufar said she does not become interdicted by it, and that is by qiyas because if the master initially gave permission to his umm walad it is valid, thus not preventing childbearing from remaining permission is more appropriate. The three agreed with Zufar's statement. The istihsan reasoning: custom has established the seclusion of mothers of children, and that he is not pleased with her going out and mixing with men in transaction and trade, and the indication of interdiction is like its explicit statement. This differs from if he explicitly gave permission to his umm walad as previously mentioned. Not by tadbir: meaning the permitted slave woman does not become interdicted by tadbir because custom has not established the seclusion of the mudabbara, and there is no indication of interdiction, so she remains as she was; there is no contradiction between the rules of tadbir and permission because the rule of tadbir is the inception of the right to freedom in the present and actual freedom in the future, and the right of permission is the removal of interdiction, and the right to freedom does not prevent removal of interdiction. (al-Bahr al-Ra’iq) And he guarantees by both etc.: meaning the master guarantees by tadbir and childbearing their value to the creditors if they had a debt because he destroyed [the creditors' right] by tadbir and childbearing since the creditors' right became attached; because by his act their sale became prevented, and by sale they would satisfy their rights. It is said in al-Muhit: So if the master guarantees the value, the creditors have no claim against the slave until he is freed, and if they wish they do not hold the master liable for the value and they seek earning from the slave for all their debt.
Source pages p. 247

Commentary
This passage discusses the circumstances under which a ma'dhun (a slave permitted to trade) becomes interdicted and the master's liability. The text first states that the ma'dhun becomes interdicted (mahjur) by running away (ibaq) and by the master begetting a child from her (istilad), but not by tadbir (promise of freedom after master's death). The passage then provides proof and addresses objections. For ibaq, the Hanafi position is that hijr occurs regardless of public knowledge, while Zufar and al-Shafi'i argue it does not because ibaq does not contradict initial permission. The Hanafi rebuttal clarifies that permission is not ongoing during ibaq; continuation depends on presumption of continuance (istishab al-hal), which is weak, and the indication from the master's displeasure overrides it. For istilad, Abu Hanifa uses istihsan based on custom that slave mothers are secluded, against the qiyas of Zufar and the three (Abu Yusuf, Muhammad, and others) who analogize to initial permission. For tadbir, no hijr occurs because tadbir does not conflict with permission. Finally, the master is liable for the value of the slave when he causes the loss through tadbir or istilad, because his act prevents sale to satisfy creditors. The creditors may choose to claim from the master or seek earnings from the slave.

Key Terms
المأذون (al-ma'dhun): A slave who has been granted permission by his master to engage in trade.
الإباق (al-ibaq): The act of a slave running away from his master.
الاستيلاد (al-istilad): The master begetting a child from his female slave, making her an umm walad.
التدبير (al-tadbir): A promise by the master to free the slave after the master's death.
الحجر (al-hajr): Legal interdiction restricting a person's capacity to act in financial matters.

Iqrar of the Ma'dhun after Hijr: Abu Hanifa vs. Sahibayn
Intra-Hanafi dispute on iqrar after hijr

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the intra-Hanafi dispute on the validity of a ma'dhun's confession after hijr and identify the basis (yad vs. idhn) that determines the ruling.

Core Idea
The ma'dhun's hand (yad) remains valid for iqrar even after hijr if need is not yet fulfilled.

This lesson explores a nuanced dispute within the Hanafi school regarding the legal capacity of a ma'dhun (a slave permitted to trade) after he is interdicted. The key question: can his confession (iqrar) regarding property in his possession be binding? Abu Hanifa holds that the physical hand (yad) remains the basis, while the Sahibayn argue that both permission and hand are invalidated by hijr.

Guided Path
1
Identify the parties
The dispute is between Abu Hanifa on one side and the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani) along with three others (including Zufar) on the other.
2
State each position
Abu Hanifa: the iqrar is valid (sahih). The Sahibayn: the iqrar is invalid (batil).
3
Identify the basis for each
Abu Hanifa says the corrector (musahhih) is the physical hand (yad). The Sahibayn say the basis could be permission (idhn) or hand, but both are nullified by hijr.
4
Analyze Abu Hanifa's evidence
The hand remains in reality; its legal nullification is conditional on the earnings exceeding need. The confession itself indicates that need is not yet met, so the hand remains effective.
The text cites Ramoos al-Haqaiq for this reasoning.
5
Note the exception
If the property was obtained by mere gathering (ihtitab, e.g., woodcutting), then the iqrar is invalid by consensus (al-ittifaq).
This shows that hand obtained without trade is not a valid basis for iqrar.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن أقر بعد إلخ: أي إن أقر المأذون له بعد حجره بما في يده لأحد صح إقراره عند أبي حنيفة، سواء أقر بأنه أو غصب، أو أقر بدين، وقالا : لا يصح إقراره، وهو القياس؛ لأن مصحح إقراره إن كان الإذن فقد زال بالحجر، وإن كان اليد فالحجر أبطلها؛ لأن يد المحجور عليه غير معتبر وبه قالت الثلاثة، وله: أن المصحح هو اليد. رمز الحقائق: ٣٦٠/٢] ولهذا لا يصح إقراره قبل الحجر فيما أخذه المولى من يده، واليد باقية حقيقة، وشرط بطلانها بالحجر حكما فراغ ما في يده من الأكساب عن حاجته، أو إقراره دليل تحققها، ثم هذا الإقرار معتبر إذا لم يكن ما في يده حصل بمثل احتطاب حتى لو كان في يده مال حصل بالاحتطاب ونحوه لا يصدق في إقراره لأحد بالاتفاق ملتقط من العيني والفتح باختصار
And if he confesses after [hijr] etc.: meaning if the ma'dhun after his hijr confesses regarding what is in his hand to someone, his confession is valid according to Abu Hanifa, whether he confesses to it being or usurpation, or confesses to a debt. And the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) said: his confession is not valid, and that is the qiyas, because the corrector of his confession, if it is the permission, has been removed by hijr; and if it is the hand, then hijr has nullified it, because the hand of the interdicted person is not considered. And the three said the same. And his (Abu Hanifa's) evidence: that the corrector is the hand. (Ramoos al-Haqaiq) For this reason, his confession before hijr regarding what the master took from his hand is not valid. The hand remains in reality, and the condition for its legal nullification by hijr is that what is in his hand from earnings is beyond his need, or his confession is proof of its verification. Then this confession is considered if what is in his hand was not obtained by something like woodcutting; for example, if he had money obtained by woodcutting and the like, his confession is not believed by agreement. (Summarized from al-‘Ayni and al-Fath)
Source pages p. 247

Commentary
This passage discusses the validity of a ma'dhun's confession (iqrar) after he has been interdicted. According to Abu Hanifa, the confession is valid because the valid basis is the physical hand (yad) of the slave, which remains in reality even after hijr. The hand's nullification by hijr is only legal and conditional on the earnings exceeding his need. The two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) argue that either permission or hand is the basis; both are invalidated by hijr, so the confession is invalid. The three (including Zufar) agree with the Sahibayn. Abu Hanifa's evidence is that the hand is the corrector, as seen by the fact that the slave's confession before hijr regarding what the master took from his hand is invalid. The confession is only valid if the property in hand was not obtained by mere collection (ihtitab), such as gathering firewood—if it was, the confession is unanimously not accepted.

Key Terms
الإقرار (al-iqrar): Confession or admission of a right or debt.
اليد (al-yad): Physical possession or control over property.
الحجر (al-hajr): Legal interdiction restricting capacity.
الاحتطاب (al-ihtitab): Gathering firewood, metaphor for property obtained through mere personal effort without trade.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
The ma'dhun's iqrar after hijr is valid based on his physical hand, which remains unless his earnings exceed need.

Master's Ownership of Ma'dhun's Earnings When Debts Encompass
Intra-Hanafi dispute on master's ownership of ma'dhun's earnings when debts encompass both property and person.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why Abu Hanifa holds that the master does not own the ma'dhun's earnings when debts encompass both property and person, and contrast this with the Sahibayn's view, identifying the practical consequence for emancipation.

Core Idea
When debts encompass the ma'dhun's property and person, earnings are occupied by need, so the master does not own them.

This lesson explores a key intra-Hanafi dispute: when a trading slave (ma'dhun) is burdened with debts that cover not only his property but also his personal value (raqaba), does his master still own his earnings? Abu Hanifa says no because the earnings are 'occupied by need' (mashghul bi-hajah), while the Sahibayn say yes because ownership of the body is the cause for ownership of earnings. We will analyse the proofs and the practical ruling regarding emancipation from such earnings.

Guided Path
1
Identify the condition and the act
The passage describes a ma'dhun whose debts encompass both his property (mal) and his person (raqaba). The act in question is the master's ownership of the ma'dhun's earnings and, as a corollary, the master's emancipation of a slave from those earnings.
Ihatah (encompassment) means the debts cover the entire value of property and the slave's worth.
2
State Abu Hanifa's position and proof
Abu Hanifa rules that the master does not own the earnings. His proof: the master's ownership of earnings is conditional on the earnings being free from need (hajah). Since the debts occupy the earnings for repayment, they are mashghul bi-hajah (occupied by need), so the master does not own them.
The 'need' here refers to the debt that must be satisfied first – it is a prior claim.
3
State the Sahibayn's position and proof
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad hold that the master does own the earnings. Their proof: ownership of the body (raqaba) is the cause (sabab) for ownership of the hand's earnings. Debts encompassed do not remove the slave from the master's ownership, just as the master retains the right to intercourse (wat') with the female slave.
They analogize to other rights of ownership that persist despite debts.
4
Analyze the Hanafi rebuttal
Abu Hanifa's response is based on the specific nature of earnings: they are for meeting the slave's needs and debts. Although the body is owned, the earnings are not free for the master to take because they are already 'occupied' by the debt (hajah). Ownership of body alone does not automatically give ownership of earnings when a need intervenes.
This is a tafriq (distinction) based on the concept of hajah.
5
Determine the practical consequence
If the master frees a slave from these earnings, Abu Hanifa says the emancipation is invalid (batil) because he is freeing what he does not own. The Sahibayn say it is valid (nafidh) but the master must pay the slave's value (qimah) to the creditors. The passage also notes that if emancipation is invalid, other dispositions are even more certainly invalid (awla).
Thamarah al-khilaf: the ruling on the master's disposal changes based on ownership.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
حجره بما في يده صح، ولم يملك سيّده ما في يده لو أحاط دينه بماله ورقبته، فيبطل تحريره من المال عند أبي حنيفة . أي يد المأذون أي دين المأذون أي رقبة المأذون كسبه، ولم يملك سيده إلخ: أي إذا لزم المأذون ديون تحيط بماله ورقبته لم يملك سيده ما في يده، وهذا عند الإمام، وقالا : يملك ذلك؛ لأن ملك الرقبة سبب لملك كسب اليد، واستغراقها بالدين لا يوجب خروج المأذون عن ملكه؛ ولهذا ملك وطء المأذونة، ولأبي حنيفة أن ملك المولى إنما يثبت في كسب العبد التاجر عند فراغه عن حاجته والمحيط به الدين مشغول بحاجته فلا يملك تكملة البحر الرائق: ١٩٦/٨] فيبطل تحريره إلخ [تفريع على عدم ملك المولى ما في يده] أي لما لم يملك السيد ما في يد المأذون إذا كانت عليه ديون محيطة بماله ورقبته بطل تحرير اليد عبدا من كسب المأذون؛ لأنه أعتق ما لا يملكه، وهذا عند الإمام، وعندهما ينفذ عتقه، ويغرم قيمته للغرماء، ولما كان العتق أقوى نفاذا صرح به ليفيد أن تصرف المولى في غيره باطل بالطريق الأولى من العيني والتكملة باختصار
His hijr regarding what is in his hand is valid, and his master does not own what is in his hand if his debt encompasses his property and his person, so his emancipation [of a slave] from the property is invalid according to Abu Hanifa. [Commentary]: i.e. the hand of the ma'dhun, i.e. the debt of the ma'dhun, i.e. the person of the ma'dhun, his earnings. And 'his master does not own' etc.: meaning if debts become obligatory on the ma'dhun that encompass his property and his person, his master does not own what is in his hand. This is according to the Imam (Abu Hanifa). And the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) said: he owns that, because ownership of the person is a cause for ownership of the earnings of the hand, and their being completely covered by debt does not necessitate the ma'dhun exiting from his ownership; for this reason he (the master) is permitted to have intercourse with the ma'dhuna. Abu Hanifa's evidence: the master's ownership is only established in the earnings of the trading slave when it is free from his need, and what is encompassed by debt is occupied with his need, so he does not own it. So his emancipation is invalid, etc. [a branch on the lack of master's ownership of what is in his hand]: meaning since the master does not own what is in the hand of the ma'dhun when there are debts encompassing his property and person, the emancipation of a slave from the ma'dhun's earnings is invalid, because he freed what he does not own. This is according to the Imam. And according to them (the two), his emancipation is executed and he pays its value to the creditors. And because emancipation is stronger in effect, it is mentioned to indicate that the master's disposal in other things is void with stronger reason. (Summarized from al-‘Ayni and al-Takmila)
Source pages p. 248

Commentary
This passage addresses the master's ownership of the ma'dhun's earnings when debts encompass both the property and the person of the slave (i.e., the slave's value is also consumed by debt). According to Abu Hanifa, the master does not own these earnings because they are 'occupied by need' (the debt is a need that must be satisfied first). Therefore, if the master emancipates a slave from such earnings, the emancipation is invalid because he is freeing what he does not own. The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) argue that the master owns the earnings because his ownership of the slave's person causes ownership of earnings; debt does not remove the slave from his ownership. Hence, according to them, the emancipation is valid and the master must pay the value to the creditors. The author then notes that if emancipation is invalid in this case, then other disposals by the master are even more certainly invalid.

Key Terms
الإحاطة (al-ihatah): The condition where debts completely cover the value of the slave's property and person.
التحرير (al-tahrir): Emancipation of a slave, here from the ma'dhun's earnings.
الرقبة (al-raqabah): The body or person of the slave, and by extension the slave's capital value.
الحاجة (al-hajah): Need; here it refers to the debt that must be satisfied first.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa, what is the ruling on the master's emancipation of a slave from the ma'dhun's earnings when debts encompass the ma'dhun's property and person?
A) Valid, but the master must pay the slave's value to creditors.
B) Invalid, because the master does not own those earnings.
C) Valid, regardless of compensation.
D) Suspended until debts are paid.

Key Takeaway
When debts encompass both the property and person of the ma'dhun, earnings are 'occupied by need', so the master does not own them according to the mufta bihi view of Abu Hanifa.
